    \mf C^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F) & = \prod_{i_0,\dots,i_p} j_{i_0,\dots,i_p,*}(\mathcal F|_{U_{i_0}\cap\cdots\cap U_{i_p}})
\end{align*} with the same alternating differential. Then the complex \begin{align*}
    0 \to \cF & \to \mf C^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F) \to \mf C^1(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F) \to \cdots
\end{align*} is a resolution of $\cF$, flasque if $\cF$ is flasque. Picking an injective resolution of $\cF$ and comparing the two resolutions gives a morphism from the Čech complex to the injective resolution, and hence a morphism on cohomology. In other words, there is a natural map
\[
    \check H^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F) \to H^p(X,\mathcal F)
\]
where the RHS is derived functor cohomology.

\begin{theorem}
    If $X$ is quasi-compact and separated, and $\mathcal F$ is quasi-coherent, then Čech cohomology with respect to any affine open cover computes sheaf cohomology.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    For $p=0$, the desired map is an isomorphism by the preceding result. For the general case, embed $\mathcal F$ into a flasque quasicoherent sheaf $\mathcal G$, and let $\mathcal R$ be the quotient:
    \[
        0\to \mathcal F\to \mathcal G\to \mathcal R\to 0.
    \]
    For each $i_0<\cdots<i_p$, the open set
    \[
        U_{i_0,\dots,i_p}:=U_{i_0}\cap\cdots\cap U_{i_p}
    \]
    is affine, since it is an intersection of affine open subsets of a separated scheme. Since $\mathcal F,\mathcal G,\mathcal R$ are quasicoherent, we have an exact sequence
    \[
        0\to
        \mathcal F(U_{i_0,\dots,i_p})
        \to
        \mathcal G(U_{i_0,\dots,i_p})
        \to
        \mathcal R(U_{i_0,\dots,i_p})
        \to 0.
    \]
    Taking products over all $i_0<\cdots<i_p$, we obtain a short exact sequence of \v Cech complexes
    \[
        0\to
        \check C^\bullet(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \to
        \check C^\bullet(\mathfrak U,\mathcal G)
        \to
        \check C^\bullet(\mathfrak U,\mathcal R)
        \to 0.
    \]
    Hence there is a long exact sequence in \v Cech cohomology. Since $\mathcal G$ is flasque, its higher \v Cech cohomology vanishes, so for $p>0$ we have
    \[
        \check H^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal G)=0.
    \]
    Therefore the long exact sequence gives an exact sequence
    \[
        0\to
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \to
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal G)
        \to
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal R)
        \to
        \check H^1(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \to 0
    \]
    and isomorphisms
    \[
        \check H^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal R)
        \xrightarrow{\sim}
        \check H^{p+1}(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \qquad
        \text{for all } p\ge 1.
    \]

    Now compare this with the long exact sequence in ordinary sheaf cohomology associated to
    \[
        0\to \mathcal F\to \mathcal G\to \mathcal R\to 0.
    \]
    Since $\mathcal G$ is flasque, it is acyclic, so $H^q(X,\mathcal G)=0$ for $q>0$. Using the case $p=0$, we know that
    \[
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)\cong H^0(X,\mathcal F),
        \qquad
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal G)\cong H^0(X,\mathcal G),
        \qquad
        \check H^0(\mathfrak U,\mathcal R)\cong H^0(X,\mathcal R).
    \]
    Comparing the two long exact sequences, we conclude that the natural map
    \[
        \check H^1(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \to
        H^1(X,\mathcal F)
    \]
    is an isomorphism.

    Finally, $\mathcal R$ is again quasicoherent, so the same argument applies to $\mathcal R$. Since
    \[
        \check H^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal R)
        \cong
        \check H^{p+1}(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
    \]
    and
    \[
        H^p(X,\mathcal R)
        \cong
        H^{p+1}(X,\mathcal F)
    \]
    for $p\ge 1$, induction on $p$ gives
    \[
        \check H^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal F)
        \cong
        H^p(X,\mathcal F)
    \]
    for all $p\ge 0$.
\end{proof}

More generally, any open cover $\mathfrak U$ such that all finite intersections of opens in $\mathfrak U$ are acyclic for $\mathcal F$ will compute the cohomology of $\mathcal F$. The separatedness condition guarantees that finite intersections of affine opens are affine, hence acyclic for quasi-coherent sheaves.


\begin{exercise}
    Let $U = \mathbb A^2_k \setminus \{(0,0)\}$ be the punctured affine plane over a field $k$. Show that $H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)$ is infinite-dimensional over $k$. Conclude that $U$ is not affine.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Let
    \[
        U = \mathbb A^2_k \setminus \{(0,0)\} = D(x)\cup D(y).
    \]
    Since \(D(x)\), \(D(y)\), and \(D(x)\cap D(y)=D(xy)\) are affine, Čech cohomology with respect to this cover computes \(H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)\). Since $C^2 = 0$ for this cover, $\cO(D(xy)) = \ker(C^1 \to C^2)$, so $H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)$ is the cokernel of the map $C^0 \to C^1$. In other words, we divide out by differences $f\vert_{D(xy)} - g\vert_{D(xy)}$ where $f\in \mathcal O(D(x))$ and $g\in \mathcal O(D(y))$. But allowing each of $f$ and $g$ to be zero, we can divide out by all differences of the form $f\vert_{D(xy)}$ with $f\in \mathcal O(D(x))$ or $f\in \mathcal O(D(y))$. Hence
    \[
        H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)\cong
        \frac{\mathcal O(D(xy))}{\mathcal O(D(x))+\mathcal O(D(y))}
    \]
    Now
    \[
        \mathcal O(D(x))=k[x,x^{-1},y],\qquad
        \mathcal O(D(y))=k[x,y,y^{-1}],
    \]
    and
    \[
        \mathcal O(D(xy))=k[x,x^{-1},y,y^{-1}].
    \]
    Therefore
    \[
        H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)
        \cong
        \frac{k[x,x^{-1},y,y^{-1}]}
        {k[x,x^{-1},y]+k[x,y,y^{-1}]}.
    \]

    A monomial \(x^i y^j\) lies in \(k[x,x^{-1},y]\) if and only if \(j\ge 0\), and it lies in \(k[x,y,y^{-1}]\) if and only if \(i\ge 0\). Hence the only monomials which survive in the quotient are those with
    \[
        i<0,\qquad j<0.
    \]
    It follows that
    \[
        H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)\cong \bigoplus_{i,j<0} k\cdot x^i y^j,
    \]
    so \(H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)\) is isomorphic to the \(k\)-vector space spanned by
    \[
        \{x^i y^j \mid i,j<0\}.
    \]
    In particular, it is infinite-dimensional over \(k\).

    Since \(\mathcal O_U\) is quasi-coherent, if \(U\) were affine then we would have
    \[ H^1(U,\mathcal O_U)=0 \] Thus \(U\) is not affine. This also shows that it is not proper since it has infinite-dimensional cohomology.
\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}
    Let $X=\mathbb A^4_k$ be the affine $4$-space over a field $k$. Let
    \[Y=V(x_1,x_2)\cup V(x_3,x_4)\subset X\] be the union of two planes meeting at the origin, and let
    \[U=X-Y.\]
    Show that $H^2(U,\mathcal O_U)\neq 0$. Conclude that $Y$ is not a set-theoretic complete intersection, and that the projective closure of $Y$ in $\mathbb P^4_k$ is also not a set-theoretic complete intersection.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Let $R=k[x_1,x_2,x_3,x_4]$ and $X=\Spec R$. Set
    \[
        Y_1=V(x_1,x_2),\qquad Y_2=V(x_3,x_4),\qquad Y=Y_1\cup Y_2.
    \]
    Then
    \[
        U=X-Y=(X-Y_1)\cap (X-Y_2).
    \]
    Now
    \[
        X-Y_1=D(x_1)\cup D(x_2),
        \qquad
        X-Y_2=D(x_3)\cup D(x_4).
    \]
    Thus $U$ is covered by the four affine opens
    \[
        U_{13}=D(x_1x_3),\quad
        U_{14}=D(x_1x_4),\quad
        U_{23}=D(x_2x_3),\quad
        U_{24}=D(x_2x_4).
    \]
    Since $X$ is separated, all finite intersections of these opens are affine. Hence \v Cech cohomology with respect to this cover computes $H^i(U,\mathcal O_U)$.

    We now isolate one monomial subcomplex of the \v Cech complex. Order the four opens as
    \[
        U_1=U_{13},\qquad U_2=U_{14},\qquad U_3=U_{23},\qquad U_4=U_{24}.
    \]
    Consider the Laurent monomial
    \[
        m:=\frac{1}{x_1x_2x_3x_4}.
    \]
    This monomial appears in the coordinate ring of an intersection precisely when that intersection inverts all four variables $x_1,x_2,x_3,x_4$.

    It does not appear on any single $U_i$. Among pairwise intersections, it appears only on
    \[
        U_1\cap U_4=D(x_1x_2x_3x_4)
        \qquad\text{and}\qquad
        U_2\cap U_3=D(x_1x_2x_3x_4).
    \]
    It appears on every triple intersection and on the quadruple intersection. Therefore the part of the \v Cech complex spanned by this monomial is
    \[
        0\to k^2\xrightarrow{d^1} k^4\xrightarrow{d^2} k\to 0,
    \]
    where the $k^2$ has coordinates corresponding to the pairs $(1,4)$ and $(2,3)$, the $k^4$ has coordinates corresponding to the triples $(1,2,3)$, $(1,2,4)$, $(1,3,4)$, and $(2,3,4)$, and the last $k$ corresponds to the quadruple intersection.

    If a $1$-cochain has values $a$ on $U_1\cap U_4$ and $b$ on $U_2\cap U_3$, then its coboundary has triple components
    \[
        d^1(a,b)=(b,-a,-a,b).
    \]
    Indeed, this follows directly from the alternating \v Cech differential.

    On the other hand, for a $2$-cochain with triple components $(u,v,w,z)$, the next differential is
    \[
        d^2(u,v,w,z)=z-w+v-u.
    \]
    Hence the $2$-cochain
    \[
        \alpha=(0,m,0,-m)
    \]
    is a cocycle, since
    \[
        d^2\alpha=(-m)-0+m-0=0.
    \]
    But $\alpha$ is not a coboundary. If it were equal to $d^1(a,b)=(b,-a,-a,b)$, then comparing components would give simultaneously
    \[
        b=0,\qquad -a=m,\qquad -a=0,\qquad b=-m,
    \]
    which is impossible. Thus $\alpha$ defines a nonzero class in $H^2(U,\mathcal O_U)$. Therefore
    \[
        H^2(U,\mathcal O_U)\neq 0.
    \]

    Now suppose $Y$ were a set-theoretic complete intersection. Since $\operatorname{codim}_X(Y)=2$, this would mean that $Y$ is cut out set-theoretically by two equations. Hartshorne's cohomological criterion says that if $Y\subset X$ is cut out set-theoretically by $r$ equations, then
    \[
        H^i(X-Y,\mathcal F)=0
        \qquad
        \text{for all } i\ge r
    \]
    for all quasicoherent sheaves $\mathcal F$ on $X$. Taking $\mathcal F=\mathcal O_X$ and $r=2$, we would get
    \[
        H^2(X-Y,\mathcal O_X)=0,
    \]
    contradicting the computation above. Therefore $Y$ is not a set-theoretic complete intersection.

    Finally, the projective closure $\overline Y\subset \mathbb P^4_k$ is also not a set-theoretic complete intersection. Indeed, if $\overline Y$ were cut out set-theoretically by two homogeneous equations in $\mathbb P^4_k$, then restricting to the affine chart $x_0\neq 0$ would cut out $Y\subset \mathbb A^4_k$ set-theoretically by two equations, contradicting the affine case.
\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}
    Let $X$ be a subscheme of $\mathbb P^2_k$ defined by a single homogeneous equation
    \[
        f(x_0,x_1,x_2)=0
    \]
    of degree $d$. Do not assume that $f$ is irreducible. Assume that $(1,0,0)$ is not on $X$. Show that $X$ can be covered by the two open affine subsets
    \[
        U=X\cap \{x_1\neq 0\},
        \qquad
        V=X\cap \{x_2\neq 0\}.
    \]
    Compute the \v Cech complex
    \[
        \Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)\oplus \Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)
        \to
        \Gamma(U\cap V,\mathcal O_X)
    \]
    explicitly, and show that
    \[
        \dim H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)=1,
        \qquad
        \dim H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)=\frac{1}{2}(d-1)(d-2).
    \]
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Since $(1,0,0)\notin X$, the coefficient of $x_0^d$ in $f$ is nonzero. After rescaling $f$, we may assume that this coefficient is $1$.

    Let
    \[
        U=X\cap D_+(x_1),
        \qquad
        V=X\cap D_+(x_2).
    \]
    These two opens cover $X$, because the only point of $\mathbb P^2_k$ not contained in $D_+(x_1)\cup D_+(x_2)$ is $(1,0,0)$, which is not on $X$.

    On $U$, set
    \[
        x=\frac{x_0}{x_1},
        \qquad
        y=\frac{x_2}{x_1}.
    \]
    Then
    \[
        \Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)=A:=k[x,y]/(F),
        \qquad
        F=f(x,1,y).
    \]
    Since $f$ has coefficient $1$ in front of $x_0^d$, the polynomial $F$ is monic of degree $d$ in $x$. Therefore $A$ is a free $k[y]$-module with basis
    \[
        1,x,\dots,x^{d-1}.
    \]
    On the intersection $U\cap V$, we invert $y$, so
    \[
        \Gamma(U\cap V,\mathcal O_X)=A_y.
    \]
    Thus $A_y$ has a $k$-basis given by monomials
    \[
        x^i y^n,
        \qquad
        0\le i\le d-1,\quad n\in \mathbb Z.
    \]

    Now on $V$, set
    \[
        u=\frac{x_0}{x_2},
        \qquad
        v=\frac{x_1}{x_2}.
    \]
    In terms of $x,y$, we have
    \[
        u=\frac{x}{y},
        \qquad
        v=\frac{1}{y}.
    \]
    Therefore
    \[
        \Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)
        =
        k[u,v]/(f(u,v,1)).
    \]
    Again $f(u,v,1)$ is monic of degree $d$ in $u$, so $\Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)$ is a free $k[v]$-module with basis
    \[
        1,u,\dots,u^{d-1}.
    \]
    Inside $A_y$, the monomial $u^i v^m$ becomes
    \[
        u^i v^m
        =
        \left(\frac{x}{y}\right)^i
        \left(\frac{1}{y}\right)^m
        =
        x^i y^{-i-m}.
    \]
    Thus $\Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)$ is spanned inside $A_y$ by monomials $x^i y^n$
    with
    \[
        0\le i\le d-1,
        \qquad
        n\le -i.
    \]
    On the other hand, $\Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)=A$ is spanned by monomials $x^i y^n$
    with
    \[
        0\le i\le d-1,
        \qquad
        n\ge 0.
    \]

    The \v Cech complex for the cover $X=U\cup V$ is
    \[
        0\to
        \Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)\oplus \Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)
        \xrightarrow{\delta}
        \Gamma(U\cap V,\mathcal O_X)
        \to 0,
    \]
    where
    \[
        \delta(a,b)=b|_{U\cap V}-a|_{U\cap V}.
    \]

    Therefore
    \[
        H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)
        =
        \ker(\delta)
        =
        \Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)\cap \Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)
        \subset A_y.
    \]
    From the monomial descriptions above, the only monomial lying in both $\Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)$ and $\Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)$ is $1$. Hence
    \[
        H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)\cong k,
    \]
    so
    \[
        \dim H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)=1.
    \]

    Similarly,
    \[
        H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)
        \cong
        \frac{\Gamma(U\cap V,\mathcal O_X)}
        {\Gamma(U,\mathcal O_X)+\Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)}
        =
        \frac{A_y}{A+\Gamma(V,\mathcal O_X)}.
    \]
    The quotient has basis given by monomials $x^i y^n$
    with
    \[
        0\le i\le d-1,
        \qquad
        -i<n<0.
    \]
    For fixed $i$, the possible values are
    \[
        n=-1,-2,\dots,-(i-1).
    \]
    Thus there are $i-1$ such monomials for $i\ge 2$, and none for $i=0,1$. Therefore
    \[
        \dim H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)
        =
        \sum_{i=2}^{d-1}(i-1)
        =
        1+2+\cdots+(d-2)
        =
        \frac{(d-1)(d-2)}{2}.
    \]
    Hence
    \[
        \dim H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)=1,
        \qquad
        \dim H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)=\frac{1}{2}(d-1)(d-2).
    \]
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
    We compute the cohomology of the line with the double origin. Let $X = U\cup V$ where $U = \Spec k[x]$ and $V = \Spec k[y]$ are two copies of the affine line, and $U\cap V = \Spec k[x,x^{-1}]$ is the punctured affine line, with the restriction maps given by $x\mapsto x$ and $y\mapsto x$. Then the \v Cech complex for the cover $X=U\cup V$ is
    \[
        0\to
        k[x]\oplus k[y]
        \xrightarrow{\delta}
        k[x,x^{-1}]
        \to 0,
    \]
    where $\delta(f(x),g(y))=g(x)-f(x)$. The kernel of $\delta$ consists of pairs $(f(x),f(y))$ where $f$ is a polynomial, so $H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)\cong k[x]$. The cokernel of $\delta$ is spanned by monomials $x^n$ with $n<0$, so $H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)$ is infinite-dimensional over $k$.
\end{example}
\subsubsection{Cohomology of $\mathbb{P}^n$}
Let $k$ be a field and $X=\mathbb{P}^n_k$.
\begin{theorem}
    For all $m\in \mathbb Z$:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $H^0(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m))$ is the space of homogeneous polynomials of degree $m$:
              \[
                  H^0(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m)) =
                  \begin{cases}
                      S_m & m \ge 0, \\
                      0   & m<0.
                  \end{cases}
              \]

        \item $H^i(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m))=0$ for $0<i<n$.

        \item Top cohomology comes exactly from monomials $x^a$ with all exponents negative and with total degree $m$:
              \[
                  H^n(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m)) \cong
                  \begin{cases}
                      0                   & m>-n-1,    \\
                      k^{\binom{-m-1}{n}} & m\le -n-1.
                  \end{cases}
              \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Sketch of the computation]
    Let
    \[
        X=\mathbb P^n_k=\Proj S, \qquad S=k[x_0,\dots,x_n],
    \]
    and cover \(X\) by the standard affine opens
    \[
        \mathfrak U=\{U_i=D_+(x_i)\}_{i=0}^n.
    \]
    For a subset \(I=\{i_0,\dots,i_p\}\), write \(U_I=U_{i_0}\cap \cdots \cap U_{i_p}\). Then
    \[
        \mathcal O_X(m)(U_I)=(S_{x_I})_m,
        \qquad x_I:=x_{i_0}\cdots x_{i_p}.
    \]
    Thus
    \[
        \check C^p(\mathfrak U,\mathcal O_X(m))
        =
        \bigoplus_{|I|=p+1}(S_{x_I})_m.
    \]

    A basis for \((S_{x_I})_m\) is given by Laurent monomials
    \[
        x^a=x_0^{a_0}\cdots x_n^{a_n},
        \qquad a_i\in \mathbb Z, \qquad \sum_{i=0}^n a_i=m,
    \]
    such that \(a_j\ge 0\) for every \(j\notin I\). Equivalently, the variables with negative exponent must all belong to \(I\).

    For such an exponent vector \(a=(a_0,\dots,a_n)\), set
    \[
        N(a):=\{i\mid a_i<0\}.
    \]
    Then the monomial \(x^a\) appears in the summand indexed by \(I\) if and only if \(N(a)\subseteq I\). Hence the Čech complex decomposes as a direct sum of monomial subcomplexes:
    \[
        \check C^\bullet(\mathfrak U,\mathcal O_X(m))
        =
        \bigoplus_{\substack{a\in \mathbb Z^{n+1}\\ a_0+\cdots+a_n=m}} C^\bullet(a),
    \]
    where \(C^\bullet(a)\) is spanned by the single monomial \(x^a\).

    Fix such an \(a\), and let \(r=|N(a)|\). Then
    \[
        C^p(a)=0 \quad \text{if } p+1<r,
    \]
    and for \(p+1\ge r\) we have
    \[
        C^p(a)=
        \bigoplus_{\substack{I\supseteq N(a)\\ |I|=p+1}} k\cdot x^a.
    \]
    Thus \(C^\bullet(a)\) is the simplicial cochain complex of a simplex on the remaining vertices \(\{0,\dots,n\}\setminus N(a)\), shifted by \(r-1\).

    There are three cases.

    \medskip

    \noindent\textbf{Case 1: \(N(a)=\varnothing\).}
    Then all exponents are nonnegative, so \(x^a\) is an honest homogeneous polynomial of degree \(m\). In this case \(C^\bullet(a)\) is the ordinary Čech complex of the full simplex, which has the cohomology of a point:
    \[
        H^q(C^\bullet(a))=
        \begin{cases}
            k & q=0, \\
            0 & q>0.
        \end{cases}
    \]
    Summing over all such monomials gives
    \[
        H^0(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m))=
        \begin{cases}
            S_m & m\ge 0, \\
            0   & m<0.
        \end{cases}
    \]

    \noindent\textbf{Case 2: $\varnothing\neq N(a)\neq \{0,\dots,n\}$.}
    Let $M=\{0,\dots,n\}\setminus N(a)$ and write each index set as
    \[
        I=N(a)\sqcup J, \qquad J\subseteq M.
    \]
    Then
    \[
        C^p(a)\cong \bigoplus_{\substack{J\subseteq M\\ |J|=p+1-|N(a)|}} k.
    \]
    Thus $C^\bullet(a)$ is the augmented simplicial cochain complex of the simplex on the vertex set $M$, shifted by $|N(a)|-1$. It is augmented because $J=\varnothing$ occurs when
    $p=r-1$.
    So the complex starts with
    $C^{r-1}(a)\cong k$ then comes $C^r(a)\cong k^{|M|}$ and higher terms.


    Since a simplex is contractible, its augmented cochain complex is exact. Therefore
    \[
        H^q(C^\bullet(a))=0
        \qquad \text{for all } q.
    \]

    \noindent\textbf{Case 3: \(N(a)=\{0,\dots,n\}\).}
    Then every exponent is negative. In this case the only subset \(I\) containing \(N(a)\) is the full set \(\{0,\dots,n\}\), so the complex is concentrated in degree \(n\):
    \[
        C^p(a)=0 \quad (p<n),
        \qquad
        C^n(a)=k\cdot x^a.
    \]
    Hence
    \[
        H^q(C^\bullet(a))=
        \begin{cases}
            k & q=n,    \\
            0 & q\ne n.
        \end{cases}
    \]
    Thus top cohomology is spanned by monomials \(x^a\) with all \(a_i<0\) and \(\sum a_i=m\).

    Writing
    \[
        a_i=-b_i-1, \qquad b_i\ge 0,
    \]
    the condition \(\sum a_i=m\) becomes
    \[
        \sum_{i=0}^n b_i=-m-n-1.
    \]
    Therefore such monomials exist if and only if
    \[
        m\le -n-1.
    \]
    When this holds, a basis for \(H^n(\mathbb P^n,\mathcal O(m))\) is given by the classes
    \[
        \left[\frac{1}{x_0^{b_0+1}\cdots x_n^{b_n+1}}\right],
        \qquad b_i\ge 0, \qquad \sum_{i=0}^n b_i=-m-n-1.
    \]
    In fact this basis is more naturally interpreted in terms of rational top differential forms on \(\mathbb P^n\). Let
    \[
        \Omega = \sum_{i=0}^n (-1)^i x_i\, dx_0 \wedge \cdots \widehat{dx_i} \cdots \wedge dx_n
    \]
    be the standard homogeneous \((n+1)\)-form on \(\mathbb A^{n+1}\setminus\{0\}\). Then one can check that $\Omega$ has weight $n+1$ with respect to the standard scaling action. Then a monomial \(x^a\) with all exponents negative corresponds to the rational differential form
    \[
        \frac{\Omega}{x_0^{b_0+1}\cdots x_n^{b_n+1}},
    \]
    which descends to a well-defined rational section of the canonical bundle \(\omega_{\mathbb P^n}\).
\end{proof}


\begin{theorem}[Serre duality and vanishing]
    Let $X$ be a smooth projective scheme of dimension $n$ over a field $k$, and let $\mathcal F$ be a coherent $\mathcal O_X$-module. Then there is a natural perfect pairing
    \[
        H^i(X,\mathcal F) \;\times\; \Ext^{n-i}_{\mathcal O_X}(\mathcal F,\omega_X)
        \;\longrightarrow\; k,
    \]
    functorial in $\mathcal F$, which induces canonical isomorphisms
    \[
        H^i(X,\mathcal F)^\vee \;\cong\;
        \Ext^{n-i}_{\mathcal O_X}(\mathcal F,\omega_X).
    \]
    Here $\omega_X := \Omega^n_{X/k}$ is the canonical (dualizing) sheaf.
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}
    If $\mathcal F$ is locally free, then
    \[
        H^i(X,\mathcal F)^\vee
        \;\cong\;
        H^{n-i}(X,\mathcal F^\vee \otimes \omega_X).
    \]
\end{corollary}


\begin{theorem}[Serre vanishing]
    Let $X$ be a projective scheme over a Noetherian ring $A$, and let $\mathcal L$ be an ample line bundle. Then for every coherent sheaf $\mathcal F$, there exists $n_0$ such that for all $n\ge n_0$:
    \[
        H^i(X,\mathcal F\otimes \mathcal L^{\otimes n}) = 0
        \quad \text{for all } i>0.
    \]
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}
    For $n\gg 0$, the sheaf $\mathcal F\otimes \mathcal L^{\otimes n}$ is generated by global sections.
\end{corollary}


\begin{exercise}
    Let $0 \to F' \to F \to F'' \to 0$ be a short exact sequence of coherent sheaves on a Noetherian scheme $X$. Show that $\chi(X,F) = \chi(X,F') + \chi(X,F'')$, where $\chi(X,F) = \sum_{i=0}^\infty (-1)^i \dim_k H^i(X,F)$ is the Euler characteristic of $F$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    This follows immediately from the long exact sequence in cohomology associated to the short exact sequence of sheaves and the alternating sum of dimensions of a long exact sequence of vector spaces is zero.
\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}
    Let $X$ be a projective scheme over a field $k$, let $\mathcal O_X(1)$ be a very ample invertible sheaf on $X$ over $k$, and let $\mathcal F$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Show that there is a polynomial $P(z)\in \mathbb Q[z]$ such that
              \[
                  \chi(\mathcal F(n))=P(n)
              \]
              for all $n\in \mathbb Z$.

        \item If $X=\mathbb P^r_k$ and $M=\Gamma_*(\mathcal F)$, show that this agrees with the usual Hilbert polynomial of the graded $S=k[x_0,\dots,x_r]$-module $M$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    We prove the first statement by induction on $d=\dim \operatorname{Supp}\mathcal F$.

    If $d=0$, then $\mathcal F$ is supported on finitely many closed points. Twisting by an invertible sheaf does not change the length of a zero-dimensional coherent sheaf, so $\chi(\mathcal F(n))$ is independent of $n$. Hence it is given by a constant polynomial.

    Now suppose $d>0$. Choose a section $s\in H^0(X,\mathcal O_X(1))$ whose zero locus does not contain any generic point of $\operatorname{Supp}\mathcal F$. Multiplication by $s$ gives a map
    \[
        \mathcal F(-1)\xrightarrow{\cdot s}\mathcal F.
    \]
    Let $\mathcal R$ be its kernel and $\mathcal Q$ its cokernel. Then
    \[
        0\to \mathcal R\to \mathcal F(-1)\to \mathcal F\to \mathcal Q\to 0.
    \]
    Since $s$ is generically injective on $\mathcal F$, both $\mathcal R$ and $\mathcal Q$ have support of dimension strictly smaller than $d$.

    Twisting by $\mathcal O_X(n)$ gives
    \[
        0\to \mathcal R(n)\to \mathcal F(n-1)\to \mathcal F(n)\to \mathcal Q(n)\to 0.
    \]
    By additivity of Euler characteristic,
    \[
        \chi(\mathcal F(n))-\chi(\mathcal F(n-1))
        =
        \chi(\mathcal Q(n))-\chi(\mathcal R(n)).
    \]
    By induction, the right-hand side is a polynomial function of $n$. Hence the finite difference of $\chi(\mathcal F(n))$ is polynomial in $n$.

    A standard property of numerical polynomials says that if $a(n)-a(n-1)$ is polynomial in $n$, then $a(n)$ is given by a polynomial in $\mathbb Q[n]$. Therefore there exists $P_{\mathcal F}(z)\in \mathbb Q[z]$ such that
    \[
        \chi(\mathcal F(n))=P_{\mathcal F}(n)
    \]
    for all $n\in \mathbb Z$.

    Now let $X=\mathbb P^r_k$, and let
    \[
        M=\Gamma_*(\mathcal F)=\bigoplus_{n\in \mathbb Z}H^0(\mathbb P^r_k,\mathcal F(n)).
    \]
    Then
    \[
        M_n=H^0(\mathbb P^r_k,\mathcal F(n)).
    \]
    By Serre vanishing, for $n\gg 0$,
    \[
        H^i(\mathbb P^r_k,\mathcal F(n))=0
        \qquad
        \text{for all } i>0.
    \]
    Hence for $n\gg 0$,
    \[
        \chi(\mathcal F(n))
        =
        \dim_k H^0(\mathbb P^r_k,\mathcal F(n))
        =
        \dim_k M_n.
    \]
    Thus the Hilbert polynomial of $\mathcal F$ agrees for large $n$ with the Hilbert function of $M$. Therefore it is the usual Hilbert polynomial of the graded $S$-module $M$.
\end{solution}

\subsubsection{Semicontinuity of cohomology, base change, and seesaw principle}
We begin with a foundational result about the coherent pushforward of cohomology.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $f : X \to Y$ be a proper morphism of Noetherian schemes, and let $\mathcal{F}$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$. Then $R^i f_* \mathcal{F}$ is coherent on $Y$ for all $i \geq 0$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Semicontinuity]\label{thm:semicontinuity}
    Let $f:X \to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, and let
    $\mathcal F$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$, flat over $Y$. Then for each $i \ge 0$, the function
    \[
        y \longmapsto h^i(y,\mathcal F)
        := \dim_{k(y)} H^i(X_y,\mathcal F_y)
    \]
    is upper semicontinuous on $Y$. Moreover, the function \begin{align*}
        y \longmapsto \sum_{i=0}^\infty (-1)^i h^i(y,\mathcal F)
    \end{align*} is locally constant on $Y$.
\end{theorem}
Equivalently, for each integer $N$, the subset $\{y \in Y : h^i(y,\mathcal F) \geq N\}$ is closed in $Y$ for each $i$.
In particular the dimension of sheaf cohomology can only jump up upon specialization, as is demonstrated by the following example.

\begin{example}
    Let $X = E \times E \to E$ be the trivial family of an elliptic curve $E$, and let $\mathcal L$ be a line bundle on $X$ whose restriction $\mathcal L_y$ varies in $\operatorname{Pic}^0(E)$, with
    $\mathcal L_0 \cong \mathcal O_E$ and $\mathcal L_y \not\cong \mathcal O_E$ for $y \neq 0$. For example, we can take $\mathcal L$ to the line bundle of the divisor $\Delta - (E \times \{0\})$, where $\Delta$ is the diagonal in $E \times E$.

    Then $\mathcal{L}$ is flat over $Y$. Note that because $\cL$ is locally free, $\cL_x\cong \cO_{X_x}$ for every $x\in X$, and so it is enough to check that $X \to E$ is flat. The latter fact holds because the map $X \to E$ is just the projection from a product, which is flat. Locally on $E$, if $E$ is covered by affine opens $\operatorname{Spec} A$, and $Y$ by affine opens $\operatorname{Spec} R$, then
    \[
        X|_{\operatorname{Spec}A\times \operatorname{Spec}R}
        =
        \operatorname{Spec}(A\otimes_k R),
    \]
    and $A\otimes_k R$ is flat over $R$, because it is actually a free $R$-module once you choose a $k$-basis of $A$. Another way to see this is that $E \times_{\Spec k} E \to E$ is the base change of the flat morphism $E \to \Spec k$ and the structure map of a variety to $\Spec k$ is always flat, and flatness is preserved under base change.

    Then we see that
    \[
        h^0(E,\mathcal L_y)=
        \begin{cases}
            1, & y=0,     \\
            0, & y\neq 0.
        \end{cases}
    \]
    This is because a nontrivial degree $0$ line bundle on an elliptic curve has no nonzero sections, while $\mathcal O_E$ has one. This is true on any smooth projective curve $C$ for example. If a line bundle $\cL$ is degree zero and has nonzero section $s$ then $s$ has an effective divisor of zeros $D$ and $\cL\cong \cO_C(D)$ and $\deg D = 0$ and $D \geq 0$ implies that $D=0$ and $\cL\cong \cO_C$.

    This also shows that one cannot naively think of \(\pi_*\mathcal L\) as obtained by "gluing together" the spaces $H^0(E,\mathcal L_p)$
    Indeed, in this example one has
    \[
        \pi_*\mathcal L = 0,
    \]
    To see this, it is enough to check that for every open $U \subseteq X$ the stalk of $\pi_*\mathcal L$ at the generic point of $U$ is zero (because $X = E\times E$ is integral). But the restriction of $\mathcal L $to the generic point of $U$ is a nontrivial degree 0 line bundle since $\eta_U$ is not equal to the closed point $0$ of $E$, so it has no nonzero sections, and hence the stalk of $\pi_*\mathcal L$ at $\eta_U$ is zero.

    Thus this example shows cohomology does not commute with taking fibers everywhere. Let's closely examine the failure of cohomology and base change here.

    The relevant base change map in degree 0 is
    \[
        \varphi^0(p):(\pi_*\mathcal L)\otimes k(p)\longrightarrow H^0(E,\mathcal L_p).
    \]
    Since $\pi_*\mathcal L=0$, the source is always $0$. So
    \[
        \varphi^0(p): 0\longrightarrow H^0(E,\mathcal L_p).
    \]

    Hence:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item if $p\neq p_0$, then
              $H^0(E,\mathcal L_p)=0$,
              so $\varphi^0(p)$ is
              $0\to 0$,
              an isomorphism;
        \item if $p=p_0$, then
              $H^0(E,\mathcal L_{p_0})\cong k$,
              so $\varphi^0(p_0)$ is
              $0\to k$,
              not surjective, hence not an isomorphism.
    \end{itemize}

    So cohomology and base change fails exactly at the special point $p_0$.

\end{example}

The semicontinuity theorem also implies that the locus where line bundles are trivial is Zariski closed, which is made precise by the following result.

\begin{proposition}
    Suppose \(X \to Y\) is a flat projective morphism with integral fibers. Let \(\mathcal L\) be a line bundle on \(X\), which is automatically flat over \(Y\) since it is locally free. Then the locus of \(y \in Y\) where \(\mathcal L_y\) is trivial on \(X_y\) is a closed set.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
    The locus where
    \[
        h^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y)\ge 1
    \]
    is closed by the Semicontinuity Theorem, and the same holds for the locus where
    \[
        h^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y^{\vee})\ge 1.
    \]
    Now invoke the fact that if $\cL$ is an invertible sheaf on an integral projective scheme $X$ such that
    both $\cL$ and $\cL^\vee$ have non-zero sections, then $\cL$ is trivial. In the proof of this fact, we can remove projective if we know that the only global functions on $X$ are the constants.

    This is because the multiplication map if \(s\) and \(t\) are nonzero sections of \(\mathcal L\) and \(\mathcal L^{\vee}\), respectively, they are both nonzero in the stalk at the generic point. Otherwise they would be the zero section; this is where we use that \(X\) is integral.

    Under the natural map
    \[
        \mathcal L \otimes \mathcal L^{\vee} \longrightarrow \mathcal O_X,
    \]
    the section \(s \otimes t\) maps to \(st\), which is also nonzero.
    But the only global functions, i.e.\ global sections of \(\mathcal O_X\), are the constants, so \(st\) is a nonzero constant.
    It follows that \(s\) is nowhere vanishing, for otherwise \(st\) would vanish somewhere.
    Hence \(\mathcal L\) has a nowhere vanishing section, and therefore $\mathcal L \cong \mathcal O_X$.
\end{proof}



\begin{theorem}[Cohomology and Base Change]\label{thm:basechange}
    Let $f:X \to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, and let
    $\mathcal F$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$, flat over $Y$. Let $y \in Y$. Then:

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item If the natural map
              \[
                  \varphi^i(y):
                  (R^i f_*\mathcal F)\otimes_{\mathcal O_Y} k(y)
                  \longrightarrow
                  H^i(X_y,\mathcal F_y)
              \]
              is surjective, then it is an isomorphism, and the same holds for all $y'$ in a suitable neighborhood of $y$.

        \item Assume that $\varphi^i(y)$ is surjective. Then the following are equivalent:
              \begin{enumerate}
                  \item $\varphi^{i-1}(y)$ is surjective;
                  \item $R^i f_*\mathcal F$ is locally free in a neighborhood of $y$.
              \end{enumerate}
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}
    One naive thing one might try to do to understand the failure of cohomology and base change is to take a \v{C}ech complex \(C^\bullet\) computing \(H^i(X,\mathcal F)\) and tensor it with \(k(s)\). The problem is that tensoring with \(k(s)\) is not exact in general, since \(k(s)\) is usually not flat over \(A\). Thus cohomology need not commute with tensor product:
    \[
        H^i(C^\bullet)\otimes_A k(s) \not\cong H^i(C^\bullet\otimes_A k(s)).
    \]
    Moreover, for a general family and a general sheaf, the terms of the \v{C}ech complex need not be free or even flat over \(A\), and a given \v{C}ech cover need not compute the actual sheaf cohomology.
\end{remark}

With this result in mind, we are ready to state the Seesaw principle, which is a powerful criterion for recognizing when a line bundle on a family of integral projective schemes is pulled back from the base.

\begin{theorem}[Seesaw principle: recognition of pullbacks]\label{thm:seesaw-pullback}
    Let \(\pi:X\to Y\) be a flat projective morphism of Noetherian schemes with
    geometrically integral fibers, and let \(\mathcal L\) be an invertible sheaf on \(X\).
    Assume that for every \(y\in Y\),
    \[
        \mathcal L|_{X_y}\cong \mathcal O_{X_y}.
    \]
    Then there exists an invertible sheaf \(\mathcal M\) on \(Y\) such that
    \[
        \mathcal L \cong \pi^*\mathcal M.
    \]
    Moreover, \(\mathcal M\) is unique up to unique isomorphism.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Discussion of the mechanism]
    The key input is cohomology and base change applied to \(\mathcal L\). Since each fiber \(X_y\) is proper and geometrically integral over \(k(y)\), we have
    \[
        H^0(X_y,\mathcal O_{X_y}) \cong k(y).
    \]
    To see this, fix the fiber
    \[
        X_y=X\times_Y \Spec k(y),
    \]
    and suppose it is proper and geometrically integral over $k(y)$. Set
    $K:=H^0(X_y,\mathcal O_{X_y})$. Because $X_y$ is proper over $k(y)$, $K$ is a finite $k(y)$-algebra. Because $X_y$ is integral, $K$ is a domain, hence a field finite over $k(y)$. We need to see that this extension is trivial. Extend scalars from $k(y)$ to an algebraic closure $\overline{k(y)}$. Then
    \[
        X_{\overline{k(y)}}:=X_y\times_{k(y)} \Spec \overline{k(y)}.
    \]
    Geometric integrality means exactly that this new scheme is still integral.

    But global functions also base change in degree $0$ here (the map $\Spec \overline{k(y)}\to \Spec k(y)$ is flat!), so
    \[
        H^0(X_{\overline{k(y)}},\mathcal O_{X_{\overline{k(y)}}})
        \cong
        K\otimes_{k(y)} \overline{k(y)}.
    \]
    So if $K\otimes_{k(y)} \overline{k(y)}$ is not a domain, then $X_{\overline{k(y)}}$ cannot be integral, because an integral scheme has its ring of global sections a domain.

    If $K/k(y)$ separable, then over the algebraic closure, $K$ splits:
    \[
        K\otimes_{k(y)} \overline{k(y)}
        \cong
        \overline{k(y)}^{\, [K:k(y)]}.
    \]
    So it is a product of fields and in particular not a domain. A product ring corresponds geometrically to several components, so the base change becomes reducible.

    If $K/k(y)$ is purely inseparable, then after base change, nilpotents appear. Consider the simplest case where in characteristic $p$ we have a degree $p$ extension
    \[
        k \subset k(\alpha),\qquad \alpha^p=a\in k,\ \alpha\notin k.
    \]
    Over an algebraic closure $\bar k$,
    \[
        K\otimes_k \bar k
        \cong
        \bar k[t]/(t-\beta)^p
    \]
    for some $\beta\in \bar k$. This ring has nilpotents, so it is not reduced, hence not integral. So in this case the base change becomes nonreduced. Thus proper and geometrically integral implies that $H^0(X_y,\mathcal O_{X_y})\cong k(y)$.
    By the hypothesis \(\mathcal L|_{X_y}\cong \mathcal O_{X_y}\), it follows that
    \[
        H^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y)\cong k(y),
    \]
    so in particular
    \[
        h^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y)=1
        \qquad\text{for all }y\in Y.
    \]

    Now consider the base change map
    \[
        \varphi^0(y):(\pi_*\mathcal L)\otimes_{\mathcal O_Y} k(y)\longrightarrow H^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y).
    \]
    Because the target has constant dimension \(1\), cohomology and base change implies that
    \(\pi_*\mathcal L\) is locally free of rank \(1\) on \(Y\), hence an invertible sheaf.
    Set
    \[
        \mathcal M:=\pi_*\mathcal L.
    \]
    There is a natural adjunction morphism
    \[
        \pi^*\pi_*\mathcal L \longrightarrow \mathcal L,
    \]
    that is,
    \[
        \pi^*\mathcal M \longrightarrow \mathcal L.
    \]
    To show that this is an isomorphism, it suffices to check on fibers.
    Restricting to \(X_y\), the induced map is
    \[
        H^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y)\otimes_{k(y)} \mathcal O_{X_y}\longrightarrow \mathcal L_y.
    \]
    Since \(\mathcal L_y\cong \mathcal O_{X_y}\) and \(H^0(X_y,\mathcal L_y)\cong k(y)\), this is the canonical map
    \[
        k(y)\otimes_{k(y)}\mathcal O_{X_y}\longrightarrow \mathcal O_{X_y},
    \]
    hence an isomorphism for every \(y\in Y\).
    Therefore
    \[
        \pi^*\mathcal M \xrightarrow{\sim} \mathcal L,
    \]
    as claimed. Uniqueness is immediate: if \(\pi^*\mathcal M_1\cong \pi^*\mathcal M_2\), then pushing forward and using
    \(\pi_*\mathcal O_X\cong \mathcal O_Y\) gives \(\mathcal M_1\cong \mathcal M_2\).
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
    Let $X$ be an integral projective scheme over an algebraically closed field $k$, and assume that
    \[
        H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)=0.
    \]
    Let $T$ be a connected scheme of finite type over $k$.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item If $\mathcal L$ is an invertible sheaf on $X\times T$, show that the invertible sheaves $\mathcal L_t:=\mathcal L|_{X\times \{t\}}$
              on $X_t:=X\times \{t\}\cong X$
              are isomorphic for all closed points $t\in T$.

        \item Show that
              \[
                  \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T)\cong \operatorname{Pic}(X)\times \operatorname{Pic}(T).
              \]
              Do not assume that $T$ is reduced.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
    Let $X$ be an integral projective scheme over an algebraically closed field $k$, and assume that $H^1(X,\mathcal O_X)=0$. Let $T$ be a connected scheme of finite type over $k$.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item If $\mathcal L$ is an invertible sheaf on $X\times T$, show that the invertible sheaves
              \[
                  \mathcal L_t:=\mathcal L|_{X\times \{t\}}
              \]
              on
              \[
                  X_t:=X\times \{t\}\cong X
              \]
              are isomorphic for all closed points $t\in T$.

        \item Show that
              \[
                  \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T)\cong \operatorname{Pic}(X)\times \operatorname{Pic}(T).
              \]
              Do not assume that $T$ is reduced.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Let $p:X\times T\to T$ and $q:X\times T\to X$ be the projections. Since $X$ is integral and projective over the algebraically closed field $k$, we have $H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)=k$.

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Let $\mathcal L\in \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T)$. We show that the class of $\mathcal L_t$ is locally constant in $t$. Fix a closed point $t_0\in T$ and set $M=\mathcal L_{t_0}$. Put $\mathcal F=\mathcal L\otimes q^*M^{-1}$. Then $\mathcal F_{t_0}\cong \mathcal O_X$, so $h^0(X,\mathcal F_{t_0})=1$ and $h^1(X,\mathcal F_{t_0})=h^1(X,\mathcal O_X)=0$. Since $h^1(X,\mathcal F_{t_0})=0$, upper semicontinuity gives an open neighborhood $U\subset T$ of $t_0$ such that $h^1(X,\mathcal F_t)=0$ for all $t\in U$. In particuar, $R^1 p_*\mathcal F$ vanishes on $U$. The base change map \[
                  \varphi^1(t): (R^1 p_*\mathcal F)\otimes k(t) \longrightarrow H^1(X,\mathcal F_t)
              \] has target zero for all $t\in U$, so it is surjective. Since $R^1 p_*\mathcal F$ vanishes on $U$, it is locally free of rank $0$ on $U$. This implies that $\varphi^0(t): (p_*\mathcal F)\otimes k(t) \longrightarrow H^0(X,\mathcal F_t)$ is an isomorphism for all $t\in U$. In particular, $h^0(X,\mathcal F_t)=1$ for all $t\in U$, since \begin{align*}
                  h^0(X,\mathcal F_t)=\chi(\mathcal F_t)=\chi(\mathcal F_{t_0})=\chi(\mathcal O_X)=1.
              \end{align*}


              A local generator of $p_*\mathcal F$ gives a section $s:\mathcal O_{X\times U}\to \mathcal F|_{X\times U}$ which is nonvanishing on the fiber over $t_0$. Let $Z = V(s)$ be the zero locus of $s$. Then $Z$ is a closed subset of $X\times U$ which does meet the fiber over $t_0$.

              Since $p$ is proper, the image $p(Z)$ is a closed subset of $U$. Since $Z$ does not meet the fiber over $t_0$, $p(Z)$ does not contain $t_0$. Hence there is an open neighborhood of $t_0$ in $U$ which does not meet $p(Z)$. Shrinking $U$ to this neighborhood, we can assume that $p(Z)=\emptyset$, so $s$ is nowhere vanishing on $X\times U$. Hence $\mathcal F|_{X\times U}\cong \mathcal O_{X\times U}$, so $\mathcal L_t\cong M$ for all closed $t\in U$. Thus $t\mapsto [\mathcal L_t]$ is locally constant, and since $T$ is connected, all $\mathcal L_t$ are isomorphic.
        \item Consider the natural map
              \[
                  \operatorname{Pic}(X)\times \operatorname{Pic}(T)\to \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T),
                  \qquad
                  (M,N)\mapsto q^*M\otimes p^*N.
              \]
              It is injective: if $q^*M\otimes p^*N\cong \mathcal O_{X\times T}$, then restricting to a closed fiber $X_t$ gives $M\cong \mathcal O_X$; then pulling back along any closed point $x\in X(k)$ gives $N\cong \mathcal O_T$.

              For surjectivity, let $\mathcal L\in \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T)$. By (a), all $\mathcal L_t$ are isomorphic. Choose a closed point $t_0\in T$ and set $M=\mathcal L_{t_0}$. Put $\mathcal F=\mathcal L\otimes q^*M^{-1}$. Then $\mathcal F_t\cong \mathcal O_X$ for every closed $t\in T$, so $h^0(X,\mathcal F_t)=1$ and $h^1(X,\mathcal F_t)=0$. By cohomology and base change, $N:=p_*\mathcal F$ is an invertible sheaf on $T$,

              We need to check that the evaluation map $p^*N\to \mathcal F$ is an isomorphism on every closed fiber. Since base change holds, we have $N\otimes k(t) \cong H^0(X_t,\mathcal F_t)$. Therefore the restriction of the evaluation map to $X_t$ is
              \[H^0(X_t,\mathcal F_t)\otimes_k \mathcal O_{X_t} \to \mathcal F_t\]
              Since $\mathcal F_t\cong \mathcal O_X$, this identifies with the canonical map
              \[H^0(X,\mathcal O_X)\otimes_k \mathcal O_X \to \mathcal O_X\]
              which is an isomorphism.
              Therefore $\mathcal F\cong p^*N$, and thus $\mathcal L\cong q^*M\otimes p^*N$. Hence
              \[
                  \operatorname{Pic}(X\times T)\cong \operatorname{Pic}(X)\times \operatorname{Pic}(T).
              \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{solution}

Note that in general, in order to identify the fiber of the evaluation map $\varepsilon: p^*N\to \mathcal F$ with the map $H^0(X_t,\mathcal F_t)\otimes_k \mathcal O_{X_t} \to \mathcal F_t$, we need to use the fact that the base change map $\varphi^0(t): N\otimes k(t) \to H^0(X_t,\mathcal F_t)$ is an isomorphism. Without base change, one can only identify $p^*N\otimes k(t) = p_* \mathcal F \otimes k(t) \otimes \cO_{X_t}$ and this may not equal $H^0(X_t,\mathcal F_t)\otimes_k \mathcal O_{X_t}$.

\subsubsection{Higher direct image sheaves}
If $f:X\to Y$ is a morphism of schemes and $\mathcal F$ is a sheaf on $X$, then the pushforward $f_*\mathcal F$ is a sheaf on $Y$. Therefore $f_*$ is a functor from the category of sheaves on $X$ to the category of sheaves on $Y$. This functor is left exact, but not necessarily right exact. The right derived functors of $f_*$ are denoted by $R^i f_*$ and are called the \textbf{higher direct image functors}.

We explicilty construct $R^i f_*$ as we do $R\Gamma$. Let $\mathcal I^\bullet$ be an injective resolution of $\mathcal F$. Then $f_*\mathcal I^\bullet$ is a complex of sheaves on $Y$, and we define \begin{align*}
    R^i f_*\mathcal F := \cH^i(f_*\mathcal I^\bullet).
\end{align*} to be the $i$-th cohomology sheaf of this complex. The resulting $R^i f_*$ is independent of the choice of injective resolution, and it is a functor from the category of sheaves on $X$ to the category of sheaves on $Y$.

It is true that $R^if_*\cF$ is the sheafification of the presheaf
\[U\mapsto H^i(f^{-1}(U),\cF)\] In particular, the stalk of $R^if_*\cF$ at a point $y\in Y$ is given by
\[(R^if_*\cF)_y\cong \varinjlim_{y\in U} H^i(f^{-1}(U),\cF).\] Moreover it is also true that for affine open subsets $U\subseteq Y$, we have
\[H^0\!\left(U,\;R^q\pi_*\mathcal F\right)
    \cong
    H^q\!\left(\pi^{-1}(U),\mathcal F\right)\]
because of the degeneration of the Leray spectral sequence for $\pi_U:\pi^{-1}(U)\to U$ \begin{align*}
    E_2^{p,q}=H^p\!\left(U,\;R^q\pi_*\mathcal F|_U\right)
    \;\Rightarrow\;
    H^{p+q}\!\left(\pi^{-1}(U),\mathcal F\right)
\end{align*} Now use the fact that $U$ is affine to conclude that $H^p(U,-)=0$ for $p>0$.

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:higher-direct-images}
    Suppose \(\pi:X\to Y\) is a quasi-compact separated morphism of schemes. Then:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item[(a)] For each \(i\ge 0\), the higher direct image
              \[
                  R^i\pi_*:\QCoh_X\to \QCoh_Y
              \]
              is a covariant functor.

        \item[(b)] There is a natural isomorphism of functors
              \[
                  R^0\pi_*\cong \pi_*.
              \]

        \item[(c)] If
              \[
                  0\to \mathcal F\to \mathcal G\to \mathcal H\to 0
              \]
              is a short exact sequence of sheaves on \(X\), then there is an induced long exact sequence of higher direct image sheaves on \(Y\):
              \[
                  0\to R^0\pi_*\mathcal F\to R^0\pi_*\mathcal G\to R^0\pi_*\mathcal H
                  \to R^1\pi_*\mathcal F\to R^1\pi_*\mathcal G\to R^1\pi_*\mathcal H\to \cdots .
              \]

        \item[(d)] If \(\pi\) is projective, \(Y\) is locally Noetherian, and \(\mathcal F\) is a coherent sheaf on \(X\), then for all \(i\),
              \[
                  R^i\pi_*\mathcal F
              \]
              is a coherent sheaf on \(Y\).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}[Computation for the projection \(\mathbf P^1\times Y\to Y\)]
    Let \(Y\) be a scheme, and let
    \[
        p:\mathbf P^1\times Y\to Y,
        \qquad
        q:\mathbf P^1\times Y\to \mathbf P^1
    \]
    be the two projections. We compute the higher direct images of \(q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)\).

    For every affine open \(U=\Spec A\subseteq Y\), we have
    \[
        p^{-1}(U)\cong \mathbf P^1_A,
        \qquad
        q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)\big|_{p^{-1}(U)}\cong \mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1_A}(n).
    \]
    Hence
    \[
        (R^ip_*q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n))(U)
        \cong
        H^i(\mathbf P^1_A,\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1_A}(n)).
    \]
    Since cohomology of \(\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)\) commutes with extension of scalars in this case (because all of the terms in the \v{C}ech complex for the standard affine cover are free \(A\)-modules), we have
    \[
        H^i(\mathbf P^1_A,\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1_A}(n))
        \cong
        H^i(\mathbf P^1_k,\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n))\otimes_k A.
    \]
    Therefore
    \[
        R^ip_*q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)
        \cong
        H^i(\mathbf P^1,\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n))\otimes_k \mathcal O_Y.
    \]

    Now recall the standard cohomology of line bundles on \(\mathbf P^1\):
    \[
        h^0(\mathbf P^1,\mathcal O(n))=
        \begin{cases}
            n+1, & n\ge 0, \\
            0,   & n<0,
        \end{cases}
        \qquad
        h^1(\mathbf P^1,\mathcal O(n))=
        \begin{cases}
            0,    & n\ge -1, \\
            -n-1, & n\le -2.
        \end{cases}
    \]
    Thus
    \[
        p_*q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)\cong
        \begin{cases}
            \mathcal O_Y^{\oplus (n+1)}, & n\ge 0, \\
            0,                           & n<0,
        \end{cases}
    \]
    and
    \[
        R^1p_*q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)\cong
        \begin{cases}
            0,                            & n\ge -1, \\
            \mathcal O_Y^{\oplus (-n-1)}, & n\le -2.
        \end{cases}
    \]
    Moreover,
    \[
        R^ip_*q^*\mathcal O_{\mathbf P^1}(n)=0
        \qquad\text{for all }i\ge 2,
    \]
    since the fibers of \(p\) are \(1\)-dimensional.
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Formal functions}
Let $f:X\to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, let $\mathcal F$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$, and let $y\in Y$. For each $n\geq 1$, define
\[
    X_n=X\times_Y \Spec(\mathcal O_{Y,y}/\mathfrak m_y^n).
\]
Thus $X_1=X_y$ is the fiber over $y$, and for $n>1$, $X_n$ is an infinitesimal thickening of $X_y$. Let
\[
    v:X_n\to X
\]
be the natural map, and set
\[
    \mathcal F_n=v^*\mathcal F.
\]

For each $n$, there are natural base-change maps
\[
    R^if_*(\mathcal F)\otimes_{\mathcal O_Y}\mathcal O_{Y,y}/\mathfrak m_y^n
    \longrightarrow
    R^if'_{*}(\mathcal F_n),
\]
where $f':X_n\to \Spec(\mathcal O_{Y,y}/\mathfrak m_y^n)$ is the induced morphism. Since $\Spec(\mathcal O_{Y,y}/\mathfrak m_y^n)$ is affine and supported at one point, the right-hand side is
\[
    H^i(X_n,\mathcal F_n).
\]
As $n$ varies, these groups form an inverse system. Passing to inverse limits gives a natural map
\[
    (R^if_*\mathcal F)^\wedge_y
    \longrightarrow
    \varprojlim_n H^i(X_n,\mathcal F_n).
\]

\begin{theorem}[Theorem on Formal Functions]
    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, let $\mathcal F$ be a coherent sheaf on $X$, and let $y\in Y$. Then the natural map
    \[
        (R^if_*\mathcal F)^\wedge_y
        \longrightarrow
        \varprojlim_n H^i(X_n,\mathcal F_n)
    \]
    is an isomorphism for all $i\geq 0$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}
    The theorem says that the completed stalk of the higher direct image $R^if_*\mathcal F$ at $y$ is determined by the cohomology of the infinitesimal thickenings of the fiber $X_y$.
\end{remark}

\begin{corollary}
    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, and set
    \[
        r=\max\{\dim X_y:y\in Y\}.
    \]
    Then
    \[
        R^if_*(\mathcal F)=0
    \]
    for all $i>r$ and all coherent sheaves $\mathcal F$ on $X$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    For any $y\in Y$, the schemes $X_n$ have the same underlying topological space as $X_y$. Hence $\dim X_n=\dim X_y\leq r$. Therefore
    \[
        H^i(X_n,\mathcal F_n)=0
    \]
    for all $i>r$. By the theorem on formal functions,
    \[
        (R^if_*\mathcal F)^\wedge_y=0
    \]
    for all $y\in Y$ and all $i>r$. Since $R^if_*\mathcal F$ is coherent, this implies $R^if_*\mathcal F=0$
    for all $i>r$.
\end{proof}

In the last step, we are invoking a Nakayama/faithful flatness of completion argument for Noetherian local rings. In particular, if for a finitely generated module $M$ over a Noetherian local ring $(A,\mathfrak m)$, if $\widehat M=0$, then $M/\mathfrak m M=0$, so Nakayama gives $M=0$.

\begin{corollary}
    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes, and assume
    \[
        f_*\mathcal O_X=\mathcal O_Y.
    \]
    Then every fiber $f^{-1}(y)$ is connected.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    Suppose, for contradiction, that
    \[
        f^{-1}(y)=X'\sqcup X''
    \]
    where $X'$ and $X''$ are nonempty disjoint closed subsets. Then for each $n$, the thickened fiber $X_n$ decomposes as
    \[
        X_n=X'_n\sqcup X''_n,
    \]
    and hence
    \[
        H^0(X_n,\mathcal O_{X_n})
        =
        H^0(X'_n,\mathcal O_{X_n})
        \oplus
        H^0(X''_n,\mathcal O_{X_n}).
    \]
    Taking inverse limits and applying formal functions with $\mathcal F=\mathcal O_X$ gives
    \[
        \widehat{\mathcal O}_{Y,y}
        =
        (f_*\mathcal O_X)^\wedge_y
        \cong
        \varprojlim_n H^0(X_n,\mathcal O_{X_n})
        =
        A'\oplus A'',
    \]
    where
    \[
        A'=\varprojlim_n H^0(X'_n,\mathcal O_{X_n}),
        \qquad
        A''=\varprojlim_n H^0(X''_n,\mathcal O_{X_n}).
    \]
    But $\widehat{\mathcal O}_{Y,y}$ is a local ring, and a local ring cannot be written as a nontrivial direct product. Indeed, a nontrivial direct product would give nontrivial idempotents, while a local ring has only the idempotents $0$ and $1$. This contradiction shows that $f^{-1}(y)$ is connected.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Zariski's Main Theorem]
    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a birational projective morphism of Noetherian integral schemes, and assume that $Y$ is normal. Then every fiber $f^{-1}(y)$ is connected.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    By the previous corollary, it suffices to prove
    \[
        f_*\mathcal O_X=\mathcal O_Y.
    \]
    This question is local on $Y$, so assume $Y=\Spec A$. Since $f$ is projective, $f_*\mathcal O_X$ is coherent, so
    \[
        B=\Gamma(Y,f_*\mathcal O_X)=\Gamma(X,\mathcal O_X)
    \]
    is a finite $A$-module. Since $f$ is birational and $X,Y$ are integral, $A$ and $B$ have the same fraction field. Moreover, $B$ is integral over $A$, because it is finite as an $A$-module. But $A$ is integrally closed, since $Y$ is normal. Hence
    \[
        A=B.
    \]
    Therefore
    \[
        f_*\mathcal O_X=\mathcal O_Y,
    \]
    and the fibers of $f$ are connected.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Stein factorization]
    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a projective morphism of Noetherian schemes. Then $f$ factors as
    \[
        X\xrightarrow{f'}Y'\xrightarrow{g}Y,
    \]
    where $f'$ is projective with connected fibers and $g$ is finite.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    Let $Y'=\Spec_Y(f_*\mathcal O_X)$
    Since $f_*\mathcal O_X$ is a coherent sheaf of $\mathcal O_Y$-algebras, the natural morphism $g:Y'\to Y$ is finite. By construction, the morphism $f:X\to Y$ factors through $g$, so we obtain
    \[
        f':X\to Y'.
    \]
    Since $g$ is separated and $f$ is projective, the induced morphism $f'$ is projective. Moreover, by construction,
    \[
        f'_*\mathcal O_X=\mathcal O_{Y'}.
    \]
    Therefore, by the connected-fibers corollary, the fibers of $f'$ are connected. Hence
    \[
        X\xrightarrow{f'}Y'\xrightarrow{g}Y
    \]
    is the desired factorization.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Curves}
\subsubsection{Valuations and local rings}
\begin{definition}
    A \textbf{curve} is a one-dimensional variety, i.e.\ an integral separated scheme of finite type over a field $k$ such that every irreducible component has dimension $1$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
    Let $K$ be a field. A \textbf{valuation} on $K$ is a function
    \[v:K^\times\to \Z
    \]satisfying the following properties:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item For all $x,y\in K^\times$, $v(xy)=v(x)+v(y)$.
        \item For all $x,y\in K^\times$ with $x+y\neq 0$, $v(x+y)\ge \min\{v(x),v(y)\}$.
    \end{itemize}
\end{definition}
The set $R = \{x \in K : v(x) \ge 0\}$ is a subring of $K$ called the \textbf{valuation ring} of $v$. It is a local ring with maximal ideal $\mathfrak m = \{x \in K : v(x) > 0\} \cup \{0\}$. If $v(k^\times) = 0$, then we say that $v$ is a \textbf{discrete valuation} on $K/k$.

\begin{exercise}
    Let $k=\overline{k}$ and let $K=k(t)$. Show that every nontrivial discrete valuation
    \[
        v:K^\times\to \mathbb Z
    \]
    which is trivial on $k^\times$ is either the valuation at a finite point $t=a$, for some $a\in k$, or the valuation at infinity. Thus the nontrivial discrete valuations of $k(t)$ over $k$ naturally correspond to the points of $\mathbb P^1_k$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Let $v:k(t)^\times\to \mathbb{Z}$ be a nontrivial discrete valuation such that $v(c)=0$ for all $c\in k^\times$. We study the values $v(t-a)$ for $a\in k$. First, at most one of these values can be positive. Indeed, if $a\neq b$ and $v(t-a)>0, v(t-b)>0$ then the difference $0=v(b-a)\geq \min\{v(t-a),v(t-b)\}>0$ is a contradiction.

    Suppose first that $v(t-a)>0$ for some $a\in k$. Then for every $b\neq a$, $v(t-b)=0$ for otherwise we would have $0=v(b-a)\geq \min\{v(t-a),v(t-b)\}>0$. Since $k$ is algebraically closed, every polynomial factors as $f(t)=c\prod_i(t-a_i)$. Therefore only the factors with $a_i=a$ contribute to $v(f)$, and $v(f)=v(t-a)\operatorname{ord}_{t=a}(f)$. Thus for every rational function $f/g$, $v(f/g)=v(t-a)\operatorname{ord}_{t=a}(f/g)$. If $v$ is normalized, this gives $v=\operatorname{ord}_{t=a}$.

    Now suppose no $v(t-a)$ is positive. Then $v(t-a)\leq 0 \qquad \text{for all } a\in k$. If all these values were zero, then every polynomial, and hence every rational function, would have valuation zero, contradicting that $v$ is nontrivial. Hence for some $a\in k$, $v(t-a)<0$. For any $b\in k$, $t-b=(t-a)+(a-b)$, and since $v(t-a)<0, \qquad v(a-b)=0$, we get $v(t-b)=v(t-a)$. Write $v(t-a)=-m$ with $m>0$. Then $v(t-b)=-m \qquad \text{for all } b\in k$. Therefore, if $f(t)$ is a polynomial of degree $d$, then $v(f)=-md$. For a rational function $f/g$, we get $v(f/g)=-m\deg(f)+m\deg(g)=m(\deg(g)-\deg(f))$. But $\operatorname{ord}_\infty(f/g)=\deg(g)-\deg(f)$. Hence $v=m\operatorname{ord}_\infty$. Normalizing gives $v=\operatorname{ord}_\infty$. Thus every nontrivial discrete valuation of $k(t)$ over $k$ is, up to normalization, either $\operatorname{ord}_{t=a}$ for some $a\in k$, or $\operatorname{ord}_\infty$. These are precisely the valuations corresponding to the points of $\mathbb P^1_k$.
\end{solution}
Note that if $k$ is not algebraically closed, for example if $k=\mathbb R$, then there would be additional valuations corresponding to the irreducible quadratics. However these valuations are still coming from the closed points of $\mathbb P^1_k$.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $(R,\mathfrak m)$ be a Noetherian local domain of dimension $1$. Then the following are equivalent:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $R$ is a discrete valuation ring;
        \item $R$ is integrally closed;
        \item $R$ is a regular local ring;
        \item $\mathfrak m$ is a principal ideal.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

A \textbf{Dedekind domain} is a Noetherian integrally closed domain of dimension $1$. Being dimension $1$ means that all the prime ideals of $R$ are either $(0)$ or maximal. The local rings of a Dedekind domain are discrete valuation rings.

\begin{example}
    Let $X$ be a nonsingular affine curve over $\overline{k}$, and let
    \[
        R=\Gamma(X,\mathcal O_X)
    \]
    be its ring of regular functions. Then $R$ is a Noetherian domain of dimension $1$.

    Moreover, $R$ is integrally closed. Indeed, integral closedness can be checked locally, it suffices to show that for every maximal ideal $\mathfrak m\subset R$, the local ring $R_{\mathfrak m}$ is integrally closed. But $R_{\mathfrak m}\cong \mathcal O_{X,x}$
    where $x\in X$ is the point corresponding to $\mathfrak m$. Since $X$ is nonsingular, $\mathcal O_{X,x}$ is a regular local ring. Because $X$ is a curve, this is a Noetherian local domain of dimension $1$. Hence, by the equivalence between regular one-dimensional local domains and DVRs, $\mathcal O_{X,x}$ is a DVR, and in particular is integrally closed.
\end{example}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $R$ be a Dedekind domain that is a finitely generated $k$-algebra, and let $K$ be its field of fractions. Let $L$ be a finite extension of $K$, and let $S$ be the integral closure of $R$ in $L$. Then $S$ is also a Dedekind domain, and is also a finitely generated $k$-algebra.
\end{theorem}

\begin{lemma}
    Suppose $(S,\mathfrak n)$, $(R,\mathfrak m)$ are two discrete valuation rings of $K/k$, with $S \subset R$ and $\mathfrak n = S \cap \mathfrak m$. Then $S = R$ and $\mathfrak n = \mathfrak m$: they are the same valuation ring.
\end{lemma}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $K/\overline{k}$ be a finitely generated field extension of transcendence degree $1$, and let $x\in K^\times$.
    Then there are only finitely many discrete valuations $v$ of $K/\overline{k}$ such that $v(x)<0$.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
    It is enough to prove the corresponding statement for zeros. Set $y=x^{-1}$. Then $v(x)<0$ if and only if $v(y)>0$. Equivalently, if $R_v$ is the valuation ring of $v$ with maximal ideal $\mathfrak m_v$, then $v(y)>0$ if and only if $y\in \mathfrak m_v$. Thus we must show that there are only finitely many discrete valuation rings $(R_v,\mathfrak m_v)$ of $K/\overline{k}$ such that $y\in \mathfrak m_v$.

    If $y\in \overline{k}$, then $v(y)=0$ for every valuation of $K/\overline{k}$, so there is nothing to prove. Hence assume $y\notin \overline{k}$. Since $\operatorname{trdeg}_{\overline{k}}K=1$, the element $y$ is transcendental over $\overline{k}$, and $K$ is a finite extension of $\overline{k}(y)$. Let $B$ be the integral closure of $\overline{k}[y]$ inside $K$. By finiteness of integral closure, $B$ is a finitely generated $\overline{k}$-algebra. It is also a Dedekind domain with fraction field $K$.

    Let $(R_v,\mathfrak m_v)$ be a discrete valuation ring of $K/\overline{k}$ such that $y\in \mathfrak m_v$. Then $\overline{k}[y]\subset R_v$. Since $R_v$ is integrally closed in $K$ (being a DVR), and $B$ is the integral closure of $\overline{k}[y]$ in $K$, we get $B\subset R_v$. Set $\mathfrak n=\mathfrak m_v\cap B$. Then $\mathfrak n$ is a maximal ideal of $B$, and since $y\in \mathfrak m_v$, we have $y\in \mathfrak n$. Thus every such valuation gives a point $\mathfrak n\in V(y)\subset \Spec B$.

    Conversely, the valuation $R_v$ is determined by this point. Indeed, localizing gives $B_{\mathfrak n}\subset R_v$. Since $B$ is a Dedekind domain, $B_{\mathfrak n}$ is a DVR. Both $B_{\mathfrak n}$ and $R_v$ are DVRs inside the same field $K$, and their maximal ideals are compatible: $\mathfrak nB_{\mathfrak n}\subset \mathfrak m_v$. By the elementary lemma that two DVRs $S\subset R$ of the same field with compatible maximal ideals must be equal, we get $B_{\mathfrak n}=R_v$. Hence the valuations with $y\in \mathfrak m_v$ inject into the set $V(y)=\{\mathfrak n\in \Spec B : y\in \mathfrak n\}$.

    It remains to note that $V(y)$ is finite. Since $B$ is a finitely generated $\overline{k}$-domain of dimension $1$ and $0\neq y\in B$, the quotient $B/(y)$ is a zero-dimensional finitely generated $\overline{k}$-algebra. Hence it is Artinian, and therefore has only finitely many maximal ideals. Thus $V(y)$ is finite.

    Consequently, there are only finitely many valuations with $v(y)>0$, and therefore only finitely many valuations with $v(x)<0$.
\end{proof}

The construction of $\Spec B$ along with a maximal ideal $\mathfrak n \subset V(y)\subset \Spec B$ implies the following.
\begin{corollary}
    Let $v$ be a discrete valuation of the field $K/\overline{k}$, a finitely generated function field of transcendence degree $1$. Then there is a nonsingular curve $C$ with function field $K$, and a point $p \in C$, such that $v$ is the valuation induced by $p$.
\end{corollary}

Note that the affine model for such a valuation may change depending on the choice of $y$. However, there is a common projective curve behind all of them. There is a unique smooth projective curve $C$ with function field $K$ over $\overline{k}$. Each choice of $y\in K\setminus \overline{k}$ gives an affine open $Y \subset C$ obtained by removing the finitely many poles of $y$.

\begin{corollary}
    Let $y\in K\setminus \overline{k}$. Then all discrete valuations of $K/\overline{k}$ such that $v(y) \ge 0$ are accounted for by points on the curve $Y$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    $v(y)\ge 0$ means $y\in R_v$. In particular $y$ has a residue class in $R_v/m_v$. If $y\cong 0 \in R_v/m_v$, then $y\in m_v$, so $v(y)>0$ and apply the proof of the previous theorem. If $y\not\cong 0 \in R_v/m_v$, then $y-a$ has valuation $0$ for some $a\in \overline{k}$, so $v(y-a)=0$. Here we are using the algebraic closedness of $\overline{k}$ to identify the residue field of $R_v$ with $\overline{k}$, so that $\overline{k}$ sits inside $K$ with valuation $0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $X$ be a nonsingular curve, let $p\in X$, let $Y$ be a projective variety, and let $\phi\colon X-\{p\}\to Y$
    be a morphism. Then there exists a unique morphism $\overline{\phi}\colon X\to Y$ extending $\phi$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Since $Y$ is a variety, it is separated. Hence uniqueness follows: two morphisms $X\to Y$ which agree on the dense open subset $X-\{p\}$ must agree on all of $X$.

    It remains to prove existence. Since $Y$ is projective, choose a closed immersion $Y\hookrightarrow \mathbb P^n$. It suffices to show that the composite $X-\{p\}\to Y\hookrightarrow \mathbb P^n$ extends to a morphism $X\to \mathbb P^n$. Indeed, once such an extension exists, its image lands in $Y$, because $Y\subset \mathbb P^n$ is closed and the image of the dense open subset $X-\{p\}$ lies in $Y$.

    Thus assume $Y=\mathbb P^n$, with homogeneous coordinates $x_0,\dots,x_n$. Let $f\colon X-\{p\}\to \mathbb P^n$ be the given morphism. After changing homogeneous coordinates if necessary, we may assume that the image of $f$ meets the open set where all $x_i$ are nonzero. Then, for each $i,j$, the rational function $x_i/x_j$ on $\mathbb P^n$ pulls back to a rational function on $X$, which we denote by $f_{ij}\in k(X)$.

    Let $v=v_p$ be the valuation on $k(X)$ associated to the nonsingular point $p$. Fix the index $0$, and set $r_i=v(f_{i0})$ for $i=0,\dots,n$. Since $f_{ij}=f_{i0}/f_{j0}$, we have $v(f_{ij})=r_i-r_j$. Choose $k$ such that $r_k$ is minimal among the $r_i$. Then for every $i$, we have $v(f_{ik})=r_i-r_k\geq 0$. Hence each $f_{ik}$ is regular at $p$.

    Define a set-theoretic extension $\overline f\colon X\to \mathbb P^n$ by setting $\overline f=f$ on $X-\{p\}$ and
    \[
        \overline f(p)=\bigl[f_{0k}(p):\cdots:f_{nk}(p)\bigr].
    \]
    This is a well-defined point of $\mathbb P^n$, since $f_{kk}=1$, so the $k$th coordinate is $1$.

    It remains to check that $\overline f$ is a morphism near $p$. Let $U_k\subset \mathbb P^n$ be the affine chart where $x_k\neq 0$. Since $\overline f(p)\in U_k$, it is enough to show that $\overline f$ is a morphism from some affine neighborhood of $p$ into $U_k$. The coordinate ring of $U_k$ is
    \[
        k[x_0/x_k,\dots,\widehat{x_k/x_k},\dots,x_n/x_k].
    \]
    Under $\overline f$, the coordinate function $x_i/x_k$ pulls back to $f_{ik}$. But each $f_{ik}$ is regular at $p$, hence regular on some affine neighborhood of $p$. Therefore the functions $f_{0k},\dots,f_{nk}$ define a morphism from a neighborhood of $p$ to $U_k$. This agrees with $f$ on the punctured neighborhood, so $\overline f$ is a morphism near $p$.

    Thus $f$ extends to a morphism $X\to \mathbb P^n$, and consequently $\phi$ extends to a morphism $X\to Y$. This proves existence, and uniqueness was already shown.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
    There are two basic ways the extension theorem can fail.

    First, the target must be proper. For example, the rational function $t$ defines a rational map
    \[
        \mathbb P^1\dashrightarrow \mathbb A^1
    \]
    which is regular on $\mathbb A^1\subset \mathbb P^1$ but has a pole at $\infty$. It cannot extend to a morphism $\mathbb P^1\to \mathbb A^1$, since every global regular function on $\mathbb P^1$ is constant.

    Second, the source must be a nonsingular curve. For example,
    \[
        \mathbb A^2\dashrightarrow \mathbb P^1,
        \qquad
        (x,y)\mapsto [x:y],
    \]
    is defined away from the origin but cannot be extended over $(0,0)$. Along the line $y=mx$, the limiting value is $[1:m]$, which depends on the tangent direction. Algebraically, an extension with value in the chart $X\neq 0$ would require $y/x$ to be regular at the origin, while an extension with value in the chart $Y\neq 0$ would require $x/y$ to be regular.
\end{remark}

\begin{exercise}
    Show that if $U$ and $V$ are two open subsets of $\mathbb A^1$, then $U\simeq V$ only if $\mathbb A^1-U$ and $\mathbb A^1-V$ have the same number of points. Is the converse true?
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Let
    $U=\mathbb A^1-\{P_1,\dots,P_r\}$ and $V=\mathbb A^1-\{Q_1,\dots,Q_s\}$.
    View both as open subsets of $\mathbb P^1$. Then
    $\mathbb P^1-U=\{\infty,P_1,\dots,P_r\}$ and
    $\mathbb P^1-V=\{\infty,Q_1,\dots,Q_s\}$.

    Suppose $U\simeq V$. Since $U$ and $V$ are dense open subsets of $\mathbb P^1$, this isomorphism gives a birational map $\mathbb P^1\dashrightarrow \mathbb P^1$. Because $\mathbb P^1$ is nonsingular and projective, the map and its inverse extend to morphisms $\mathbb P^1\to \mathbb P^1$. These extensions are inverse to each other, since their compositions agree with the identity on dense open subsets. Hence the original isomorphism extends to an automorphism of $\mathbb P^1$.

    Therefore the automorphism sends the complement of $U$ in $\mathbb P^1$ bijectively onto the complement of $V$ in $\mathbb P^1$. Thus
    $|\mathbb P^1-U|=|\mathbb P^1-V|$, so $r+1=s+1$, and hence $r=s$.

    The converse is false in general. For example, over $k=\mathbb C$, consider
    $U=\mathbb A^1-\{0,1,2\}$ and $V=\mathbb A^1-\{0,1,3\}$.
    If $U\simeq V$, then the isomorphism would extend to an automorphism of $\mathbb P^1$ carrying
    $\{\infty,0,1,2\}$ onto $\{\infty,0,1,3\}$. Checking the cross ratio of these four points shows that no such automorphism exists.
\end{solution}


\begin{theorem}
    Every nonsingular separated curve $C$ is quasiprojective.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Since $C$ is Noetherian, we may cover it by finitely many nonsingular affine open subsets
    \[
        C=\bigcup_{i=1}^r U_i.
    \]
    For each $i$, choose a closed immersion $U_i\hookrightarrow \mathbb P^{n_i}.$ Let $Y_i\subset \mathbb P^{n_i}$ be the projective closure of $U_i$.

    The open immersion $U_i\hookrightarrow Y_i$ defines a morphism from the open subset $U_i\subset C$ to the projective variety $Y_i$. Since $C$ is nonsingular and $Y_i$ is projective, the extension theorem for morphisms from punctured nonsingular curves implies that this map extends uniquely to a morphism
    \[
        \phi_i:C\to Y_i.
    \]
    Taking the product of these morphisms gives
    \[
        \phi=(\phi_1,\dots,\phi_r):C\to \prod_{i=1}^r Y_i.
    \]
    Since each $Y_i$ is projective, their product is projective. By the Segre embedding, we may view $\prod_i Y_i$ as a closed subvariety of some projective space $\mathbb P^N$. Let
    \[
        Y=\overline{\phi(C)}\subset \mathbb P^N
    \]
    be the closure of the image. Then $Y$ is projective, and $\phi(C)$ is a locally closed subset of $Y$. Since $C$ is a curve and $\phi$ is nonconstant on each irreducible component, $Y$ has dimension $1$.

    We claim that $\phi$ is an isomorphism from $C$ onto its image $\phi(C)$. It is enough to construct a morphism
    \[
        \psi:\phi(C)\to C
    \]
    which is inverse to $\phi$ on points.

    Fix $i$. On $U_i$, the morphism $\phi$ factors as
    \[
        U_i \longrightarrow C \xrightarrow{\phi} \prod_j Y_j \longrightarrow Y_i,
    \]
    and the composite is precisely the original open immersion
    \[
        U_i\hookrightarrow Y_i.
    \]
    Therefore, on the subset $\phi(U_i)\subset \phi(C)$, we may recover the point of $U_i$ by projecting to $Y_i$ and then using the inverse of the open immersion onto its image. Thus we obtain a morphism
    \[
        \psi_i:\phi(U_i)\to U_i
    \]
    which is inverse to $\phi|_{U_i}$.

    On overlaps $\phi(U_i)\cap \phi(U_j)$, the morphisms $\psi_i$ and $\psi_j$ agree on the dense open subset corresponding to $U_i\cap U_j$. Since $C$ is separated, morphisms into $C$ which agree on a dense open subset agree wherever both are defined. Hence the $\psi_i$ glue to a morphism
    \[
        \psi:\phi(C)\to C.
    \]
    By construction, $\psi$ is inverse to $\phi$ on points. Therefore $\phi:C\to \phi(C)$
    is an isomorphism. Thus $C$ is isomorphic to the locally closed subset $\phi(C)$ of the projective variety $Y\subset \mathbb P^N$. Hence $C$ is quasiprojective.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}
    Every nonsingular separated curve is birational to a projective curve.
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}
    The proof invokes two important facts that I was not familiar.

    Let $f:X\to Y$ be a morphism of varieties over a field $k$. Then $\dim \overline{f(X)}\leq \dim X$. In particular, if $X$ is a curve and $f$ is nonconstant, then $\overline{f(X)}$ is a curve.


    \begin{proof}
        First assume $X$ is irreducible, and let $Z=\overline{f(X)}$ with the reduced induced structure. Then $f$ factors through a dominant morphism $X\to Z$, hence induces an inclusion of function fields $k(Z)\hookrightarrow k(X)$. Therefore $\operatorname{trdeg}_k k(Z)\leq \operatorname{trdeg}_k k(X)$. Since dimension equals transcendence degree for irreducible varieties, $\dim Z\leq \dim X$.

        In general, if $X_1,\dots,X_r$ are the irreducible components of $X$, then $\overline{f(X)}=\bigcup_i \overline{f(X_i)}$, so $\dim \overline{f(X)}=\max_i \dim \overline{f(X_i)}\leq \max_i\dim X_i=\dim X$. \qedhere
    \end{proof}

    Let $X$ and $Y$ be reduced separated schemes. Suppose there are morphisms $f:X\to Y$ and $g:Y\to X$ which are inverse to one another on the set of closed points. Then $f$ and $g$ are inverse isomorphisms.
    \begin{proof}
        We show $g\circ f=\id_X$; the proof of $f\circ g=\id_Y$ is identical. Consider the two morphisms $g\circ f,\id_X:X\to X$.
        Pull the diagonal $\Delta_X\subset X\times X$ back along the morphism $(g\circ f,\id_X):X\to X\times X$. This gives a closed subscheme $E \to X$ whose set of closed points $E$ is the set of points $x\in X$ such that  the two maps to $X$ agree as morphisms after passing to the residue field $\kappa(x)$. Being the pullback of a closed immersion, $E\to X$ is a closed immersion.

        By assumption, $g\circ f$ and $\id_X$ agree on the underlying topological space, so $E$ has underlying closed subset all of $X$. If $\mathcal I\subset \mathcal O_X$ is the ideal sheaf defining $E\hookrightarrow X$, then $\sqrt{\mathcal I}=0$. Since $X$ is reduced, $\mathcal I=0$. Hence $E=X$, so $g\circ f=\id_X$ as morphisms.

        Similarly, using that $Y$ is reduced and separated, one gets $f\circ g=\id_Y$. Therefore $f$ and $g$ are inverse isomorphisms. \qedhere
    \end{proof}
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}
    Let $C_1$ and $C_2$ be separated nonsingular curves, and let
    \[
        \alpha:C_1\dashrightarrow C_2
    \]
    be a birational map. Then $C_1$ and $C_2$ can be glued along $\alpha$: there exists a nonsingular curve
    \[
        C=U_1\cup U_2
    \]
    such that $U_i\simeq C_i$, and the birational map $U_1\dashrightarrow U_2$ induced by this gluing is $\alpha$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
    Since $C_1$ and $C_2$ are birational, there are dense open subsets which are identified by $\alpha$. Thus we may choose a common dense open curve
    \[
        V\subset C_1,\qquad V\subset C_2.
    \]
    By quasiprojectivity of nonsingular separated curves, we may regard each $C_i$ as an open subvariety of a projective curve
    \[
        C_i\subset Y_i\subset \mathbb P^{n_i}.
    \]
    Consider the morphism
    \[
        \phi:V\to \mathbb P^{n_1}\times \mathbb P^{n_2}
    \]
    given by the two embeddings of $V$ into $C_1$ and $C_2$.

    The rational map
    \[
        \phi_1:C_1\dashrightarrow \mathbb P^{n_1}\times \mathbb P^{n_2}
    \]
    extends to a morphism, because $C_1$ is nonsingular and the target is projective. Similarly, we get a morphism
    \[
        \phi_2:C_2\to \mathbb P^{n_1}\times \mathbb P^{n_2}.
    \]
    Moreover, each $\phi_i$ is an isomorphism onto its image: for example, projecting
    \[
        \phi_1(C_1)\subset \mathbb P^{n_1}\times \mathbb P^{n_2}
    \]
    to the first factor recovers the original embedding of $C_1$.

    Now define
    \[
        C=\phi_1(C_1)\cup \phi_2(C_2)
        \subset \mathbb P^{n_1}\times \mathbb P^{n_2}.
    \]
    The two images contain the common open subset $\phi(V)$, and their union is precisely the gluing of $C_1$ and $C_2$ along the birational identification given by $\alpha$. Since each $\phi_i(C_i)$ is nonsingular and the two curves agree on the overlap, $C$ is nonsingular. Also, since $C$ is a subvariety of a projective variety, it is separated.
\end{proof}

The above proposition suggests how to construct a projective curve birational to a given nonsingular separated curve $C$. Let $K/\overline{k}$ be a finitely generated field extension of transcendence degree $1$. Choose $y\in K\setminus \overline{k}$ and let $B$ be the integral closure of $\overline{k}[y]$ in $K$. Then $Y_0=\Spec B$ is a nonsingular affine curve with function field $K$. Its closed points correspond to the discrete valuations $v$ of $K/\overline{k}$ for which $v(y)\geq 0$.

The missing valuations are precisely those for which $v(y)<0$. By the finiteness theorem, there are only finitely many such valuations, say $v_1,\dots,v_r$. For each $v_i$, choose a nonsingular affine curve $Y_i$ with function field $K$ such that $v_i$ is represented by a closed point of $Y_i$. Since all the $Y_i$ have the same function field $K$, they are birational. Gluing the finitely many curves $Y_0,Y_1,\dots,Y_r$ along their common birational open subsets gives a nonsingular separated curve $C$ with function field $K$.

By construction, every discrete valuation of $K/\overline{k}$ occurs as the local ring at some point of $C$. Thus $C$ has no missing valuation points. This is the precise sense in which one has glued finitely many birational affine curves together until the result is complete. Since $C$ is nonsingular and separated, it is quasiprojective; and the valuative criterion then shows that $C$ is proper. Hence $C$ is projective.
\begin{lemma}
    Let $X$ be a quasiprojective scheme over $k$. If $X$ is proper over $k$, then $X$ is projective over $k$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
    Since $X$ is quasiprojective, there is an open immersion
    \[
        j:X\hookrightarrow \overline X
    \]
