    \item In particular, the space of harmonic forms is at most $2g$-dimensional because it injects into the space of period vectors $\R^{2g}$.
    \item There are $g$ linearly independent holomorphic differentials on $R$ and $g$ linearly independent antiholomorphic differentials on $R$, where holomorphic and antiholomorphic are defined as the $\pm i$ eigenspaces of the Hodge star operator $*$. Since eigenspaces don't intersect, we have \begin{align*}
              \dim H & \geq 2g
          \end{align*} Combining with the previous point, we have \begin{align*}
              \dim H & = 2g
          \end{align*} and the holomorphic and antiholomorphic differentials give bases for the eigenspaces.
\end{enumerate}

Let $a_1, \ldots, a_g, b_1, \ldots, b_g$ be the edges of the polygonal decomposition of $R$. Let $\phi, \phi'$ be holomorphic differentials, and $A_i = \int_{a_i} \phi$, $B_i = \int_{b_i} \phi$ and similarly for $\phi'$. Using Stokes' theorem, we can compute \begin{align*}
    0 & = \int_R \phi \wedge \phi' = \sum_{i=1}^g \left( \int_{a_i} \phi \int_{b_i} \phi' - \int_{b_i} \phi \int_{a_i} \phi' \right) = \sum_{i=1}^g (A_i B_i' - B_i A_i')
\end{align*} where the second equality comes from writing $\phi = dF$ (locally! there is monodromy) so that \begin{align*}
    \int_X \phi \wedge \phi' & = \int_P dF\wedge\phi'                                                                                                                               \\
                             & = \int_P d(F\phi')-F\,d\phi'                                                                                                                         \\
                             & = \oint_{\partial P} F\,\phi'                                                                                                                        \\
                             & = \sum_{i=1}^g \left( \int_{a_i} F\phi' + \int_{b_i} F\phi' - \int_{b_i^{-1}} F\phi' - \int_{a_i^{-1}} F\phi' \right)                                \\
                             & = \sum_{i=1}^g \left( \int_{a_i}F\phi' - \int_{a_i^{-1}} (F\phi') \right) + \sum_{i=1}^g \left( \int_{b_i} F\phi' - \int_{b_i^{-1}} (F\phi') \right) \\
\end{align*} and the difference between integrating over $a_i$ and $a_i^{-1}$ is given by the period of $\phi$ over the cycle, that is precisely $A_i$. Similarly for $b_i$.
We also have the relation \begin{align*}
    \sum_i (A_i \overline{B_i} - B_i \overline{A_i}) = i\langle\phi, \phi'\rangle
\end{align*}
We have the following consequences:
\begin{itemize}
    \item For any nonzero holomorphic differential $\phi$
          \begin{align*}
              \Im( \sum _i A_i \overline{B_i} ) & > 0
          \end{align*}
    \item A holomorphic differential with all periods real is zero. This is because \begin{align*}
              \sum_i (A_i \overline{B_i} - B_i \overline{A_i}) & = 0 = i\langle\phi, \phi'\rangle
          \end{align*} so $\phi = 0$.
    \item We can choose a basis of holomorphic differentials $\phi_1, \ldots, \phi_g$ such that \begin{align*}
              \int_{a_i} \phi_j & = \delta_{ij}
          \end{align*} Then the period matrix \begin{align*}
              \Pi_{ij} & = \int_{b_i} \phi_j
          \end{align*} is symmetric with positive definite imaginary part. In particular \begin{align*}
              [A | B] \to [I | \Pi]
          \end{align*} where $[A|B]$ is the old period matrix, $\Pi$ is symmetric and $\Im \Pi$ is positive definite.
    \item The periods form a lattice in $\C^g$ of rank $2g$.
\end{itemize}
We are finally able to give a criterion for when a divisor is principal, completing the multiplicative Cousin problem.
\begin{theorem}[Abel's theorem]
    A divisor $D = \sum n_i p_i$ on a compact Riemann surface $R$ is principal if and only if \begin{align*}
        \deg D                               & = 0         \\
        \sum n_i \int_{p_0}^{p_i} \vec{\Phi} & \in \Lambda
    \end{align*} for all $j=1, \ldots, g$, where $\vec{\Phi} = (\phi_1, \ldots, \phi_g)$ is a vector whose entries are a basis of holomorphic differentials normalized so that $\int_{a_i} \phi_j = \delta_{ij}$ and $\Lambda$ is the period lattice generated by the integrals of the $\phi_j$ over the $a_i$ and $b_i$ cycles.
\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}
    The \textbf{Jacobian variety} of a compact Riemann surface $R$ of genus $g$ is the complex torus \begin{align*}
        J(R) & = \C^g / \Lambda
    \end{align*} where $\Lambda$ is the period lattice generated by the integrals of a basis of holomorphic differentials over the $a_i$ and $b_i$ cycles.
\end{definition}
The Jacobian is independent of the choice of basis of holomorphic differentials and choice of symplectic basis of $H_1(R, \Z)$ up to isomorphism of complex tori. It is a compact complex manifold of dimension $g$, and an abelian group. One can show that every connected abelian group which is a complex manifold (and where the group structure is holomorphic) is a quotient of $\C^g$ by a co-compact lattice. The specific properties of $[A|B]$ ($A^{-1}B$ is symmetric with positive
definite imaginary part) impose a further restriction. They give rise to principally polarized abelian varieties.

We can give another interpretation of Abel's theorem.
\begin{theorem}
    [Abel's theorem] The kernel of the Abel-Jacobi map \begin{align*}
        \operatorname{Div}^0(R) & \to J(R)                                     \\
        D = \sum n_i p_i        & \mapsto \sum n_i \int_{p_0}^{p_i} \vec{\Phi}
    \end{align*} is precisely the group of principal divisors on $R$. In particular, the Abel-Jacobi map descends to an isomorphism \begin{align*}
        \operatorname{Pic}^0(R) & \to J(R)
    \end{align*}
    $J$ is isomorphic to the group $\Div^0(R)/\PDiv(R)$ of isomorphism classes of
    degree-zero line bundles on $R$.
\end{theorem}


On line bundles, the group operation is the tensor product.
Note that both sides have natural holomorphic structures:
the divisor side can be built from symmetric powers of $R$,
and the period map to $J$ was given by integrating holomorphic forms,
and is thus holomorphic:
so we should suspect a stronger statement behind this.

Choose a point $r \in R$.
On the product $R \times J$, there is the family of holomorphic line bundles
over $R$ parametrized by the points of $J$, with trivialized fibers over the point $r$.
Without trivializing at $r$, each line bundle has a group $\C^\times$ of automorphisms,
and the family of line bundles is not uniquely determined:
for instance, you can tensor it with your favorite line bundle over the base $J$,
without changing the isomorphism type on any factor of $R$.

It turns out that these individual line bundles on $R$
assemble to a holomorphic line bundle
\[
    \mathcal P \;\to\; R \times J,
\]
the \textbf{universal} or \textbf{Poincaré} line bundle (of degree zero). These objects $\mathcal P$ and $J$ can be characterized by a universal property:

\begin{proposition}
    Fix $r\in R$. There exists a line bundle $\mathcal P$ on $R\times J$
    together with a trivialization
    \[
        \mathcal P|_{\{r\}\times J} \cong \mathcal O_J
    \]
    such that for any complex space $B$ and line bundle $L$ on $R\times B$
    equipped with a trivialization along $\{r\}\times B$, there exists a unique
    morphism $f_L:B\to J$ and a unique isomorphism
    \[
        (\mathrm{id}_R\times f_L)^*\mathcal P \cong L
    \]
    compatible with the given trivializations.
\end{proposition}



For each degree $d \ge 0$, we have a holomorphic map
\[
    AJ_d : \Sym^d R \longrightarrow J_d,
\]
defined by integrating the holomorphic differentials:
choose an auxiliary base point $r \in R$, and then send a divisor
$D \in \Sym^d R$ to the sum of integrals along a chosen collection of paths
from $r$ to the points of $D$.
A change of paths will change the integrals by an integral linear combination of periods,
so the map to $J_0$ is well defined;
now use the divisor $d\{r\}$ to identify $J_0$ with $J_d$,
and the resulting map to $J_d$ is now also independent of your choice of $r$. The image of the divisor $D$
is the isomorphism class of the line bundle $\mathcal{O}(D)$. In particular, the image of the Abel-Jacobi map is precisely the set of isomorphism classes of line bundles which admit a nontrivial holomorphic section
(i.e., those line bundles which can be represented as $\mathcal{O}(D)$ for some effective divisor $D$).

\begin{remark}
    [Identifing the differential of $AJ_d$] First we identify the tangent space of $J_d$ at a point $[L]$ representing a line bundle $L$ on $R$. Since $J_d \cong J_0 \cong H^0(R,K_R)^*/H_1(R,\Z)$, we have
    \[T_{J_d,[L]} \cong H^0(R,K_R)^*.
    \]

    Use the Abel-Jacobi map in holomorphic 1-form coordinates. Pick a basis $\{\omega_1,\dots,\omega_g\}$ of $H^0(R,K_R)$ and periods so that
    \[
        AJ_1(p)=\left(\int_{r_0}^p \omega_1,\dots,\int_{r_0}^p \omega_g\right)\in J
    \]
    For reduced $D=p_1+\cdots+p_d$, we have $AJ_d(D)=\sum_{i=1}^d AJ_1(p_i)$. Let $v=(v_1,\dots,v_d)\in \bigoplus_i T_{p_i}R$ be tangent to $\Sym^dR$ at $D$. Vary $p_i(t)$ with $ p'_i(0)=v_i$. Then the derivative of an integral of a holomorphic form along a path with moving endpoint is just evaluation of the form at that endpoint against the velocity vector:
    \[
        \left.\frac{d}{dt}\right|_{t=0}\int_{r_0}^{p_i(t)}\omega_k=\omega_k(p_i)(v_i)
    \]
    Summing over $i$ gives the differential
    \[
        d(AJ_d)_D: \bigoplus_{i=1}^d T_{p_i}R \longrightarrow H^0(R,K_R)^*
    \]
    \[
        (v_1,\dots,v_d) \longmapsto \left(\omega \mapsto \sum_{i=1}^d \omega(p_i)(v_i)\right)
    \]
    The situation is more delicate if $D$ is not reduced. Choose a local coordinate $z$ near each $p_i$. Write $\omega = f(z)\,dz$, so $f(z)$ is holomorphic.

    A tangent vector to $\Sym^d R$ at $D$ corresponds to a choice of infinitesimal deformation of each $m_i p_i$, i.e. an element of $H^0(\mathcal O_R(D)|_D) \cong \bigoplus_i \mathbb C[z]/(z^{m_i})$. That means you specify the coefficients of $(1, z, z^2, \ldots, z^{m_i-1})$ at each $p_i$.

    Then the differential of the Abel-Jacobi map acts by:
    \[
        d(AJ_d)_D: \bigoplus_i \mathbb C[z]/(z^{m_i}) \longrightarrow H^0(K_R)^*
    \]
    \[
        (v_{i,0}, v_{i,1}, \ldots, v_{i,m_i-1})_i \mapsto \left(\omega \mapsto \sum_i \sum_{k=0}^{m_i-1} \frac{1}{k!}\, \omega^{(k)}(p_i)\, v_{i,k}\right)
    \]
    where $\omega^{(k)}(p_i)$ means the $k$-th derivative (the $k$-jet) of the local coefficient function $f(z)$ of $\omega=f(z)\,dz$.

    In general, there is a uniform way to describe the differential valid for all $D$:
    The differential \[d(AJ_d)_D : H^0(\mathcal O_R(D)|_D) \to H^1(\mathcal O_R)\] is given by the connecting homomorphism in the long exact sequence \[0 \to \mathcal O_R \to \mathcal O_R(D) \to \mathcal O_R(D)|_D \to 0\]
\end{remark}

The Abel Jacobi map will hold the key to Riemann's solution of the multiplicative Cousin problem,
but we need to settle some of its properties first.
The word generic in the next theorem means
away from an analytic subspace of strictly lower dimension.
Analytic subspaces are locally cut out by a collection of holomorphic functions; they are usually not submanifolds,
but they are always stratified by locally closed submanifolds of decreasing dimensions.

\begin{theorem}[Properties of the Abel-Jacobi map]
    Let $AJ_d : \Sym^d(R) \to J_d$ be the Abel-Jacobi map which sends a degree $d$ effective divisor $D$ on $R$ to the isomorphism class of the line bundle $\mathcal{O}(D)$.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item The fiber of $AJ_d$ at a point $j \in J$ representing a line bundle $L$ on $R$
              is isomorphic to the projective space $\mathbb{P}H^0(R;L)$.
              (This is empty if $h^0(R;L)=0$.)
        \item For $d \le g$, the map $AJ_d$ is generically injective,
              and a local embedding in $J_d$.
        \item For $d = g$, it is also surjective,
              and therefore a generic isomorphism.
        \item For $d \ge 2g - 1$, the map $AJ_d$ realizes $\Sym^d(R)$
              as a holomorphic projective bundle over $J_d$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    \leavevmode
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item A point in the fiber over $j$ is an effective divisor $D$ of degree $d$
              such that $\mathcal{O}(D) \cong L$.
              The set of such divisors is in bijection with the set of nonzero holomorphic sections
              of $L$, modulo scaling,
              which is precisely $\mathbb{P}H^0(R;L)$.
        \item The fibers are connected. Local injectivity/isomorphism can be checked by computing the differential of $AJ_d$ and checking that it is injective at generic points. The differential at $(p_1 + \cdots + p_d) \in \Sym^d R$ is given by the map \begin{align*}
                  T_{p_1} R \oplus \cdots \oplus T_{p_d} R & \to T_{AJ_d(p_1 + \cdots + p_d)} J_d \cong H^0(R, K_R)^*       \\
                  (v_1, \ldots, v_d)                       & \mapsto \left( \omega \mapsto \sum_{i=1}^d \omega(v_i) \right)
              \end{align*} The map fails to be injective if and only if there exist nonzero $v_i \in T_{p_i} R$ such that \begin{align*}
                  \sum_{i=1}^d \omega(v_i) & = 0
              \end{align*} for all $\omega \in H^0(R, K_R)$. This is equivalent to saying that there exists a holomorphic differential $\omega$ vanishing at all $p_i$. By Riemann-Roch, for generic choices of $p_i$, there are no nontrivial holomorphic differentials vanishing at all $p_i$ if $d \leq g$. Hence, the differential is injective at generic points, making $AJ_d$ a local embedding.
        \item The argument is the same as local injectivity.
        \item By Riemann-Roch, for $d \geq 2g - 1$, we have \begin{align*}
                  h^0(R, L) & = d - g + 1
              \end{align*} for all line bundles $L$ of degree $d$ because $H^1(R, L) = 0$ by Serre duality. Therefore, the fibers of $AJ_d$ are all projective spaces of dimension $d - g$. This gives $\Sym^d(R)$ the structure of a holomorphic projective bundle over $J_d$. The map is holomorphic submersion because the differential is surjective at all points, and moreover the map is proper since the domain and target are compact. Hence, by Ehresmann's fibration theorem, it is a holomorphic fiber bundle with fibers $\mathbb{P}^{d-g}$.
              \qedhere
    \end{enumerate}
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Non-effective line bundles of nonnegative degree]\label{rmk:non-effective}

    The discussion of the Abel Jacobi map shows that for $g \geq 1$, there exist line bundles of degree $0 \leq d < g$ with no nontrivial holomorphic sections.

    Consider the Abel-Jacobi map
    \[
        i_r:X\longrightarrow \Pic^0(X),\qquad s\longmapsto \mathcal{O}_X(s-r).
    \]
    Its image is a 1-dimensional subvariety of the $g$-dimensional torus $\Pic^0(X)$ and hence cannot be all of $\Pic^0(X)$.

    Pick a degree-zero line bundle $T\in\Pic^0(X)$ not in $i_r(X)$.
    Set
    \[
        L = \mathcal{O}_X(r)\otimes T \in \Pic^1(X).
    \]
    Then $\deg L=1$, but $h^0(X,L)=0$. This is because if $h^0(X,L)>0$ then $L\simeq \mathcal{O}_X(p)$ for some $p\in X$. Hence
    $T \simeq L\otimes \mathcal{O}_X(-r) \simeq \mathcal{O}_X(p-r)$,
    so $T\in i_r(X)$, contradicting the choice of $T$. Thus $L$ is a non-effective class of nonnegative degree.
    More generally, for any $0\le d<g$, choose $T\in\Pic^0(X)\setminus (W_d - D_0)$ and set $L=\mathcal{O}_X(D_0)\otimes T$ with $\deg D_0=d$; then $h^0(X,L)=0$.
\end{remark}

\subsubsection{Exercises}
\begin{exercise}
    Show that every vector bundle on $\mathbb{P}^1$ is a direct sum of line bundles.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
    Calculate $\Pic$ of the nodal cubic and cuspidal cubic.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
Suppose $f$ and $g$ are (nice) homogeneous polynomials of degrees d and e in $\mathbb{C}[X_0, \ldots, X_n]$.  Let $X$ be the subvariety of $\mathbb{P}^n$ cut out by $f$ and $g$.  Calculate the cohomology of $\mathcal{O}_X$.
\end{exercise}

\section{Lie groups and Lie algebras}

\subsection{Lie Groups and Algebraic Groups}The primary reference for this section is Kirillov's book \textit{An Introduction to Lie Groups and Lie Algebras} chapters 2-7.

\subsubsection{Exponential Map and Campbell--Hausdorff Formula}
\begin{proposition}
    Let $G$ be a Lie group, $\mathfrak g = T_1G$, and let $x\in \mathfrak g$. Then there exists a unique
    morphism of Lie groups
    \[
        \gamma_x \colon \mathbb R \to G
    \]
    such that
    \[
        \dot\gamma_x(0)=x,
    \]
    where the dot denotes differentiation with respect to $t$. The map $\gamma_x$ will be called the
    one-parameter subgroup corresponding to $x$.
\end{proposition}

The uniqueness immediately implies that for any $\lambda\in \mathbb R$
\[
    \gamma_x(\lambda t)=\gamma_{\lambda x}(t)
\]
\begin{definition}
    Let $G$ be a Lie group, $\mathfrak g=T_1G$. Then the \textbf{exponential map}
    \[
        \exp \colon \mathfrak g \to G
    \]
    is defined by
    \[
        \exp(x)=\gamma_x(1),
    \]
    where $\gamma_x(t)$ is the one-parameter subgroup with tangent vector at $1$ equal to $x$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
    \leavevmode
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\exp(x)=1+x+\cdots$ (that is, $\exp(0)=1$ and
              $\exp_*(0)\colon \mathfrak g \to T_1G=\mathfrak g$ is the identity map).

        \item The exponential map is a diffeomorphism of some neighborhood of $0$ in $\mathfrak g$
              with a neighborhood of $1$ in $G$. The local inverse map will be denoted by $\log$.

        \item $\exp((t+s)x)=\exp(tx)\exp(sx)$.

        \item For any morphism of Lie groups $\varphi\colon G_1 \to G_2$ and any
              $x\in \mathfrak g_1$, we have
              \[
                  \exp(\varphi_*(x))=\varphi(\exp(x)).
              \]

        \item For any $X\in G$, $y\in \mathfrak g$, we have
              \[
                  X\exp(y)X^{-1}=\exp(\operatorname{Ad}_X(y)),
              \]
              where $\operatorname{Ad}$ is the adjoint action of $G$ on $\mathfrak g$
              defined by~(2.3).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

In general, the exponential map is not surjective. However, it can be shown that for compact Lie groups, the exponential map is surjective.

If $x,y\in \mathfrak g$ are small enough, then $\exp(x)\exp(y)$ is also close to $1$, so it can be written as $\exp(z)$ for some $z\in \mathfrak g$. The Campbell--Hausdorff formula gives an explicit expression for $z$ in terms of $x,y$ and the Lie bracket.
\begin{theorem}[Campbell--Hausdorff formula]
    For small enough $x,y\in \mathfrak g$ one has
    \[
        \exp(x)\exp(y)=\exp(\mu(x,y))
    \]
    for some $\mathfrak g$-valued function $\mu(x,y)$ which is given by the following
    series which converges in some neighborhood of $(0,0)$:
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:3.14}
        \mu(x,y)=x+y+\sum_{n\ge 2}\mu_n(x,y),
    \end{equation}
    where $\mu_n(x,y)$ is a Lie polynomial in $x,y$ of degree $n$, i.e.\ an expression
    consisting of commutators of $x,y$, their commutators, etc., of total degree $n$ in $x,y$. This expression is universal: it does not depend on the Lie algebra
    $\mathfrak g$ or on the choice of $x,y$.

    It is possible to write the expression for $\mu$ explicitly:
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:3.15}
        \mu(x,y)
        = x+y+\frac{1}{2}[x,y]
        +\frac{1}{12}\big([x,[x,y]]+[y,[y,x]]\big)+\cdots.
    \end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}
    The group operation in a connected Lie group $G$ can be recovered from the
    commutator in $\mathfrak g = T_1G$. Indeed, locally the group law is determined by the Campbell--Hausdorff formula, and $G$ is generated by a neighborhood of $1$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{exercise}
    Is the exponential map $\exp : \mathfrak{sl}(2, \mathbb{R}) \to \mathrm{SL}(2, \mathbb{R})$ surjective?
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    No. The matrix $\begin{pmatrix} -2 & 0 \\ 0 & -\frac{1}{2} \end{pmatrix}$ is in $\mathrm{SL}(2, \mathbb{R})$ but is not in the image of the exponential map. This is because if this matrix had a logarithm, then the logarithm would have eigenvalues $\log(-2) + 2\pi i k$ and $\log(-\frac{1}{2}) + 2\pi i m$ for some integers $k,m$. However, the logarithm being real, would have to have eigenvalues coming in complex conjugate pairs, which is not the case here.
\end{solution}
In general, the exponential map is surjective for compact connected Lie groups and for connected, simply connected nilpotent Lie groups. This is because for compact groups, every element is contained in a maximal torus, and the exponential map is clearly surjective on the torus. The map $\exp: \mathfrak{t} \to T$ is a surjective group homomorphism with kernel given by the cocharacter lattice of $G$ (by definition). This intermediate lattice determines the global form of $G$ among connected compact groups with fixed semisimmple Lie algebra. The simply connected form has the smallest exponential lattice $\Lambda_G = Q^\vee$ and the adjoint form has the largest $\Lambda_G = P^\vee$.


\subsubsection{Lie Group--Lie Algebra Correspondence}

\begin{proposition}
    If \(G\) is a Lie group, then $\Lie(G)=T_eG$ with Lie bracket induced by left-invariant vector fields. In particular, every $v\in T_eG$ extends to a unique left-invariant vector field on $G$, and the bracket of two vector fields is defined by the action of first order differential operators. The bracket respects $G$-equivariance, so it induces a Lie algebra structure on $T_eG$. If \(f:G\to H\) is a Lie group homomorphism, then the differential at the identity
    \[
        (df)_e:\Lie(G)\to\Lie(H)
    \]
    is a Lie algebra homomorphism.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proposition}
    Let \(G\) be connected, and let \(f_1,f_2:G\to H\) be Lie group homomorphisms.
    If $(df_1)_e=(df_2)_e$ then \(f_1=f_2\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
    For any $X\in \Lie(G)$, the one-parameter subgroup $t\mapsto \exp_G(tX)$ is sent by $f_i$ to
    \[
        f_i(\exp_G(tX))=\exp_H\bigl(t\,(df_i)_e(X)\bigr).
    \]
    Since the differentials agree, these are equal for $i=1,2$. Hence
    \[
        f_1(\exp_G(tX))=f_2(\exp_G(tX))
    \]
    for all $t$, so $f_1$ and $f_2$ agree on all one-parameter subgroups. Because $G$ is connected, it is generated by any neighborhood of the identity, and in particular by exponentials from a small neighborhood in $\Lie(G)$. Therefore $f_1=f_2$ on all of $G$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Lie's second theorem]
    Let \(G\) be a connected and simply connected Lie group with Lie algebra \(\mathfrak g\), and let \(H\) be a Lie group with Lie algebra \(\mathfrak h\).
    For every Lie algebra homomorphism $
        \varphi:\mathfrak g\to\mathfrak h$
    there exists a unique Lie group homomorphism
    $\Phi:G\to H$ such that $d\Phi_e = \varphi$.
\end{theorem}

The following examples show that the hypothesis in the previous theorem are necessary. \begin{example}
    Let $G_1=S^1=\mathbb R/\mathbb Z$, $G_2=\mathbb R$. Then the Lie algebras are
    $\mathfrak g_1=\mathfrak g_2=\mathbb R$ with zero bracket. Consider the identity map $\mathfrak g_1\to \mathfrak g_2$. Then the corresponding
    morphism of Lie groups, if it exists, should be given by $\theta\mapsto \theta$; on the other hand, it must also satisfy $f(\mathbb Z)=\{0\}$. Thus, this morphism of Lie algebras cannot be lifted to a morphism of Lie groups.
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    Let $G=T^2=\mathbb R^2/\mathbb Z^2$; then $\mathfrak g=\mathbb R^2$ with zero
    bracket. Choose an irrational number $\alpha$ and consider the following subspace
    \[
        \mathfrak h=\{(t,\alpha t)\mid t\in\mathbb R\}\subset \mathfrak g.
    \]
    Since $\mathfrak g$ is commutative, $\mathfrak h$ is automatically a
    one-dimensional Lie subalgebra. However, there is no Lie subgroup corresponding
    to $\mathfrak h$: the image of $\mathfrak h$ under the exponential map
    $\mathbb R^2\to \mathbb R^2/\mathbb Z^2$ is everywhere dense on the torus.
\end{example}

\begin{theorem}[Universal covering Lie group]
    If \(G\) is a connected Lie group, then its universal cover
    \[
        p:\widetilde G\to G
    \]
    admits a unique Lie group structure such that \(p\) is a Lie group homomorphism. Moreover:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item \(\widetilde G\) is connected and simply connected;
        \item \(\Lie(\widetilde G)\cong \Lie(G)\);
        \item \(\ker(p)\) is discrete and central in \(\widetilde G\);
        \item \(\ker(p)\cong \pi_1(G)\).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    The nontrivial part of this theorem is that we can put a Lie group structure on the universal cover $\tilde G$ of $G$ such that the covering map $p:\tilde G \to G$ is a homomorphism. Once we have this, all of the other statements follow from general covering space theory and the fact that $p$ is a local diffeomorphism. In particular remark that the kernel will be discrete because $p$ is locally injective.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Closed subgroup theorem, Lie's first theorem]
    If \(H\subset G\) is a closed subgroup of a Lie group, then \(H\) is an embedded Lie subgroup and $\Lie(H)\subset \Lie(G)$
    is a Lie subalgebra.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Lie's third theorem]
    Every finite-dimensional real Lie algebra is the Lie algebra of a Lie group.
    Equivalently, every such Lie algebra integrates to a connected simply connected Lie group.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
    We give an example of a Lie algebra homomorphism that does not integrate to a Lie group homomorphism. Consider the Lie algebras $\mathfrak{su}(2)$ and $\mathfrak{so}(3)$. The map $\mathrm{SU}(2)\to \mathrm{SO}(3)$ is the universal double cover, with kernel $\{\pm I\}$, so it induces an isomorphism of Lie algebras $\mathfrak{su}(2)\cong\mathfrak{so}(3)$.

    The inverse Lie algebra isomorphism $\mathfrak{so}(3)\to\mathfrak{su}(2)$ does not integrate to a Lie group homomorphism $S^3 = \mathrm{SO}(3)\to \mathrm{SU}(2) = \R\P^3$, because if it did, it would split the covering $\mathrm{SU}(2)\to\mathrm{SO}(3)$. But the covering is nontrivial, so no splitting exists.
\end{example}


\subsubsection{Fundamental Group and Center}

Let $G$ be a connected Lie group, and let
\[
    p:\widetilde G \to G
\]
be its universal covering space. A basic theorem says that $\widetilde G$ carries a unique Lie group structure for which $p$ is a Lie group homomorphism. Then $\pi_1(G) \cong \ker(p)$ is a discrete subgroup of $\widetilde G$. Moreover $\ker(p) \subset Z(\widetilde G)$ because for any $\gamma \in \ker(p)$, its orbit under conjugation by $\widetilde G$ is connected because $\widetilde G$ is connected, and it is contained in the discrete set $\ker(p)$, so it must be a single point. Hence $\gamma$ is central in $\widetilde G$. Moreover, if $G$ is semisimple adjoint, then by definition $G = \Ad(\widetilde G)$ where $\Ad : \widetilde G \to \mathrm{Aut}(\Lie(\widetilde G))$ is the adjoint representation. Then the kernel of the projection $\widetilde G \to G$ is precisely the kernel of the adjoint representation, which is the center $Z(\widetilde G)$. This is because if $c\in Z(\widetilde G)$, then $\Ad_c$ is the identity map on $\Lie(\widetilde G)$, and thus $c\in \ker(\Ad)$. The other direction is immediate. Thus the projection $\widetilde G \to G$ can be identified with the quotient map $\widetilde G \to \widetilde G/Z(\widetilde G)$, so $\pi_1(G)\cong Z(\widetilde G)$ in this case. This is because

In general one only has
\[
    \pi_1(G)\cong \ker(\widetilde G\to G)\subset Z(\widetilde G),
\]
and for $G$ connected semisimple, we get equality precisely when $G$ is adjoint.

\begin{example}
    For $G=S^1$
    the universal cover is $\widetilde G=\mathbb R$
    with covering map $t\mapsto e^{2\pi i t}$. Then $\pi_1(S^1)\cong \mathbb Z$ is only a proper discrete subgroup of the center $Z(\mathbb R)=\mathbb R$.
\end{example}

For compact connected semisimple Lie groups, one can compute the center and the possible fundamental groups very explicitly from the root datum. If $\widetilde G$ is the simply connected form with root lattice $Q$ and weight lattice $P$ after fixing a maximal torus $T$, then
\[
    Z(\widetilde G)\cong P/Q.
\]
This follows from the sequence of following observations. Note that $Z(\widetilde G)\subset T$ because any $z\in Z(\widetilde G)$ lies in some maximal torus $T'$ of $\widetilde G$, and all maximal tori are conjugate, so we can conjugate $z$ into $T$. But $z$ being central, $z$ also lies in $T$.

Then
$t\in Z(\widetilde G)$ if and only if $\alpha(t)=1\ \forall \alpha\in\Phi$. This is because $Z(\widetilde G)$ is the kernel of the adjoint representation, and the weights of the adjoint representation are precisely the roots. Hence
\[
    Z(\widetilde G)=\{t\in \Hom(P,\mathbb C^\times): t|_Q=1\}
    \cong \Hom(P/Q,\mathbb C^\times).
\]
where we are thinking of $t\in T$ as a homomorphism $P\to \mathbb C^\times$ by evaluating $t$ on the weights. The quotient $P/Q$ is finite because $P$ and $Q$ are full lattices of the same rank in the same real vector space $\mathfrak h_\mathbb R^*$, so $Q\subset P$ has finite index.


\begin{example}
    For type $A_{n-1}$:
    \[
        G_{\mathrm{sc}}=\SL_n(\mathbb C),
        \qquad
        G_{\mathrm{ad}}=\PGL_n(\mathbb C).
    \]
    The fact that $\SL_n(\mathbb C)$ is simply connected can be reduced to that of $\SU(n)$ by the polar decomposition, where the assertion follows from the long exact sequence of homotopy groups associated to the fibration $\SU(n)\to \SU(n+1)\to S^{2n+1}$, and induction on $n$ starting from $\SU(1)=\{1\}$.
    Being in the center of $\SL_n(\mathbb C)$ is equivalent to being a scalar matrix. The determinant condition then implies that the scalar must be an $n$-th root of unity. Thus the center of the simply connected form is
    \[
        Z(\SL_n(\mathbb C))=\mu_n,
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For type $B_n$:
    \[
        G_{\mathrm{sc}}=\Spin(2n+1),
        \qquad
        G_{\mathrm{ad}}=\SO(2n+1),
    \]
    We can show that $\pi_1(\SO(2n+1))\cong \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z$ by the long exact sequence of homotopy groups associated to the fibration $\SO(2n)\to \SO(2n+1)\to S^{2n}$. This sequence implies that $\pi_1(\SO(m)) \cong \pi_1(\SO(3))$ for all $m\geq 4$, and then the identification $\SO(3)\cong \R\P^3$ clearly gives $\pi_1(\SO(3))\cong \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z$.
    Therefore we see that
    \[
        Z(\Spin(2n+1))\cong \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z.
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For type $C_n$:
    \[
        G_{\mathrm{sc}}=\Sp_{2n}(\mathbb C),
        \qquad
        G_{\mathrm{ad}}=\P \Sp_{2n}(\mathbb C),
    \]
    and
    \[
        Z(\Sp_{2n}(\mathbb C))\cong \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z.
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For type $D_n$:
    \[
        G_{\mathrm{sc}}=\Spin(2n),
        \qquad
        G_{\mathrm{ad}}=\P \SO(2n),
    \]
    and
    \[
        Z(\Spin(2n))\cong
        \begin{cases}
            \mathbb Z/4\mathbb Z,                            & n \text{ odd},  \\
            \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z\times \mathbb Z/2\mathbb Z, & n \text{ even}.
        \end{cases}
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{exercise}
    Show that $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ is not a matrix group, i.e.\ it does not admit a faithful finite-dimensional representation.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    The group $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ is a $\Z$-cover of $\SL_2(\R)$. I claim that every finite-dimensional representation of $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ factors through $\SL_2(\R)$, in particular the generator of the kernel $\Z$ acts trivially. This claim will also imply that no finite sheeted cover of $\SL_2(\R)$ admits a faithful finite-dimensional representation.

    Let $V$ be a finite-dimensional representation of $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$. Differentiating the action gives a Lie algebra representation of $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\R)$ on $V$. We can complexify this to a representation of $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\C)$ on $V_\C$. This representation integrates to a representation of $\SL_2(\C)$ on $V_\C$ because $\SL_2(\C)$ is simply connected. Restricting this representation to $\SL_2(\R)$ gives a representation of $\SL_2(\R)$ on $V_\C$.

    Now write $V$ as a direct sum of irreducible $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\C)$-representations. Each of these is of the form $\Sym^n(\C^2)$ for some $n\geq 0$. Knowing this representation explicitly allows us to identify \begin{align*}
        \Sym^n(\C^2) & \cong \Sym^n(\R^2)\otimes_\R \C
    \end{align*} and clearly the action of $\SL_2(\R)$ on $\Sym^n(\C^2)$ factors through $\Sym^n(\R^2)$, so we see that we get a representation of $\SL_2(\R)$ on $V$. Now we have two representations of $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ on $V$: the original one, and the one obtained by pulling back the representation of $\SL_2(\R)$ we just constructed. They have the same differential, so by uniqueness they must be the same. This shows that the original representation of $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ factors through $\SL_2(\R)$, so the kernel $\Z$ acts trivially. Hence no finite-dimensional representation of $\widetilde{\SL_2(\R)}$ is faithful.
\end{solution}

\subsubsection{Compact Lie Groups, complexification, compact and split forms}

Recall the classification of compact Lie groups. \begin{itemize}
    \item Let $G$ be a compact Lie group. Let $G_0$ be the identity component of $G$. Then there is a short exact sequence of Lie groups
          \[1 \to G_0 \to G \to \pi_0(G) \to 1.\]
          The component group $\pi_0(G)$ is finite.
    \item Let $G$ be a compact, connected Lie group. Then $G$ has a universal cover $\tilde{G}$, and there is a short exact sequence
          \[1 \to \pi_1(G) \to \tilde{G} \to G \to 1.\]
          The fundamental group $\pi_1(G)$ is an abelian group. If $G$ is already compact semisimple then $\pi_1(G)$ is finite. If we have torus factors then $\pi_1(G)$ has free abelian parts.
          Moreover, $\tilde G$ is the product of a simply connected compact Lie group and a torus.
    \item Let $G$ be a compact, connected, simply connected Lie group. Then $G$ is the product of a simply connected simple Lie groups.
    \item Let $G$ be a compact, connected, simply connected simple Lie group. Then $G$ is determined up to isomorphism by its Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$. The simple compact Lie algebras are classified by the Dynkin diagrams $A_n$, $B_n$, $C_n$, $D_n$, $E_6$, $E_7$, $E_8$, $F_4$, and $G_2$.
\end{itemize}


\begin{definition}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a real Lie algebra. Its \textbf{complexification} is the complex
    Lie algebra
    \[
        \mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}=\mathfrak g\otimes_{\mathbb R}\mathbb C
        = \mathfrak g \oplus i\mathfrak g
    \]
    with the commutator defined by
    \[[x_1 + i y_1, x_2 + i y_2] = [x_1, x_2] - [y_1, y_2] + i([x_1, y_2] + [y_1, x_2])\]
    for $x_1, x_2, y_1, y_2 \in \mathfrak g$. In particular, note that this bracket is $\mathbb C$-linear in both arguments, so $\mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}$ is a complex Lie algebra. We say that $\mathfrak g$ is a \textbf{real form} of $\mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}$.
\end{definition}

\begin{example}
    If
    $\mathfrak g=\mathfrak{sl}(n,\mathbb R)$, then
    $\mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}=\mathfrak{sl}(n,\mathbb C)$.
    If $\mathfrak g=\mathfrak u(n)$ then $\mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}
        =\mathfrak{gl}(n,\mathbb C)$. This follows from the fact that any complex matrix can be uniquely written as a sum of skew-hermitian (i.e., from $\mathfrak u(n)$) and a hermitian (i.e.\ $i\mathfrak u(n)$), namely \begin{align*}
        X & = \frac{X - X^*}{2} + \frac{X + X^*}{2}
    \end{align*}
    The Lie bracket also matches, because both sides use the commutator bracket and the map $\mathfrak u(n) \oplus i\mathfrak u(n) \to \mathfrak{gl}(n,\mathbb C)$ is compatible with the Lie bracket.
\end{example}

These notions can be extended to Lie groups. For simplicity, we only consider
the case of connected groups.

\begin{definition}
    Let $G$ be a connected complex Lie group, $\mathfrak g=\Lie(G)$ and let
    $K\subset G$ be a real Lie subgroup in $G$ such that
    $\mathfrak k=\Lie(K)$ is a real form of $\mathfrak g$. Then $K$ is called
    a \emph{real form} of $G$.
\end{definition}

It is true that if $\mathfrak g=\Lie(G)$ is the Lie
algebra of a connected complex Lie group $G$, then every real form $\mathfrak k\subset \mathfrak g$ can be obtained from a real form $K\subset G$ of the Lie group.

Going in the opposite direction, from a real Lie group to a complex one, is more
subtle: there are real Lie groups that cannot be obtained as real forms of a
complex Lie group. It is still possible, however, to define a complexification
$G_{\mathbb C}$ for any real Lie group $G$; however, in general $G$ is not a
subgroup of $G_{\mathbb C}$. It is defined as the complex Lie group which is initial among those receive a map from $G$. If $G$ is connected and simply connected, one can consider $\mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}$ and the simply connected complex Lie group $G_{\mathbb C}$ integrating it. The hypothesis on $G$ guarentee that we can integrate the map $\mathfrak g \to \mathfrak g_{\mathbb C}$.

\begin{example}
    The group $G=\mathrm{SU}(n)$ is a compact real form of the complex group $\mathrm{SL}(n,\mathbb C)$.
\end{example}

Complexification defines an equivalence between connected compact real Lie groups and connected complex reductive Lie groups.

\begin{theorem}[Compact--reductive correspondence]
    There is an equivalence between the following categories:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Connected compact Lie groups (with smooth homomorphisms),
        \item Connected complex reductive algebraic groups (with morphisms of algebraic groups).
    \end{itemize}
    given as follows.

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item To a connected compact Lie group $K$, associate its complexification
              $G = K_{\mathbb C}$, which is a connected complex reductive algebraic group
              with Lie algebra
              \[
                  \Lie(G) \cong \Lie(K) \otimes_{\mathbb R} \mathbb C.
              \]

        \item To a connected complex reductive algebraic group $G$, associate a
              maximal compact subgroup $K \subset G$, which is a compact real form of $G$,
              satisfying
              \[
                  \Lie(K) \otimes_{\mathbb R} \mathbb C \cong \Lie(G).
              \]
    \end{enumerate}

    These constructions are inverse to each other up to isomorphism.
\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}
    A real form is \textbf{split} if it contains a Cartan subalgebra $\mathfrak h$ such that every root of $\mathfrak g_\mathbb C$ with respect to $\mathfrak h_\mathbb C$ takes real values on $\mathfrak h$, equivalently if it contains a maximal abelian subalgebra consisting of semisimple elements of dimension equal to the complex rank.
\end{definition}

For a complex semisimple Lie algebra, both compact and split real forms exist, and are unique up to isomorphism.

\begin{theorem}
    Every complex semisimple Lie algebra admits:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item a compact real form, unique up to inner automorphism;
        \item a split real form, unique up to isomorphism.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

The compact form is the one with the most negative-definite Killing form. The split form is the one with the largest possible noncompact torus.


\begin{example}
    For $\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb C)$:
    \[
        \text{compact real form: }\mathfrak{su}(n),
        \qquad
        \text{split real form: }\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R).
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For $\mathfrak{so}_{2n+1}(\mathbb C)$:
    \[
        \text{compact real form: }\mathfrak{so}(2n+1),
        \qquad
        \text{split real form: }\mathfrak{so}(n+1,n).
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For $\mathfrak{sp}_{2n}(\mathbb C)$:
    \[
        \text{compact real form: }\mathfrak{sp}(n),
        \qquad
        \text{split real form: }\mathfrak{sp}_{2n}(\mathbb R).
    \]
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    For $\mathfrak{so}_{2n}(\mathbb C)$:
    \[
        \text{compact real form: }\mathfrak{so}(2n),
        \qquad
        \text{split real form: }\mathfrak{so}(n,n).
    \]
\end{example}

One way to construct the compact real form is from a Chevalley basis. If
\[
    \mathfrak g_\mathbb C = \mathfrak h \oplus \bigoplus_{\alpha\in \Phi}\mathfrak g_\alpha
\]
with root vectors $X_\alpha$, then the compact real form is spanned over $\mathbb R$ by
\[
    iH_\alpha,\qquad X_\alpha-X_{-\alpha},\qquad i(X_\alpha+X_{-\alpha}).
\]
The split real form is spanned over $\mathbb R$ by the same Chevalley basis without inserting the factors of $i$.

\begin{example}
    For $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb C)$ we have the Chevalley basis $E,F,H$ with $[H,E]=2E$, $[H,F]=-2F$, and $[E,F]=H$. The compact real form $\mathfrak{su}(2)$ is spanned by the matrices \begin{align*}
        A = \begin{pmatrix}i&0\\0&-i\end{pmatrix},\qquad B=\begin{pmatrix}0&1\\-1&0\end{pmatrix},\qquad C= \begin{pmatrix}0&i\\i&0\end{pmatrix}
    \end{align*} subject to the relations $[A,B]=2C$, $[B,C]=2A$, and $[C,A]=2B$. Note that the Cartan subalgebra is $\mathbb RiH$ acts by imaginary eigenvalues on the root spaces, unlike the split form $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb R)$ \begin{align*}
        \mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb R) = \mathfrak k\oplus\mathfrak p = \mathbb R(E-F) \oplus \left(\mathbb RH\oplus \mathbb R(E+F)\right).
    \end{align*}
\end{example}

\begin{exercise}
    Show that $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb C)$ has exactly two nonisomorphic real forms: the compact form $\mathfrak{su}(2)$ and the split form $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb R)$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{proof}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a real form of $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb C)$. The adjoint representation $\mf g \to \so(\mf g, \kappa)$ is injective because $\mf g$ is semisimple, and $\so(\mf g, \kappa)$ is 3-dimensional, because after choosing a basis of $\mf g$ in which $\kappa$ is given by an invertible symmetric matrix $X$, the invariance condition becomes those $A\in \GL(\mf g)$ such that $A^TS + SA = 0$. That is, $SA$ is skew-symmetric, so $A$ is determined by the skew-symmetric matrix $SA$, which has 3 independent entries.

    This shows that $\mf g \to \mf{so}(\mf g, \kappa)$ is an isomorphism, so $\mf g$ is isomorphic to $\so(3)$ or $\so(2,1)$. The former is the compact form $\mathfrak{su}(2)$ and the latter is the split form $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb R)$. The first identification can be read off from the double cover $\SU(2)\to \SO(3)$ which is modeled on the unit quaternions acting on purely imaginary quaterions $\cong \R^3$ by conjugation, which clearly preserves orientation.

    The second identification follows from a direct calcuation of the signature of the Killing form on $\mathfrak{sl}_2(\mathbb R)$. On $\sl_n$ the Killing form is given by $\kappa(X,Y)=2n\tr(XY)$ which follows from direct calculation. Then \begin{align*}
        \kappa(H,H) & = 8,              \\
        \kappa(E,F) & = 4,              \\
        \kappa(E,E) & = \kappa(F,F) = 0
    \end{align*} and then the basis $E-F$, $H$, and $E+F$ is orthogonal with respect to $\kappa$ and has signature $(2,1)$.
\end{proof}

In general, real forms cannot be classified by the signature of the Killing form. The classification is given by Satake diagrams, which are Dynkin diagrams where some vertices are colored black and some are white, and some pairs of vertices are connected by an arrow.


\subsubsection{Cartan Involution and Maximal Compact Subgroups}

A central structural fact for real semisimple Lie groups is that they admit a canonical decomposition into a compact and a noncompact direction. This is encoded by a Cartan involution.
\begin{definition}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a real semisimple Lie algebra. A \textbf{Cartan involution} is a Lie algebra automorphism
    \[
        \theta : \mathfrak g \to \mathfrak g
    \]
    such that $\theta^2 = 1$ and the bilinear form
    \[
        B_\theta(X,Y) := -B(X,\theta Y)
    \]
    is positive definite, where $B$ is the Killing form of $\mathfrak g$.
\end{definition}

Since $\theta$ is an involution, $\mathfrak g$ decomposes into its $\pm 1$ eigenspaces:
\[
    \mathfrak g = \mathfrak k \oplus \mathfrak p,
    \qquad
    \mathfrak k = \{X \in \mathfrak g : \theta(X)=X\},
    \qquad
    \mathfrak p = \{X \in \mathfrak g : \theta(X)=-X\}.
\]
This is the \textbf{Cartan decomposition} of the Lie algebra. Since $\theta$ is a Lie algebra automorphism, the bracket satisfies
\[
    [\mathfrak k,\mathfrak k] \subseteq \mathfrak k,
    \qquad
    [\mathfrak k,\mathfrak p] \subseteq \mathfrak p,
    \qquad
    [\mathfrak p,\mathfrak p] \subseteq \mathfrak k.
\]

The positivity condition implies that the Killing form has opposite signs on the two summands. Indeed, if $X \in \mathfrak k$ then $\theta X = X$, so
\[
    B_\theta(X,X) = -B(X,X) < 0
\]
whereas if $Y \in \mathfrak p$ then $\theta Y = -Y$, so
\[
    B_\theta(Y,Y) = B(Y,Y) > 0
\]
Then the compact real form of $\mathfrak g$ is $\mathfrak k \oplus i\mathfrak p$, where the Killing form is clearly negative definite on both summands.

\begin{example}
    Let $\mf g \subset M_n(\mathbb R)$ be a real matrix Lie algebra closed under transpose, and every matrix is a sum of a symmetric and an antisymmetric matrix \begin{align*}
        X = \frac{X-X^*}{2} + \frac{X+X^*}{2}
    \end{align*} where $X^*$ is the conjugate transpose of $X$. Then letting $\mf k$ be the antisymmetric matrices and $\mf p$ be the symmetric matrices, we get a Cartan decomposition $\mf g = \mf k \oplus \mf p$ and the compact real form is \begin{align*}
        \mf u_0 = \mf k \oplus i\mf p
    \end{align*} sits inside skew-Hermitian matrices $\mf u(n)$. This is morally why $\mf u_0$ is the compact real form of $\mf g$.
\end{example}

At the group level, if $G$ is a connected semisimple Lie group with Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$, a Cartan involution on $\mathfrak g$ integrates to a Lie group automorphism
\[
    \Theta : G \to G
\]
with differential $d\Theta_e = \theta$. The fixed-point subgroup
\[
    K = G^\Theta = \{g \in G : \Theta(g)=g\}
\]
has Lie algebra $\mathfrak k$.
More precisely, the identity component of $G^\Theta$ has Lie algebra $\mathfrak k$, and one often writes $K$ for a maximal compact subgroup of $G$ with this Lie algebra.

The key theorem is that every connected semisimple Lie group has maximal compact subgroups, and they are controlled by Cartan involutions.

\begin{theorem}[Cartan decomposition and maximal compact subgroups]
    Let $G$ be a connected semisimple Lie group with finite center. Then there exists a Cartan involution $\Theta$ of $G$. If
    \[
        \mathfrak g = \mathfrak k \oplus \mathfrak p
    \]
    is the corresponding eigenspace decomposition of $d\Theta_e$, and $K = G^\Theta$, then:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $K$ is a maximal compact subgroup of $G$;
        \item every maximal compact subgroup of $G$ is conjugate to $K$;
        \item the map
              \[
                  K \times \mathfrak p \to G,
                  \qquad
                  (k,X) \mapsto k\exp(X)
              \]
              is a diffeomorphism.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

Thus one may think of $G$ as built from the compact group $K$ and the vector space $\mathfrak p$. The decomposition is not a decomposition by subgroups, since $\exp(\mathfrak p)$ is generally not a subgroup, but it gives a very efficient global parametrization of $G$.

\begin{exercise}[Past Quals]
    Let $G = \mathrm{SL}(n,\mathbb R)$. Show that $\Theta(g) = (g^T)^{-1}$
    is a Cartan involution of $G$, identify the corresponding decomposition $\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R) = \mathfrak k \oplus \mathfrak p$
    and deduce that $\mathrm{SO}(n)$ is a maximal compact subgroup of $\mathrm{SL}(n,\mathbb R)$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Compute the differential. Writing
    $g(t)=I+tX+O(t^2)$,
    we have
    \[\Theta(g(t)) = (g(t)^T)^{-1} = (I+tX^T+O(t^2))^{-1} = I-tX^T+O(t^2)\]
    Hence
    $d\Theta_e(X) = -X^T$ is the Cartan involution on $\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R)$. We compute the eigenspaces. \begin{align*}
        \mathfrak k & = \{X : -X^T = X\} = \{X : X^T = -X\} = \mathfrak{so}(n) \\
        \mathfrak p & = \{X : -X^T = -X\} = \{X : X^T = X\}
    \end{align*}
    Thus
    \[\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R) = \mathfrak{so}(n) \oplus \{X \in \mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R) : X^T = X\}\]

    Now we check the Cartan condition. The Killing form on $\mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb R)$ is a positive scalar multiple of
    $(X,Y) \mapsto \operatorname{tr}(XY)$.
    Therefore
    $-B(X,\theta X)$
    is a positive scalar multiple of
    $-\operatorname{tr}(X(-X^T)) = \operatorname{tr}(XX^T)$.
    But
    $\operatorname{tr}(XX^T) = \sum_{i,j} x_{ij}^2 > 0$
    for every nonzero matrix $X$. So $B_\theta(X,X) = -B(X,\theta X)$ is positive definite, and $\theta$ is a Cartan involution.

    The fixed points of $\Theta$ are the matrices satisfying
    $g^Tg = I$, thus $G^\Theta = \mathrm{SO}(n)$ is a maximal compact subgroup of $\mathrm{SL}(n,\mathbb R)$.
\end{solution}


\subsubsection{Borel, Parabolic, and Levi Subgroups}

Let $G$ be a connected reductive algebraic group over an algebraically closed field $k$.

\begin{definition}
    A \textbf{solvable group} is a group $G$ such that the derived series
    \[G^{(0)}=G,\qquad G^{(n+1)}=[G^{(n)},G^{(n)}]\]
    eventually becomes trivial, $[x,y]$ denoting the commutator $xy x^{-1} y^{-1}$. A \textbf{Borel subgroup} of $G$ is a maximal connected solvable closed subgroup of $G$.
\end{definition}

A fundamental fact is that Borel subgroups always exist, and they are all conjugate to each other. The basic example is $G=\GL_n$, where the subgroup $B$ of invertible upper triangular matrices is a Borel subgroup. More generally, any conjugate of $B$ is again a Borel subgroup, and in fact every Borel subgroup of $\GL_n$ is of this form. In $\GL_n$, the quotient $\GL_n/B$ identifies with the variety of complete flags
\[
    0\subset V_1\subset V_2\subset \cdots \subset V_n=k^n,
    \qquad \dim V_i=i.
\]


\begin{definition}
    A \textbf{parabolic subgroup} of $G$ is a closed subgroup $P\subset G$ containing a Borel subgroup.
\end{definition}


For $G=\GL_n$, the parabolic subgroups are exactly the stabilizers of partial flags. For example, the subgroup of block upper triangular matrices of type $(n_1,\dots,n_r)$ is parabolic, and it stabilizes a flag
\[
    0\subset V_{n_1}\subset V_{n_1+n_2}\subset \cdots \subset k^n.
\]

\begin{definition}
    If $P$ is a parabolic subgroup, its \textbf{unipotent radical} $R_u(P)$ is the largest connected normal unipotent subgroup of $P$. The quotient $P/R_u(P)$ is the maximal reductive quotient of $P$ and a Levi subgroup $L\subset P$ is a choice of splitting of
    \[1\to R_u(P)\to P\to P/R_u(P)\to 1\]
    The Levi subgroup $L$ is not canonical, but it is unique up to conjugation by an element of $R_u(P)$. The decomposition
    \[
        P=L\ltimes R_u(P).
    \]
    is called a \textbf{Levi decomposition} of $P$.
\end{definition}

For $G=\GL_n$, if $P$ is the subgroup of block upper triangular matrices of type $(n_1,\dots,n_r)$, then
\[
    R_u(P)=
    \left\{
    \text{block upper triangular matrices with identity diagonal blocks}
    \right\},
\]
and a Levi subgroup is
\[
    L\cong \GL_{n_1}\times \cdots \times \GL_{n_r},
\]

Fix a maximal torus $T\subset G$ and a Borel subgroup $B\supset T$. Then $B$ determines a set of positive roots $\Phi^+$ and a set of simple roots $\Delta$. With this choice, the standard parabolic subgroups containing $B$ are classified by subsets $\Theta\subset \Delta$. For each such $\Theta$, one gets a parabolic subgroup $P_\Theta$.

Its Levi subgroup $L_\Theta$ is the reductive subgroup generated by $T$ together with the root subgroups corresponding to the roots in the span of $\Theta$. Its unipotent radical is generated by the positive root subgroups not belonging to that Levi factor.
\subsubsection{Topology of Lie Groups}

\begin{exercise}
    Are there any Lie groups which are homeomorphic to $S^2$?
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Every Lie group is parallelizable, by picking a basis of the tangent space at the identity and translating it to every point. $S^2$ is not parallelizable because if it were, by definition its tangent bundle would be trivial, and integrating the Euler class of the tangent bundle would give zero, contradicting the fact that the Euler characteristic of $S^2$ is 2. Hence there are no Lie groups homeomorphic to $S^2$.
\end{solution}

\begin{example}
    $S^3$ is a Lie group, namely $\SU(2)$ the group of unit quaternions.
    A unit quaternion $q$ acts on the purely imaginary quaternions
    $V = \operatorname{Im}(\mathbb H)=\{bi+cj+dk\}\cong \mathbb R^3$
    by conjugation $v \mapsto qvq^{-1}$.
    The operator $T_q(v)=qvq^{-1}$ preserves lengths and angles, so it gives an element of $\SO(V) \cong \SO(3)$. Thus we get a homomorphism $\SU(2) \to \SO(3)$
    whose kernel is the center of $\SU(2)$, $\{\pm 1\}$, so it is the universal double cover.
\end{example}


\subsection{Lie Algebras}
\subsubsection{Solvable and Nilpotent Lie Algebras}

Let $V$ be finite dimensional over an algebraically closed field. Let $T$ be a linear operator on $V$. $T$ has generalized eigenspaces $V_\lambda$ for $\lambda \in k$ \begin{align*}
    V_\lambda & = \{v \in V : (T-\lambda)^n v = 0 \text{ for some } n\}
\end{align*}
\begin{theorem}
    $V$ is the direct sum of its generalized eigenspaces:
    \[V = \bigoplus_{\lambda \in k} V_\lambda.
    \] There are polynomials $p_\lambda(x) \in k[x]$ so that $p_\lambda(T)$ is the projection onto $V_\lambda$. The dimension of $V_\lambda$ is the multiplicity of $\lambda$ as a root of the characteristic polynomial of $T$. The restriction of $T$ to $V_\lambda$ has the form $\lambda + N$, where $N$ is nilpotent.
\end{theorem}
We say that a linear operator $T$ is \textbf{semisimple} if it is diagonalizable. We say that $T$ is \textbf{nilpotent} if all of its eigenvalues are zero, or equivalently if $T^n=0$ for some $n$. The theorem is obtained by letting $T_s = \sum_\lambda \lambda p_\lambda(T)$ and $T_n = T - T_s$.
\begin{theorem}
    [Jordan decomposition] Let $T$ be a linear operator on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$ over an algebraically closed field. Then there exist unique linear operators $T_s$ and $T_n$ on $V$ such that
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $T=T_s+T_n$;
        \item $T_s$ is semisimple (diagonalizable);
        \item $T_n$ is nilpotent;
        \item $T_s$ and $T_n$ commute.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $k$ be algebraically closed, and let $L$ be a linear operator on a finite-dimensional vector space $V$. Let $M$ be a linear operator on $V$. Then $M$ is killed by some power of $\ad L$ if and only if $M$ preserves each generalized eigenspace of $L$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}

    Let
    \[
        V=\bigoplus_{\lambda\in \sigma(L)}V_\lambda
    \]
    be the generalized eigenspace decomposition of \(L\). Then
    \[
        \End(V)=\bigoplus_{\lambda,\mu}\Hom(V_\lambda,V_\mu).
    \]
    If \(T\in \Hom(V_\lambda,V_\mu)\), then on \(V_\lambda\) and \(V_\mu\) we may write
    \[
        L|_{V_\lambda}=\lambda I+N_\lambda,
        \qquad
        L|_{V_\mu}=\mu I+N_\mu,
    \]
    where \(N_\lambda,N_\mu\) are nilpotent. Hence
    \[
        (\ad L)(T)=LT-TL
        =(\mu-\lambda)T+N_\mu T-TN_\lambda.
    \]
    The operator
    \[
        T\mapsto N_\mu T-TN_\lambda
    \]
    on \(\Hom(V_\lambda,V_\mu)\) is nilpotent. Therefore \(\ad L\) acts on
    \(\Hom(V_\lambda,V_\mu)\) with generalized eigenvalue \(\mu-\lambda\). Thus the generalized eigenspaces of \(\ad L\) on \(\End(V)\) are indexed by
    differences \(\mu-\lambda\). In particular, \(M\) is killed by some power of
    \(\ad L\) if and only if \(M\) has no component
    \[
        V_\lambda\to V_\mu
        \qquad (\lambda\neq \mu).
    \]
    Equivalently, \(M\) preserves each generalized eigenspace \(V_\lambda\) of \(L\) \qedhere.
\end{proof}

We say that a Lie algebra $\mf n$ is \textbf{nilpotent} if $\ad x$ is nilpotent for every $x\in \mf n$. We saw that for a given linear operator, we can write $V$ as a direct sum of generalized eigenspaces, and the operator acts on each generalized eigenspace as a scalar plus a nilpotent operator. If $\mf n$ is nilpotent and $V$ is a $\mf n$-module, then we can write $V$ as a direct sum of generalized eigenspaces for the action of $\mf n$.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a nilpotent Lie algebra over $k$, let $V$ be a
    finite-dimensional $k$-vector space, and let
    \[
        \rho:\mathfrak g\to \End(V)
    \]
    be a representation. Then the weight subspaces of $\rho$ corresponding to
    distinct weights are linearly independent. If $k$ is algebraically closed, and if $\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_r$ are all the
    distinct weights of $\rho$, then
    \[
        V=\bigoplus_{i=1}^r V_{\rho,\lambda_i}.
    \]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Idea of proof]
    The main point is to prove the decomposition when $k$ is algebraically closed.
    We argue by induction on $\dim V$.

    If every $\rho(X)$ has only one eigenvalue, say $\lambda(X)$, then
    \[
        V=V_{\rho,\lambda},
    \]
    and there is nothing to prove.

    Otherwise choose $X_0\in \mathfrak g$ such that $\rho(X_0)$ has at least two
    distinct eigenvalues $\mu_1,\dots,\mu_r$. Then the generalized eigenspace
    decomposition for the single operator $\rho(X_0)$ gives
    \[
        V=\bigoplus_{i=1}^r V_{\rho(X_0),\mu_i}.
    \]
    Each summand has smaller dimension than $V$.

    The key point is that these summands are actually $\mathfrak g$-subrepresentations.
    Indeed, for any $X\in \mathfrak g$, since $\mathfrak g$ is nilpotent, there is
    some $p\geq 1$ such that
    \[
        (\ad X_0)^p(X)=0.
    \]
    Applying the representation $\rho$, this gives
    \[
        (\ad \rho(X_0))^p(\rho(X))=0.
    \]
    By the linear algebra criterion for generalized eigenspaces, this implies that
    $\rho(X)$ preserves each generalized eigenspace $V_{\rho(X_0),\mu_i}$
    Therefore each $V_{\rho(X_0),\mu_i}$ is a $\mathfrak g$-stable subspace.

    Now apply the induction hypothesis to the restricted representation on each
    \[
        V_i:=V_{\rho(X_0),\mu_i}.
    \]
    Each $V_i$ decomposes as a direct sum of its weight spaces. Putting these
    decompositions together gives
    \[
        V=\bigoplus_{\lambda} V_{\rho,\lambda}.
    \]
    The linear independence of weight spaces corresponding to distinct weights
    follows from the same generalized-eigenspace separation: if
    \(\lambda\neq \mu\), then for some \(X\in \mathfrak g\) one has
    \(\lambda(X)\neq \mu(X)\), so the corresponding vectors lie in distinct
    generalized eigenspaces of \(\rho(X)\).
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
    Let \(G\) be a connected nilpotent analytic group with Lie algebra
    \(\mathfrak g\).

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item There is a polynomial map
              \[
                  P:\mathfrak g\times \mathfrak g\to \mathfrak g
              \]
              such that
              \[
                  \exp X\exp Y=\exp P(X,Y)
                  \qquad (X,Y\in \mathfrak g).
              \]

        \item Let \(\mathfrak z\subseteq \mathfrak g\) be the center, and define
              \[
                  D:=\{X\in \mathfrak z:\exp X=1\}.
              \]
              Then \(D\) is a discrete additive subgroup of \(\mathfrak g\), and the
              exponential map induces an analytic diffeomorphism
              \[
                  \mathfrak g/D \xrightarrow{\sim} G.
              \]
              In particular, \(\exp:\mathfrak g\to G\) is surjective, and \(\mathfrak g\)
              is a covering manifold of \(G\) with covering map \(\exp\).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    The Baker--Campbell--Hausdorff formula gives, for \(X,Y\) sufficiently close
    to \(0\),
    \[
        \exp X\exp Y=\exp\left(
        X+Y+\frac12[X,Y]
        +\frac1{12}[X,[X,Y]]
        -\frac1{12}[Y,[X,Y]]
        +\cdots
        \right).
    \]
    Since \(\mathfrak g\) is nilpotent, all sufficiently long iterated brackets
    vanish. Hence the Baker--Campbell--Hausdorff series terminates, so it defines
    a polynomial map
    \[
        P:\mathfrak g\times \mathfrak g\to \mathfrak g
    \]
    such that
    \[
        \exp X\exp Y=\exp P(X,Y)
    \]
    near \((0,0)\). Since both sides are analytic in \(X\) and \(Y\), the identity
    holds for all \(X,Y\in \mathfrak g\).

    We next prove that \(\exp\) is surjective. Let
    \[
        G'=\exp(\mathfrak g)\subseteq G.
    \]
    The BCH formula implies that \(G'\) is closed under multiplication:
    \[
        \exp X\exp Y=\exp P(X,Y)\in G'.
    \]
    It is also closed under inverses, since
    \[
        (\exp X)^{-1}=\exp(-X).
    \]
    Thus \(G'\) is a subgroup of \(G\). Since \(\exp\) is a local diffeomorphism
    near \(0\in \mathfrak g\), the subset \(G'\) contains an open neighborhood of
    the identity in \(G\). Therefore \(G'\) is an open subgroup. But \(G\) is
    connected, so every open subgroup is also closed and must equal \(G\). Hence $\exp$ is surjective.
    Now consider the kernel of the exponential map,
    \[
        \ker(\exp)=\{X\in \mathfrak g:\exp X=1\}.
    \]
    Since \(\exp\) is locally a diffeomorphism near \(0\), this kernel is discrete.
    Moreover, if \(\exp X=1\), then for every \(Y\in\mathfrak g\),
    \[
        \exp X\exp Y\exp(-X)=\exp Y.
    \]
    Infinitesimally, conjugation by \(\exp X\) acts as $e^{\ad X}$. Thus \(e^{\ad X}Y=Y\) for every \(Y\in\mathfrak g\). Since \(\ad X\) is
    nilpotent, the equality \(e^{\ad X}=I\) implies
    \[
        \ad X=0.
    \]
    Therefore \(X\in \mathfrak z\), and hence
    \[
        \ker(\exp)=D=\{X\in \mathfrak z:\exp X=1\}.
    \]

    Thus \(\exp:\mathfrak g\to G\) is a surjective local diffeomorphism with
    discrete kernel \(D\). Therefore it descends to an analytic diffeomorphism $\mathfrak g/D \xrightarrow{\sim} G$.
\end{proof}
If $G$ is simply connected, then $\mf g \to G$ being the universal cover implies that $D = 0$ and hence $\exp : \mf g \to G$ is a global diffeomorphism. Moreover, if $H \subset G$ is an analytic subgroup with Lie algebra $\mf h \subset \mf g$, then $H$ is closed, simply connected, and $\exp : \mf h \to H$ is a global diffeomorphism.

\begin{theorem}
    Let \(V\) be a finite-dimensional vector space over \(\mathbb R\) or
    \(\mathbb C\), and set \(p=\dim V\). Let $\bar{\mathfrak g}\subseteq \mathfrak{gl}(V)$
    be a Lie subalgebra consisting entirely of nilpotent endomorphisms of \(V\),
    and let \(\bar G\subseteq GL(V)\) be the analytic subgroup with Lie algebra
    \(\bar{\mathfrak g}\). Then \(\bar{\mathfrak g}\) is nilpotent, and
    \(\bar G\) is a simply connected unipotent algebraic subgroup of \(GL(V)\).

    Moreover, for \(X\in \bar{\mathfrak g}\),
    \[
        \exp X
        =
        I+\sum_{1\leq s<p}\frac{X^s}{s!},
    \]
    and for \(x\in \bar G\),
    \[
        \log x
        =
        \sum_{1\leq s<p}(-1)^{s-1}\frac{(x-I)^s}{s}.
    \]
    In particular, \(\exp:\bar{\mathfrak g}\to \bar G\) and
    \(\log:\bar G\to \bar{\mathfrak g}\) are mutually inverse polynomial maps.
\end{theorem}

Two of the basic classes of Lie algebras are \textbf{solvable} and \textbf{nilpotent} Lie algebras. They may be viewed as the Lie-theoretic analogues of upper triangular and strictly upper triangular matrix algebras.

Given a Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$, its \textbf{derived series} is defined by
\[
    \mathfrak g^{(0)}=\mathfrak g,\qquad
    \mathfrak g^{(1)}=[\mathfrak g,\mathfrak g],\qquad
    \mathfrak g^{(n+1)}=[\mathfrak g^{(n)},\mathfrak g^{(n)}].
\]
We say that $\mathfrak g$ is \textbf{solvable} if $\mathfrak g^{(n)}=0$ for some $n$. Equivalently, $\mathfrak g$ is solvable if and only its derived subalgebra $[\mathfrak g,\mathfrak g]$ is nilpotent.

The \textbf{lower central series} is
\[
    \mathfrak g_1=\mathfrak g,\qquad
    \mathfrak g_{n+1}=[\mathfrak g,\mathfrak g_n].
\]
We say that $\mathfrak g$ is \textbf{nilpotent} if $\mathfrak g_n=0$ for some $n$.

\begin{theorem}
    [Engel's theorem] If every $\operatorname{ad}(x)$ is nilpotent, then $\mathfrak g$ itself is nilpotent.
\end{theorem}

Every nilpotent Lie algebra is solvable, but the converse is false. For example, the Lie algebra of strictly upper triangular matrices in $\mathfrak{gl}_n(\mathbb C)$ is nilpotent, while the Lie algebra of all upper triangular matrices is solvable but not nilpotent.

Solvable and nilpotent Lie algebras are the ``building blocks'' that appear inside general Lie algebras. Every finite-dimensional Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ has a largest solvable ideal, called the \textbf{radical} $\operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)$, and a largest nilpotent ideal, called the \textbf{nilradical}.


\begin{theorem}[Lie's theorem]
    If $\mathfrak g$ is a solvable Lie algebra over $\mathbb C$ and $V$ is a nonzero finite-dimensional $\mathfrak g$-module, then there exists a basis of $V$ in which all operators from $\mathfrak g$ are upper triangular.
\end{theorem} Equivalently, $V$ contains a common eigenvector for $\mathfrak g$, namely pick a basis $e_1,\dots,e_n$ of $V$ such that the operators from $\mathfrak g$ are upper triangular, and then $e_1$ is a common eigenvector.

\begin{corollary}
    Every irreducible representation of a solvable Lie algebra is one-dimensional.
\end{corollary}



Every finite-dimensional Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ over $\mathbb{C}$ admits a decomposition
\[
    \mathfrak g \cong \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g) \rtimes \mathfrak s,
\]
where $\operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)$ is the maximal solvable ideal and $\mathfrak s$ is a semisimple subalgebra (a Levi subalgebra). The radical is uniquely determined, while the Levi subalgebra is unique up to conjugation. In particular, every Lie algebra is built from a semisimple Lie algebra by extension by a solvable one.
\begin{example}
    \[
        \mathfrak{gl}_n = \mathfrak{sl}_n \oplus \mathbb{C}
    \]
    where $\mathfrak{sl}_n$ is the semisimple part and $\mathbb{C}$ is the center, which is solvable.
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    Upper triangular matrices $\mathfrak{b} \subset \mathfrak{gl}_n$ have no semisimple part, so $\operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak{b}) = \mathfrak{b}$ and $\mathfrak s = 0$.
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Semisimple and Reductive Lie Algebras}
\begin{definition}
    A finite-dimensional Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ over $\mathbb C$ is called \textbf{semisimple} if it has no nonzero solvable ideals. Equivalently, its radical $\rad(\mathfrak g)$ is zero.
\end{definition}
The fundamental examples are the classical matrix Lie algebras
\[
    \mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb C),\qquad
    \mathfrak{so}_n(\mathbb C),\qquad
    \mathfrak{sp}_{2n}(\mathbb C),
\]
together with the five exceptional Lie algebras of types $G_2,F_4,E_6,E_7,E_8$.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $V$ be an irreducible representation of $\mathfrak g$. Then any $h \in \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)$ acts in $V$ by scalar operators:
    \[
        \rho(h) = \lambda(h)\,\mathrm{id}.
    \]
    Also, any $h \in [\mathfrak g, \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)]$ acts by zero.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    By Lie's theorem, there is a common eigenvector in $V$ for all $h \in \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)$:
    \[
        \rho(h)v = \lambda(h)v
    \]
    for some $\lambda : \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g) \to \mathbb{C}$. Define
    \[
        V_\lambda = \{ w \in V \mid \rho(h)w = \lambda(h)w \text{ for all } h \in \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g) \}.
    \]
    Then the same argument as in the proof of Proposition 6.15 shows that for any $x \in \mathfrak g$, one has $\rho(x)(V_\lambda) \subset V_\lambda$. Thus, $V_\lambda$ is a subrepresentation; since it is non-zero and $V$ is irreducible, we must have $V = V_\lambda$, which proves the first statement of the theorem. The second statement immediately follows from the first one.
\end{proof}

From the point of view of representation theory, having non-zero elements which act by zero in any irreducible representation significantly complicates the theory. Thus, it is natural to consider a class of algebras for which
\[
    [\mathfrak g, \operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g)] = 0.
\]
Let $\mf z = \{x\in \mathfrak g : [x,y]=0 \text{ for all } y\in \mathfrak g\}$ be the center of $\mathfrak g$.
\begin{definition}
    A Lie algebra is called \textbf{reductive} if $\operatorname{rad}(\mathfrak g) = \mathfrak z(\mathfrak g)$. i.e.\ if $\mathfrak g / \mathfrak z(\mathfrak g)$ is semisimple.
\end{definition}
Any semisimple Lie algebra is reductive. The model for a reductive Lie algebra which is not semisimple is $\mathfrak{gl}_n(\mathbb C) = \mathfrak{sl}_n(\mathbb C) \oplus \mathbb C$, where the center $\mathbb C$ is the radical. In general, a reductive Lie algebra is a direct sum of a semisimple Lie algebra and an abelian Lie algebra.

\begin{proposition}
    The following are equivalent for a finite-dimensional Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ over $\mathbb{C}$:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\mathfrak g$ is reductive;
        \item every finite-dimensional representation of $\mathfrak g$ is completely reducible;
        \item the adjoint representation of $\mathfrak g$ is completely reducible.
        \item $\mf g$ = $\mathfrak z \oplus \mathfrak g_{ss}$, where $\mathfrak z$ is abelian and $\mathfrak g_{ss}$ is semisimple.
    \end{enumerate}

\end{proposition}

\subsubsection{Killing Form and Complete Reducibility}
Recall that the Killing form of a Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ is the symmetric bilinear form defined by
\[\kappa(x,y) = \operatorname{tr}(\operatorname{ad}(x)\operatorname{ad}(y)).\]
The Killing form is invariant under the adjoint action, meaning that
\[\kappa([x,y],z) = \kappa(x,[y,z]) \qquad\text{for all }x,y,z \in \mathfrak g.\]
This follows from the cyclicity of the trace $\operatorname{tr}(ABC) = \operatorname{tr}(BCA)$.
\begin{theorem}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a finite-dimensional Lie algebra over $\mathbb{C}$. Then $\mf g$ is solvable if and only if $\kappa(\mathfrak g, [\mathfrak g, \mathfrak g]) = 0$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
    A finite-dimensional Lie algebra is semisimple if and only if its Killing form is nondegenerate.\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Suppose $\rad g \neq 0$. Choose the last nonzero term in the derived series of $\rad g$, say $\mf a$ which has the property that $[\mf a, \mf a] = 0$, i.e. $\mf a$ is abelian. Also $[\mf g, \mf a] \subset \mf a$ because each term in the derived series is an ideal (this is quickly verified by induction). Now we will show that $\kappa(\mf g, \mf a) = 0$. Observe that the operator $\ad x \ad y$ where $x\in \mf a$ and $y\in \mf g$ carries $\mf g$ to $\mf a$. Therefore its trace on $\mf g$ is the same as that of its trace to the restriction to $\mf a$. This is elementary linear algebra, because in a basis adapted to $\mf a \subset \mf g$, the matrix is of block form \begin{align*}
        \begin{pmatrix}
            * & * \\
            0 & 0
        \end{pmatrix}
    \end{align*} But this operator is zero when restricted to $\mf a$, because $\ad x\circ \ad y = 0$ for $x,y\in \mf a$ since $\mf a$ is abelian. Thus the Killing form is singular if $\rad g \neq 0$. Conversely, suppose that $\kappa$ is degenerate. Then define its radical \begin{align*}
        \mf m = \{x\in \mf g : \kappa(x,y) = 0 \text{ for all } y\in \mf g\}.
    \end{align*} Then $\mf m$ is an ideal of $\mf g$ because $\kappa([x,z],y) = \kappa(x,[z,y]) = 0$. Because $\mf m$ is an ideal, $\ad X \circ \ad Y$ carries $\mf g$ to $\mf m$ for any $X,Y\in \mf m$. Therefore \begin{align*}
        \operatorname{tr}_{\mathfrak g}(\operatorname{ad}X\operatorname{ad}X')
        =
        \operatorname{tr}_{\mathfrak m}(\operatorname{ad}_{\mathfrak m}X\operatorname{ad}_{\mathfrak m}X')
    \end{align*} But the left hand side is zero by definition of $\mf m$, so the right hand side is also zero. Thus the Killing form of $\mf m$ is degenerate, so $\mf m$ is solvable. Thus $\rad g \neq 0$ and $\mf g$ is not semisimple. \qedhere
\end{proof}

If $\mf g$ is semisimple, then $\mf z(\mf g) = 0$ because the center is an abelian ideal. Thus, the adjoint representation of a semisimple Lie algebra is faithful. Moreover, $\tr(\ad x) = 0$ for all $x \in \mf g$ because $x$ can be written as a commutator and $\tr([A,B]) = 0$ for all $A,B$.

\begin{exercise}[Past Quals]
    Let $G$ be a connected Lie group whose Killing form $B$ on $\mf g$ is negative definite. Show that $G$ is compact.
\end{exercise}

The general fact to know here is that a real Lie algebra with negative definite Killing form is the Lie algebra of a compact simply connected Lie group.
\begin{solution}
    The negative of $B$ defines an inner product on $\mf g$ which is positive definite and $\Ad$-invariant. Hence there is a map $G \to O(\mf g)$ given by the adjoint representation. This map has kernel $Z(G)$, the center of $G$. The image of $G$ is a subgroup of $O(\mf g)$, which is compact. Therefore, we must see that $Z(G)$ is finite and the image is closed.

    Observe that the Lie algebra center is zero. Indeed, if $X\in Z(\mathfrak g)$, then \(\ad_X=0\), so for every $Y\in\mathfrak g$,
    \[
        B(X,Y)=\operatorname{tr}(\ad_X\ad_Y)=0.
    \]
    Since B is nondegenerate, this implies $X=0$. Hence $Z(\mathfrak g) = 0$ and so we see that $Z(G)$ is discrete. Also we immediately see that $\mf g$ is semisimple since Cartan's criterion says that the Killing form is nondegenerate if and only if $\mf g$ is semisimple if and only if the center of $\mf g$ is zero.

    Next we show that the image is closed.
    Let $H := \overline{\Ad(G)} \subset O(\mathfrak{g})$.
    Since $O(\mathfrak{g})$ is compact, $H$ is compact.

    Now compare Lie algebras. The Lie algebra of $\Ad(G)$ is $\ad(\mathfrak{g}) \subset \mathfrak{so}(\mathfrak{g})$
    because $d(\Ad)_e = \ad$. Note the funny fact that the Lie algebra of $O(n)$ is $\mathfrak{so}(n)$, 	because $O(n)$ has two connected components, distinguished by $\det=\pm 1$. $SO(n)$ is the identity component of $O(n)$.

    The closure $H$ has the same Lie algebra, so
    \[
        \Lie(H) = \ad(\mathfrak{g}) = \Lie(\Ad(G))
    \]
    Now $\Ad(G)$ is connected, because $G$ is connected. In a Lie group, a connected Lie subgroup is determined by its Lie algebra. Since $\Ad(G)$ and $H$ are connected Lie subgroups of $O(\mathfrak{g})$ with the same Lie algebra, they must coincide so $\Ad(G) = H$ is closed. Hence $G/Z(G) \cong \Ad(G)$ is compact. We now have a covering map $G \to G/Z(G) = \Ad(G)$ with compact base and discrete fiber. We need to know that the fiber is in fact finite. We can prove this by reasoning about $\pi_1$.

    $\pi_1(G)$ is abelian so $\pi_1(G)\cong H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})$ by the Hurewicz map. Thus it is enough to show $H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})$ is finite. On a compact connected Lie group, every de Rham cohomology class has a left-invariant representative. So it suffices to compute closed left-invariant $1$-forms.

    A left-invariant $1$-form is the same as an element $\alpha\in \mathfrak{g}^*$. For left-invariant vector fields $X,Y$,
    $$d\alpha(X,Y)=-\alpha([X,Y]).$$
    Thus $\alpha$ is closed iff
    $$\alpha([X,Y])=0 \qquad \forall X,Y\in\mathfrak{g},$$
    that is, iff $\alpha$ vanishes on $[\mathfrak{g},\mathfrak{g}]$. But $\mathfrak{g}$ is semisimple, so $[\mathfrak{g},\mathfrak{g}]=\mathfrak{g}$.
    Hence the only closed left-invariant $1$-form is $0$. Therefore $H^1(G,\mathbb{R})=0$.

    Since $G$ is a compact manifold, $H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})$ is a finitely generated abelian group. Also
    $$H^1(G,\mathbb{R})\cong \operatorname{Hom}(H_1(G,\mathbb{Z}),\mathbb{R}).$$
    Because $H^1(G,\mathbb{R})=0$, there are no nonzero homomorphisms
    $$H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})\to \mathbb{R}.$$
    So $H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})$ has no free $\mathbb{Z}$-part. Being finitely generated abelian, it must therefore be finite. Thus $\pi_1(G)\cong H_1(G,\mathbb{Z})$ is finite.
\end{solution}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a semisimple Lie algebra. If $\mf h$ is an ideal of $\mf g$, then $\mf h^\perp$ is also an ideal, $\mathfrak g = \mathfrak h \oplus \mathfrak h^\perp$, and both $\mathfrak h$ and $\mathfrak h^\perp$ are semisimple. In particular, $\mathfrak g$ is a direct sum of simple Lie algebras.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    We have the crucial fact that if $\mf a$ is an ideal of $\mf g$, then $\kappa_{\mf a}(x,y) = \kappa_{\mf g}(x,y)$ for $x,y \in \mf a$. Applying this fact to the ideal $\mf h \cap \mf h^\perp$, we see that $\mf h \cap \mf h^\perp$ is a solvable ideal of $\mf g$, so it must be zero.
\end{proof}

This theorem implies that for semisimple $\mf g$, we have $\mf g = [\mf g, \mf g]$ because otherwise, $\mf g / [\mf g, \mf g]$ would be nonzero, abelian, and semisimple, which is impossible because every abelian Lie algebra is solvable.

\begin{theorem}
    If $\mf g$ is semisimple, then every derivation $D$ of $\mf g$ is inner, i.e. there is some $x \in \mf g$ such that $D = \ad x$. Any
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Let $D$ be a derivation of $\mf g$. Let $\lambda(Y) = \tr(D \circ \ad Y)$ be a linear functional, which by the nondegeneracy of $\kappa$ can be written as $\lambda(Y) = \kappa(X,Y)$ for some $X \in \mf g$. We will show that $D = \ad X$. Put $D' = D - \ad X$. Then $D'$ is also a derivation with the property that $\tr(D' \circ \ad Y) = 0$ for all $Y$. We will show that $D' = 0$, which will complete the proof.

    Fix $Z\in \mf g$. We have the identity since $D'$ is a derivation, \begin{align*}
        [D', \ad Z](W) & = D'([Z,W]) - [Z, D'(W)]           \\
                       & = ([D'Z,W] + [Z,D'W]) - [Z, D'(W)] \\
                       & = [D'Z,W] = \ad(D'Z)(W)
    \end{align*} Now we compute $\operatorname{tr}\left(\operatorname{ad}Y\,[D',\operatorname{ad}Z]\right) = \operatorname{tr}(\operatorname{ad}Y\operatorname{ad}(D'Z)) = \kappa(Y,D'Z)$. But at the same time \begin{align*}
        \tr(\ad y \ad(D'Z)) = \operatorname{tr}\left(\operatorname{ad}Y\,[D',\operatorname{ad}Z]\right)
         & =
        \operatorname{tr}\left(\operatorname{ad}Y D'\operatorname{ad}Z-\operatorname{ad}Y\operatorname{ad}Z D'\right) \\
         & = \tr(D' \ad Z  \ad Y) - \tr(D'  \ad Y  \ad Z)                                                             \\
         & = \tr(D' \ad[Z,Y])                                                                                         \\
         & = 0
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}
The left hand side is equal to $\kappa(Y,D'Z)$, so $\kappa(Y,D'Z) = 0$ for all $Y$, which implies $D'Z = 0$. Since $Z$ was arbitrary, we have $D' = 0$ and hence $D = \ad X$ is inner.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $\Ad: G\to \gl(\mathfrak g)$ be the adjoint representation of a connected semisimple Lie group $G$. Then $\Ad(G)$ is a closed subgroup of $\gl(\mathfrak g)$, and coincides with the connected component of the identity in $\Aut(\mathfrak g)$. Moreover $\Aut^0(\mathfrak g) = \Ad(G) \subset \mf{sl}(\mathfrak g)$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    An element $A \in \GL(\mf g)$ is an automorphism of the Lie algebra $\mf g$ precisely if $[Ax,Ay] = A[x,y]$ for all $x,y \in \mf g$. Picking a basis for $\mf g$ expresses this condition in terms of polynomial equations in the entries of $A$.  The Lie algebra of $\Aut(\mf g)$ are those infinitesimal automorphisms of $\mf g$. Pick a curve $A(t) \in \Aut(\mf g)$ with $A(0) = I$. Writing $A(t) = I + tX + O(t^2)$, expanding the automorphism condition and comparing the $t$-linear terms gives $[Xx,y] + [x,Xy] = X[x,y]$ for all $x,y \in \mf g$. This is precisely the condition that $X$ is a derivation of $\mf g$. If $\mf g$ is semisimple, then every derivation is inner, so we see that \begin{align*}
        \Lie(\Aut(\mf g)) = \Der(\mf g) = \ad(\mf g)
