    If $\mf g = \mf{sl}_n(\C)$, then the nilpotent orbits are classified by partitions of $n$, because the eigenvalues of a nilpotent matrix are all zero, so the only invariant is the sizes of the Jordan blocks. In this case, it is clear that there are only finitely many nilpotent orbits, and that they are cones. However, for an arbitrary semisimple Lie algebra, the classification of nilpotent orbits is more complicated, and it is not a priori clear that there are only finitely many of them, or that they are cones.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}
    If $x\in \mathfrak g$ is nilpotent, then by Jacobson-Morozov there exists an $\mathfrak{sl}_2$-triple $(x,h,y)$. Then
    \[
        \Ad(\exp(th))(x)=e^{2t}x.
    \]
    So every nonzero scalar multiple of $x$ lies in the same adjoint orbit. That is exactly the statement that the nilpotent orbit is a cone under scaling.
\end{remark}
\begin{theorem}[Jacobson--Morozov]
    Let $\mathfrak g$ be a finite-dimensional semisimple Lie algebra over a field of characteristic 0. If $e\in \mathfrak g$ is nilpotent, then there exist elements $h,f\in \mathfrak g$ such that
    $[h,e]=2e, [h,f]=-2f, [e,f]=h$.
\end{theorem}


\section{Algebraic Topology}
\subsection{Fundamental groups}
\begin{theorem}[Van Kampen's theorem]
    Let $X$ be the union of path connected open subsets $X_\alpha$ such that $X_\alpha \cap X_\beta$ is path connected for all $\alpha,\beta$. Then there is a surjection \begin{align*}
        \ast_\alpha \pi_1(X_\alpha) \twoheadrightarrow \pi_1(X)
    \end{align*} whose kernel is the normal subgroup generated by elements of the form $i_{\alpha\beta}(\gamma)i_{\beta\alpha}(\gamma)^{-1}$, where $\gamma \in \pi_1(X_\alpha \cap X_\beta)$ and $i_{\alpha\beta} : \pi_1(X_\alpha \cap X_\beta) \to \pi_1(X_\alpha)$ is the map induced by inclusion.
\end{theorem}
A priori, loops in $X$ can move through different $X_\alpha$, but if the cover is nice enough, we can subdivide the loop into pieces that lie in a single $X_\alpha$. This gives a surjection from the free product of the $\pi_1(X_\alpha)$ to $\pi_1(X)$. However, if we have a loop that lies in $X_\alpha \cap X_\beta$, then it can be viewed as a loop in $X_\alpha$ or in $X_\beta$, and these two views must be identified in $\pi_1(X)$. This gives the relations that generate the kernel of the surjection.

Note that the subgroup generated by the elements $i_{\alpha\beta}(\gamma)i_{\beta\alpha}(\gamma)^{-1}$ is not necessarily normal, so we must take the normal closure to get the kernel.

\begin{corollary}
    If $X$ is obtained from $Y$ by attaching a $2$-cell along a loop $\gamma:S^1 \to Y$, then $\pi_1(X)$ is the quotient of $\pi_1(Y)$ by the normal subgroup generated by $[\gamma]$.
\end{corollary}
The same argument shows that attaching $n$-cells for $n \ge 3$ does not change the fundamental group.

\begin{proof}
    Take the open cover $A = Y \cup_\gamma \set{z\in D^2 \vert |z| > 1/3}$ and $B = \set{z\in D^2 \vert |z| < 2/3}$. Then $A$ is homotopy equivalent to $Y$, $B$ is contractible, and $A \cap B$ is homotopy equivalent to $S^1$. We get \begin{align*}
        \pi_1(X) & \cong \pi_1(Y)/\Z
    \end{align*} where the generator of $\pi_1(S^1)$ maps to $[\gamma]$ in $\pi_1(Y)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
    The genus $g$ oriented surface $M_g$ has a CW structure with one $0$-cell, $2g$ $1$-cells, and one $2$-cell. The attaching map of the $2$-cell is given by the loop $\gamma = [a_1,b_1]\cdots [a_g,b_g]$, where $a_i,b_i$ are the loops corresponding to the $1$-cells. In particular, $\pi_1(M_g)$ is generated by $a_i,b_i$ with a single relation $\prod_{i=1}^g [a_i,b_i]=1$.
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    If we attach a 2 cell to the wedge sum of g circles by the word $a_1^2\cdots a_g^2$, we obtain a nonorientable surface $N_g$. For $g=1$, we get $\mathbb{RP}^2$, and for $g=2$ we get the Klein bottle, which can also be presented by the work $abab^{-1}$.
\end{example}

\subsection{Covering spaces}
A covering space of a topological space $X$ is a topological space $\tilde X$ together with a continuous surjective map $p : \tilde X \to X$ such that for every $x \in X$, there exists an open neighborhood $U$ of $x$ such that $p^{-1}(U)$ is a disjoint union of open sets in $\tilde X$, each of which is homeomorphic to $U$ via $p$.

\begin{proposition}
    Covering spaces have the homotopy lifting property, that is if $p : \tilde X \to X$ is a covering space, and $f : Y \times [0,1] \to X$ is a homotopy, and $\tilde f_0 : Y \to \tilde X$ is a lift of $f_0 = f(-,0)$, then there exists a unique homotopy $\tilde f : Y \times [0,1] \to \tilde X$ such that $p \circ \tilde f = f$ and $\tilde f(-,0) = \tilde f_0$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{theorem}
    The map $p_* : \pi_1(\tilde X) \to \pi_1(X)$ induced by a covering space $p : \tilde X \to X$ is an injective homomorphism. The image of $p_*$ is a subgroup of $\pi_1(X)$ consisting of those loops in $X$ that lift to loops in $\tilde X$. The index of $p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X))$ in $\pi_1(X)$ is equal to the number of sheets of the covering space.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $p: (\tilde X, x_0) \to (X, x)$ be a covering space. Let $(Y,y)$ be path connected and locally path connected. Then $f: (Y,y) \to (X,x)$ lifts to a map $\tilde f : (Y,y) \to (\tilde X,x_0)$ if and only if $f_*(\pi_1(Y,y)) \subset p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,x_0))$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
    Let $p: (\tilde X, x_0) \to (X, x)$ be a covering space. Then if $Y$ is connected and two lifts of a map $f: (Y,y) \to (X,x)$ agree at a point, they are equal.
\end{theorem}

Having established the basic theory of covering spaces, we can now classify them. Let $X$ be a path connected, locally path connected, and semilocally simply connected space. Then there is a universal cover $\tilde X$ of $X$, characterized by the property that $\tilde X$ is simply connected. Every other covering space of $X$ is a quotient of $\tilde X$.

The universal cover $\tilde X$ is modeled on the set of homotopy classes of paths in $X$ starting at $x$. The map $p : \tilde X \to X$ sends a path to its endpoint. A consequence of the lifting criterion is that the universal cover $\tilde X$ is a covering space of any other path connected covering space of $X$.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $X$ be path-connected, locally path-connected, and semilocally simply connected.
    Then there is a bijection between the set of basepoint-preserving isomorphism classes of path-connected covering spaces
    \[
        p:(\widetilde X,\widetilde x_0)\to (X,x_0)
    \]
    and the set of subgroups of $\pi_1(X,x_0)$, obtained by associating to such a covering space the subgroup
    \[
        p_*\bigl(\pi_1(\widetilde X,\widetilde x_0)\bigr)
        \subseteq \pi_1(X,x_0).
    \]
    If basepoints are ignored, this correspondence gives a bijection between isomorphism classes of path-connected covering spaces
    \[
        p:\widetilde X\to X
    \]
    and conjugacy classes of subgroups of $\pi_1(X,x_0)$.
\end{theorem}

Covering spaces can be represented by permutations. Let $[\gamma]\in\pi_1(X,x_0)$ be a loop based at $x_0$. Then for each point $\tilde x_0\in p^{-1}(x_0)$, there is a unique lift $\tilde \gamma$ of $\gamma$ starting at $\tilde x_0$. The endpoint of $\tilde \gamma$ is another point in $p^{-1}(x_0)$, and this gives a permutation of the fiber $p^{-1}(x_0)$. This defines a homomorphism from $\pi_1(X,x_0)$ to the symmetric group on the fiber $p^{-1}(x_0)$, and the image of this homomorphism is called the monodromy group of the covering space. The cover is connected if and only if the monodromy group acts transitively on the fiber. Relabeling the sheets changes the permutation by conjugation. Thus we have the following.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $X$ be path-connected, locally path-connected, and semilocally simply connected, with basepoint $x_0$. Isomorphism classes of connected $n$-sheeted covering spaces $p:\widetilde X\to X$ are equivalent to transitive permutation representations $\rho:\pi_1(X,x_0)\to S_n$ up to conjugation by $S_n$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}
    The group of \textbf{deck transformations} of a covering space $p:\widetilde X\to X$ is the group of homeomorphisms $f:\widetilde X\to \widetilde X$ such that $p\circ f=p$. The covering space is called \textbf{Galois} if the group of deck transformations acts transitively on the fibers of $p$.
\end{definition}

The covering spaces $\R\to S^1$ and $S^1 \to S^1$ given by $z\mapsto z^n$ are Galois, with deck transformation groups $\Z$ and $\Z/n\Z$ respectively. Galois covering spaces are in correspondense with normal subgroups of $\pi_1(X,x_0)$, and the deck transformation group is isomorphic to the quotient of $\pi_1(X,x_0)$ by the corresponding normal subgroup.

\begin{proposition}
    Let $p:(\tilde X,\tilde x_0)\to (X,x_0)$ be a covering space. Let \[H=p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_0))\subseteq \pi_1(X,x_0)\] be the stabilizer in $\pi_1(X,x_0)$ of the chosen lift $\tilde x_0$ of $x_0$.
    Then \begin{enumerate}
        \item the covering space is Galois if and only if $H$ is a normal subgroup of $\pi_1(X,x_0)$.
        \item $\Aut(p)\cong N_{\pi_1(X,x_0)}(H)/H$, where $N_{\pi_1(X,x_0)}(H)$ is the normalizer of $H$ in $\pi_1(X,x_0)$.
    \end{enumerate}
    In particular, if $H$ is normal, then $\Aut(p)\cong \pi_1(X,x_0)/H$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
    We need to think about loops in $X$ which move the chosen lift $\widetilde x_0$ to another point of the fiber in a way that comes from a deck transformation. Recall that if $\widetilde x_1$ is another point in the fiber, then $p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_1))$ is a conjugate of $H$ in $\pi_1(X,x_0)$, and the conjugating element is given by the projection of any path $[\gamma]$ from $\widetilde x_0$ to $\widetilde x_1$. If there is a deck transformation sending $\widetilde x_0$ to $\widetilde x_1$, then $\tau_*$ induces an isomorphism from $\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_0)$ to $\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_1)$. The identity $p\circ \tau = p$ implies that $p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_0))=p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_1))$, so we see that we must have \begin{align*}
        [\gamma]H[\gamma]^{-1} & = H
    \end{align*} In particular $[\gamma]$ must lie in the normalizer of $H$. The lifting criterion gives the converse, so there is a deck transformation taking $\widetilde x_0$ to $\widetilde x_1$ if and only if
    $H_{\widetilde x_0}=H_{\widetilde x_1}$.


    Given $[\gamma]\in N(H)$ we can construct a deck transformation $\tau_{[\gamma]}$ as follows. Lift $\gamma$ to a path starting at $x_0$. Since $[\gamma]$ normalizes $H$, the endpoint of this lift is a point $\widetilde x_1$ in the fiber such that $p_*(\pi_1(\tilde X,\tilde x_1))=H$. Hence, by the lifting criterion, there is a deck transformation $\tau_{[\gamma]}$ taking $\widetilde x_0$ to $\widetilde x_1$. The map $[\gamma]\mapsto \tau_{[\gamma]}$ is a homomorphism from $N(H)$ to $\Aut(p)$. It is clearly surjective because given $\tau \in \Aut(p)$, we can take any path from $\widetilde x_0$ to $\tau(\widetilde x_0)$, and the projection of this path is a loop in $X$ whose class lies in $N(H)$ and maps to $\tau$. The kernel of this homomorphism is exactly $H$ because if $[\gamma]$ induces the identity deck transformation, then the lift of $\gamma$ starting at $\widetilde x_0$ is a loop, so $[\gamma]\in H$.
\end{proof}

\begin{exercise}
    Find all 2-sheeted covering spaces of $S^1\vee S^1$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    The fundamental group of $S^1\vee S^1$ is the free group on two generators, say $a$ and $b$. A 2-sheeted covering space is determined by the action of $\pi_1(S^1\vee S^1)$ on the fiber of the cover, which is a set of two points. Therefore, we simply have to classify homomorphisms $F_2 \to S_2$ with transitive image, up to conjugation by $S_2$. There are three such homomorphisms \begin{align*}
        (a,b) & \mapsto (1,1) \\
        (a,b) & \mapsto (0,1) \\
        (a,b) & \mapsto (1,0)
    \end{align*}
\end{solution}

\begin{exercise}
    Find all 3-sheeted covering spaces of $S^1\vee S^1$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    Homomorphisms from $F_2$ to $S_3$ are determined by the images of the generators $a$ and $b$. Thus we need to classify pairs of elements of $S_3$ that generate a transitive subgroup, up to simultaneous conjugation by $S_3$. There are two transitive subgroups of $S_3$, namely $S_3$ itself and $A_3\cong \Z/3$.

    Consider the cyclic monodromy cases where $\langle \sigma,\tau\rangle=A_3$. Since $A_3=\{e,(123),(132)\}$ and conjugation by a transposition swaps $(123)$ and $(132)$, the nonzero pairs in $A_3^2$ modulo this symmetry give 4 cases:
    \begin{align*}
        (e,(123)), ((123),e), ((123),(123)), ((123),(132))
    \end{align*}
    These are normal covers, because the stabilizer of a single sheet, say labeled 1, is the subgroup of $A_3$ that fixes 1, which is $H = \{e\}$, and $H$ is normal in $A_3$.

    Now suppose $\langle \sigma,\tau\rangle=S_3$. Then there are three more simultaneous-conjugacy classes:
    \begin{align*}
        ((12),(23)), ((12),(123)),((123),(12))
    \end{align*}
    These are genuinely non-normal covers, because the stabilizer of a single sheet, say labeled 1, is the subgroup of $S_3$ that fixes 1, which is $H = \{e,(23)\}$, and $H$ is not normal in $S_3$. Note that a surjective homomorphism $F_2 \to S_3$ carries normal subgroups to normal subgroups, and preimages of normal subgroups are normal.
\end{solution}

\subsection{Homology and cohomology}

We begin with a discussion of cellular homology. The degree of a map $f : S^n \to S^n$ is the integer $d$ such that $f_* : H_n(S^n) \to H_n(S^n)$ is multiplication by $d$.

Let $X$ be a CW complex. Define a chain complex $C_\bullet(X)$ by letting $C_n(X)$ be the free abelian group generated by the $n$-cells of $X$, and differential $d_n : C_n(X) \to C_{n-1}(X)$ be given by the degree of the attaching map of each $n$-cell. \begin{align*}
    d_n(e^n_\alpha) & = \sum_\beta d_{\alpha\beta} e^{n-1}_\beta
\end{align*} where $d_{\alpha\beta}$ is the degree of the following map of $S^{n-1}$:
\[S^{n-1} \xrightarrow{\text{attaching map}} X^{n-1} \to X^{n-1}/X^{n-2} \cong \bigvee_{\gamma} S^{n-1} \to S^{n-1}\] where the last map collapses all spheres except the one corresponding to $e^{n-1}_\beta$. The differential obeys $d^2 = 0$ because it is derived from the long exact sequence of the triple $(X^n,X^{n-1},X^{n-2})$.

\begin{theorem}
    Let $(X,A)$ be a CW pair. Then there is a long exact sequence in homology
    \[\cdots \to H_n(A) \to H_n(X) \to H_n(X,A) \to H_{n-1}(A) \to \cdots\]
    Moreover the quotient map $X \to X/A$ induces an isomorphism \[H_n(X,A) \cong H_n(X/A,A/A) \cong \tilde{H}_n(X/A)\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
    [Excision] For subspaces $A,B$ whose interiors cover $X$, the inclusion $(B,B\cap A) \to (X,A)$ induces an isomorphism in homology
    \[H_n(B,B\cap A) \cong H_n(X,A).
    \] In particular, the statement applies to CW complexes $X$ covered by two subcomplexes $A$ and $B$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}
    Consider the genus $g$ oriented surface $M_g$. We can give $M_g$ a CW structure with one $0$-cell, $2g$ $1$-cells, and one $2$-cell. The cellular chain complex is
    \[0 \to \Z \xrightarrow{0} \Z^{2g} \xrightarrow{0} \Z \to 0\]
    The differential $d_2:C_2(M_g) \to C_1(M_g)$ is zero because the coefficient of a 1-cell in $d_2(e^2)$ is the total exponent of that generator in the attaching word, which is zero because the commutator $[a_i,b_i]$ has total exponent zero for both $a_i$ and $b_i$. The differential $d_1:C_1(M_g) \to C_0(M_g)$ is zero because each 1-cell is has both endpoints at the single 0-cell. Hence the homology groups are
    \begin{align*}
        H_0(M_g) & \cong \Z      \\
        H_1(M_g) & \cong \Z^{2g} \\
        H_2(M_g) & \cong \Z
    \end{align*}
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    We consider the homology of $\R\P^n$, which as a CW complex has one cell in each dimension from $0$ to $n$. The cellular chain complex is
    \[0 \to \Z e^n \to \Z e^{n-1} \to \cdots \to \Z e^1 \to \Z \to 0\]
    where the degrees of the differentials are given by studying the attaching maps \[S^{k-1}\to \R\P^{k-1} \to \R\P^{k-1}/\R\P^{k-2}\cong S^{k-1}\] This map identifies with the antipodal map on $S^{k-1}$, which has degree $(-1)^k$. This can be seen by embedding $S^{k-1}$ in $\R^k$ and noting that the antipodal map is given by multiplication by $-1$, which has determinant $(-1)^k$. Hence the cellular chain complex is
    \[0 \to \ e^n \xrightarrow{(-1)^n} \Z e^{n-1} \xrightarrow{(-1)^{n-1}} \cdots \xrightarrow{-1} \ \Z e^1 \xrightarrow{1} \Z \to 0\]
    The homology groups are
    \begin{align*}
        H_k(\R\P^n) & \cong \begin{cases}
                                \Z     & k=0                 \\
                                \Z/2\Z & 0<k<n \text{ odd} \\
                                0      & \text{otherwise}
                            \end{cases} \\
        H_n(\R\P^n) & \cong \begin{cases}
                                \Z & n \text{ odd}  \\
                                0  & n \text{ even}
                            \end{cases}
    \end{align*}
\end{example}


\begin{theorem}[Mayer--Vietoris]
    Suppose that $X = A \cup B$ where the interiors of $A$ and $B$ cover $X$. Then there is a long exact sequence in homology \begin{align*}
        \cdots \to H_n(A\cap B) \to H_n(A)\oplus H_n(B) \to H_n(X) \to H_{n-1}(A\cap B) \to \cdots
    \end{align*} where the map \begin{align*}
        H_n(A\cap B) & \to H_n(A)\oplus H_n(B)        \\
        x            & \mapsto (i_{A*}(x),-i_{B*}(x))
    \end{align*} is induced by inclusion and \begin{align*}
        H_n(A) & \oplus H_n(B)\to H_n(X) \\
        (a,b)  & \mapsto a+b
    \end{align*}
\end{theorem}
There is an identical Mayer--Vietoris sequence in reduced homology.
\begin{exercise}
    Compute $H_i(S^n \backslash X)$ for $X= S^k \vee S^\ell$ and $X = S^k \coprod S^\ell$.
\end{exercise}

\begin{solution}
    If $X = S^k \vee S^\ell$, then we can write $U = S^n \backslash S^k$ and $V = S^n \backslash S^\ell$. Then $U\cap V = S^n \backslash X$ and $U\cup V = S^n \backslash * \cong \R^n$. The Mayer--Vietoris sequence for the cover $U\cup V$ of $S^n \backslash X$ gives
    \begin{align*}
        \cdots \to H_i(U\cap V) & \to H_i(U)\oplus H_i(V) \to H_i(S^n \backslash X) \to H_{i-1}(U\cap V) \to \cdots
    \end{align*}
    Since $U\cup V$ is contractible, the map $H_i(U)\oplus H_i(V) \to H_i(S^n \backslash X)$ is an isomorphism for $i\ge 1$. In degree $i=0$, $S^n \backslash X$ is path connected, so $H_0(S^n \backslash X)\cong \Z$. We can identify $H_i(U) = H_i(S^n \backslash S^k) \cong H_i(S^{n-k-1})$ and $H_i(V) = H_i(S^n \backslash S^\ell) \cong H_i(S^{n-\ell-1})$ because $S^n \backslash S^k$ is homotopy equivalent to $S^{n-k-1}$.

    Put $S^k$ as the equator $\{(x,0)\}\subset S^n\subset \mathbb R^{k+1}\oplus \mathbb R^{n-k}$. In the complement, the normal coordinate $y$ is nonzero. The map $(x,y)\mapsto (0,y/|y|)$ is a deformation retraction, obtained by shrinking the x-coordinate to zero and renormalizing. Thus the complement retracts onto the sphere of normal directions $S^{n-k-1}$.

     Hence we have that $H_i(S^n \backslash X)$ is $\Z$ in degrees $0$, $n-k-1$, and $n-\ell-1$, and is zero otherwise.
\end{solution}

Let $G$ be an abelian group. The cohomology with coefficients in $G$ is defined by dualizing the chain complex $C_\bullet(X)$ which defines homology. The failure of the homology of the two chain complexes to agree is measured by the functor $\Ext^1_\Z(-,G)$.

\begin{theorem}
    [Universal coefficient theorem for cohomology] Let $X$ be a topological space and let $G$ be an abelian group. Then there is a short exact sequence
    \[0 \to \Ext^1_\Z(H_{n-1}(X),G) \to H^n(X;G) \to \Hom_\Z(H_n(X),G) \to 0\]
    which splits, but not naturally.
\end{theorem}
The second map $h$ in the sequence is given by evaluating a cohomology class on homology classes, i.e. $[\alpha]\mapsto (x\mapsto \alpha(x))$. The first map is given by the following construction. The kernel of $h$ is those cohomology classes $[\alpha]$ whose cocycle representative $\alpha\in C^n(X;G)$ has the following property. Restrict $\alpha:Z_n(X)\to G$. Since $\alpha$ is a cocycle, $\alpha$ vanishes on $B_n(X)$, so $\alpha$ induces a homomorphism from $Z_n(X)/B_n(X)=H_n(X)$ to $G$. The kernel of $h$ are those $[\alpha]$ so that the induced map $\alpha: H_n(X)\to G$ is zero. Now we can identify $\Ext_\Z^1(H_{n-1}(X),G)$ with the kernel of $h$. Being in the kernel of $h$ means that $\alpha: Z_n(X)\to G$ is $0$. There is a short exact sequence \begin{align*}
    0 \to Z_n \to C_n \to B_{n-1} \to 0
\end{align*}Since $\varphi$ vanishes on $Z_n$, it factors uniquely through the quotient $C_n/Z_n\cong B_{n-1}$. So there is a unique homomorphism $f:B_{n-1}\to G$ such that $\varphi=f\circ d_n$. We have to divide by coboundaries, which can be identified with $\im(\Hom_\Z(C_{n-1},G)\to \Hom_\Z(B_{n-1},G))$. Altogether we have \begin{align*}
    \ker h
\cong
\frac{\operatorname{Hom}(B_{n-1},G)}
{\operatorname{im}\bigl(\operatorname{Hom}(Z_{n-1},G)\to \operatorname{Hom}(B_{n-1},G)\bigr)} \cong \operatorname{Ext}^1_\Z(H_{n-1}(X),G)
\end{align*}
The last identification follows from applying $\Hom(-,G)$ to the short exact sequence \begin{align*}
0 \to B_{n-1} \to Z_{n-1} \to H_{n-1}\to 0
\end{align*}

\begin{proposition}
    [Facts about $\Ext^1_\Z$] Let $A$ and $B$ be abelian groups. Then \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\Ext^1_\Z(A,B) = 0$ if $A$ is free or $B$ is injective.
        \item $\Ext^1_\Z(\Z/n\Z,B) \cong B/nB$
        \item In particular, $\Ext^1_\Z(\Z/n\Z,\Z) \cong \Z/n\Z$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\subsubsection{Cup product}
We are now prepared to introduce an important structure on cohomology, namely the cup product. It is defined on cochains by the formula
\begin{align*}
    (\alpha\cup \beta)(\sigma) & = \alpha(\sigma|_{[0,\ldots,p]})\cdot \beta(\sigma|_{[p,\ldots,p+q]})
\end{align*}where $\sigma$ is a singular simplex of dimension $p+q$, and $\alpha$ and $\beta$ are cochains of degree $p$ and $q$ respectively. The cup product satisfies the relation \begin{align}
    \delta(\alpha\cup \beta) & = \delta\alpha\cup \beta + (-1)^p \alpha\cup \delta\beta
\end{align}
This formula implies that the cup product of two cocycles is a cocycle, and the cup product of a coboundary with any cochain is a coboundary. Hence the cup product descends to cohomology, giving $H^\bullet(X;G)$ the structure of a graded ring. Moreover the cup product respects pullback, in the sense that if $f:X\to Y$ is a continuous map, then $f^*(\alpha\cup \beta) = f^*(\alpha)\cup f^*(\beta)$.

\begin{proposition}
    The cup product on cohomology is graded commutative, in the sense that if $\alpha\in H^p(X;G)$ and $\beta\in H^q(X;G)$, then $\alpha\cup \beta = (-1)^{pq} \beta\cup \alpha$.
\end{proposition}

The external cup product map \begin{align*}
    H^*(X;R) \times H^*(Y;R) & \to H^*(X\times Y;R) \\
    (\alpha,\beta) & \mapsto \pi_X^*(\alpha)\cup \pi_Y^*(\beta) 
\end{align*} is bilinear and therefore induces a homomorphism of $R$-modules \begin{align*}
    H^*(X;R) \otimes_R H^*(Y;R) & \to H^*(X\times Y;R)
\end{align*}
If we define the multiplication on the left hand side by the formula \begin{align*}
    (\alpha_1\otimes \beta_1)(\alpha_2\otimes \beta_2) & = (-1)^{|\beta_1||\alpha_2|}(\alpha_1\cup \alpha_2)\otimes (\beta_1\cup \beta_2)
\end{align*} then the external cup product map is a homomorphism of graded rings. The Künneth formula gives a criterion for when this map is an isomorphism.

\begin{theorem}[Künneth formula]
    The external cup product map \[H^*(X;R) \otimes_R H^*(Y;R) \to H^*(X\times Y;R)\] is an isomorphism if $X$ and $Y$ are CW complexes and $H^*(X;R)$ is a finitely generated free $R$-module in each degree.
\end{theorem}
In fact, the CW approximation theorem implies that we can remove the CW hypothesis. However the freeness is indispensable.

\subsection{Poincare duality}
Let $M$ be an $n$-manifold. A local orientation of $M$ with coefficients in a ring $R$ at a point $x\in M$ is a choice of generator of the $R$-module $H_n(M,M\backslash \{x\};R) \cong H_{n-1}(S^{n-1};R)$. An orientation of $M$ with coefficients in $R$ is an assignment $x\mapsto o_x$ of a local orientation to each point $x\in M$ such that for every $x\in M$, there is an open neighborhood $U$ of $x$ such that the local orientations $o_y$ for $y\in U$ are compatible in the following sense. For every $y,z\in U$, the local orientations $o_y$ and $o_z$ are compatible if they induce the same generator of $H_n(U,U\backslash \{y,z\};R)$. 

In other words, an orientation of $M$ is a section of the orientation sheaf $\mathcal O_M$ of $M$, which is the locally constant sheaf on $M$ whose stalk at $x$ is $H_n(M,M\backslash \{x\};R)$. If $R = \Z$ then the total space of this sheaf is a double sheeted cover of $M$, 
\begin{proposition}
    Let $R = \Z$. $M$ is orientable if and only if the orientation sheaf $\mathcal O_M$ is trivial, i.e. admits a global section, if and only if $\tilde M \to M$ has two connected components. In particular, this is the case if $M$ is simply connected or $\pi_1(M)$ has no subgroup of index 2.
\end{proposition}

In general, one has the canonical map \begin{align*}
H_n(M;R) & \to \Gamma(M,\mathcal O_M) \\
[\alpha] & \mapsto (x\mapsto \alpha(o_x))
\end{align*}
The following theorem asserts that this map is an isomorphism when $M$ is closed. If moreover $M$ is connected, then a section of a local system is determined by its value at a single point, provided that value is invariant under monodromy. If $M$ is $R$-orientable, then the monodromy is trivial and any nonzero value at a single point gives a global section. In this case, we can identify $\Gamma(M,\mathcal O_M)$ with $R$, and the isomorphism $H^n(M;R)\cong \Gamma(M,\mathcal O_M)$ gives an isomorphism $H^n(M;R)\cong R$. If $M$ is not $R$-orientable, then the monodromy is nontrivial and acts by multiplication by $-1$ on the fiber of $\mathcal O_M$. In this case, the invariant sections of $\mathcal O_M$ are those $r\in R$ such that $-r=r$, the $2$-torsion elements of $R$. In summary we have the following theorem: 

\begin{theorem}
    Let $M$ be a connected closed $n$ manifold. If $M$ is $R$-orientable, then $H_n(M;R)\cong R$ and if $M$ is not $R$-orientable, then $H_n(M;R)\cong \{r\in R: 2r=0\}$.
\end{theorem}

The Poincare duality theorem asserts for a closed connected $R$-orientable manifold $M$ that there is a $H^*(M;R)$-module structure on $H_*(M;R)$ which induces isomorphisms $H^k(M;R)\cong H_{n-k}(M;R)$ for all $k$. The cap product $C^p(X;G)\times C_q(X;G) \to C_{q-p}(X;G)$ is defined on cochains by the formula \begin{align*}
    \alpha\cap \sigma = \alpha(\sigma|_{[v_0,\ldots,v_p]})\cdot \sigma|_{[v_p,\ldots,v_q]}
\end{align*} where $\sigma$ is a singular simplex of dimension $q$ and $\alpha$ is a cochain of degree $p$. The cap product descends to homology and cohomology, thereby inducing \begin{align*}
    \cap : H^p(X;G)\times H_q(X;G) & \to H_{q-p}(X;G) 
\end{align*} functorial in the sense that \begin{align*}
    f_*(\alpha\cap x) & = f^*(\alpha)\cap f_*(x)
\end{align*} for any continuous map $f:X\to Y$. 

\begin{theorem}
    [Poincare duality] Let $M$ be a closed connected $R$-orientable $n$-manifold. Then capping with a generator of $H_n(M;R)$ gives an isomorphism of $H^*(M;R)$-modules $H^k(M;R)\cong H_{n-k}(M;R)$ for all $k$.
\end{theorem}

Let $M$ be connected. There is a natural evaluation pairing \[H^k(M;R)\times H_{k}(M;R) \to H_0(M;R)\cong R\] given by evaluating a cohomology class on a homology class. Note that $H^0(M;R)\cong R$ is generated by the class of the constant function $1$. If $M$ is a closed oriented $n$-manifold and $B\subset M$ is an oriented codimension-$k$ submanifold, then $\operatorname{PD}([B])\in H^k(X;\mathbb Z)$ is characterized by the property that for every $k$-cycle $A\subset X$,
\[\left\langle \operatorname{PD}([B]),[A]\right\rangle
=
[B]\cdot [A]\]
where the right side is the signed intersection number, assuming $A$ and $B$ are transverse.

\begin{example}
    Recall that the additive cohomology of $\R\P^n$ with $\Z/2\Z$ coefficients is $\Z/2\Z$ in each degree from $0$ to $n$. Let $x\in H^1(\R\P^n;\Z/2\Z)$ be the generator. We will show that $x^k$ is the generator of $H^k(\R\P^n;\Z/2\Z)$ for each $k$. Let $H\subset \R\P^n$ be a projective hyperplane, so $H\cong \R\P^{n-1}$. The class $x\in H^1(\R\P^n;\Z/2\Z)$ may be identified with the Poincaré dual of $H$. 

    Let $x^k\in H^k(\R\P^n;\Z/2\Z)$ be the $k$-fold cup product of $x$ with itself. Then $x^k = m\eta_k$ where $\eta_k$ is the generator of $H^k(\R\P^n;\Z/2\Z)$ and $m\in \Z/2\Z$. We want to show that $m=1$. We can calculate $m$ using the intersection pairing. We have \begin{align*}
        m = \langle x^k, [H]\rangle & = \#\{H\cap H\cap \cdots \cap H\} \mod 2 \\
        & = 1
    \end{align*}
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    The cup product allows us to distinguish $\C\P^2$ from $S^2 \vee S^4$. The cohomology groups of both spaces are $\Z$ in degrees $0$, $2$, and $4$, and are zero otherwise. However \begin{align*}
    H^*(\C\P^2) & \cong \Z[x]/(x^3) \\
    H^*(S^2 \vee S^4) & \cong \Z [y,z]/(y^2,yz,z^2)
    \end{align*} where $\deg y = 2$ and $\deg z = 4$. The key difference is that $y^2 = 0$ because $y^2$ is a degree $4$ class that is supported on $S^2$, of which there are none.
\end{example}

\begin{theorem}
Let $X$ be a CW complex, and let $X^n$ denote its $n$-skeleton. Then for any coefficient group $A$,
\[
H_k(X;A)\cong \varinjlim_n H_k(X^n;A).
\]
Moreover, there is a natural Milnor exact sequence
\[
0\to \varprojlim^1 H^{k-1}(X^n;A)
\to H^k(X;A)
\to \varprojlim_n H^k(X^n;A)
\to 0.
\]
For the skeletal filtration of a CW complex, the inverse system $\{H^k(X^n;A)\}_n$ is eventually constant, so $\varprojlim^1=0$. Hence
\[
H^k(X;A)\cong \varprojlim_n H^k(X^n;A).
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    The key reason for stabilization is that the inclusion $X^n\hookrightarrow X$ induces \[ H^k(X;A)\xrightarrow{\cong} H^k(X^n;A) \] whenever $n>k$. This is because the relative cohomology group $H^k(X,X^n;A)$ vanishes for $n>k$. This is because $X/X^n$ has no cells in dimensions $\le n$, so $H^k(X/X^n;A)=0$ for $n>k$.

For fixed $k$, once the skeleton is past dimension $k$, the cohomology group no longer changes. Thus the inverse system is eventually constant, and the possible $\varprojlim^1$ obstruction disappears.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
    Consider the infinite-dimensional complex projective space $\C\P^\infty$. It has the CW structure with one cell in each even dimension. The cohomology groups are $\Z$ in even degrees and zero in odd degrees. This can be seen directly from the cellular chain complex, or from the skeleton filtration and the Milnor exact sequence. 

    To compute the cup product, let $x\in H^2(\C\P^\infty)$ be the generator. We will show that $x^k$ is the generator of $H^{2k}(\C\P^\infty)$ for each $k$. Let $N > k$ and pullback $x$ to $H^2(\C\P^N)$. Pullback respects cup product, and we have already shown that $x^k$ is the generator of $H^{2k}(\C\P^N)$. Since $N > k$, the pullback $H^{2k}(\C\P^\infty) \to H^{2k}(\C\P^N)$ is an isomorphism, so $x^k$ is the generator of $H^{2k}(\C\P^\infty)$ as well.
\end{example}


\subsection{Higher homotopy groups}

\begin{definition}
    The $n$-th homotopy group of a pointed space $(X,x)$ is the set of homotopy classes of maps $S^n \to X$ sending the base point to $x$. We denote it by $\pi_n(X,x)$ or simply $\pi_n(X)$ if $X$ is path connected.
\end{definition}
The group law is given by writing $S^n \to S^n \vee S^n$ as the pinch map and then composing with the wedge of two maps $S^n \to X$. $\pi_n(X,x)$ can also be modeled by the set of homotopy classes of maps $I^n \to X$ sending the boundary to $x$. In this model, the group law can be defined by concatenating along the $1$st coordinate. 
The homotopy groups are abelian for $n\geq 2$.

There is an action of $\pi_1(X)$ on $\pi_n(X)$ for $n\geq 2$ by dragging the base point around loops. We say that $X$ is \textbf{$n$-simple} if the action of $\pi_1(X)$ on $\pi_n(X)$ is trivial, and $X$ is \textbf{simple} if it is $n$-simple for all $n$. 

\begin{proposition}
    A covering space projection $p: \tilde X \to X$ induces an isomorphism on $\pi_n$ for $n\geq 2$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
    The map is surjective because we can lift maps $S^n \to X$ to $\tilde X$ because $S^n$ is simply connected. The map is injective because if a map $S^n \to \tilde X$ becomes nullhomotopic in $X$, then we can lift the nullhomotopy to $\tilde X$ and get a nullhomotopy there.
\end{proof}

Moreover, if $\tilde X$ is the universal cover of $X$, then $\pi_1(X)$ acts on $\tilde X$ by deck transformations and this action induces the action of $\pi_1(X)$ on $\pi_n(\tilde X) \cong \pi_n(X)$. 

\begin{proposition}
    A product of path connected spaces $X_\alpha$ has homotopy groups given by the product of the homotopy groups of the factors, i.e. $\pi_n(\prod X_\alpha) \cong \prod \pi_n(X_\alpha)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
This follows immediately from the universal property of products. 
\end{proof}

Another useful notion is the relative homotopy groups $\pi_n(X,A,x)$ for a pair of spaces $A \subset X$. \begin{definition}
Let $x\in A \subset X$ be a pointed pair of spaces. The $n$-th relative homotopy group $\pi_n(X,A,x)$ is the set
    of homotopy classes of maps $(I^n, \partial I^n,J^{n-1}) \to (X,A,x)$ where \[J^{n-1}=\overline{\partial I^n\setminus (I^{n-1}\times\{1\})}\] with homotopies through such maps. Equivalently, it is homotopy classes of maps \[(D^n, S^{n-1}, *) \to (X,A,x)\] modulo homotopies through such maps. 
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
    There is a long exact sequence of homotopy groups \begin{align*}
    \dots \to \pi_n(A) \to \pi_n(X) \to \pi_n(X,A) \to \pi_{n-1}(A) \to \dots
    \end{align*} coming from the inclusion of pairs. The boundary map is the restriction of a map $D^n \to X$ to the boundary $S^{n-1} \to A$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Whitehead's theorem]
    A map $f: X \to Y$ between CW complexes is a homotopy equivalence if and only if it induces isomorphisms on all homotopy groups.
\end{theorem}

Note that two CW complexes may have the same homotopy groups but not be homotopy equivalent. In particular, having a map inducing isomorphisms on homotopy groups is a much stronger condition than just having isomorphic homotopy groups. One situation where the latter implies the former is when all homotopy groups vanish, in which case the inclusion of a point is a homotopy equivalence.

\begin{example}
    Consider \(X=\mathbb{R}\P^2\) and \(Y=S^2\times \mathbb{R}\P^\infty\). These both have fundamental group \(\mathbb{Z}/2\Z\). Their higher homotopy groups are isomorphic since their universal covers \(S^2\) and \(S^2\times S^\infty\) are homotopy equivalent, \(S^\infty\) being contractible. But \(\mathbb{R}P^2\) and \(S^2\times \mathbb{R}P^\infty\) are not homotopy equivalent since their homology groups are vastly different, \(S^2\times \mathbb{R}P^\infty\) having nonvanishing homology in infinitely many dimensions since it retracts onto \(\mathbb{R}P^\infty\). Recall that retracts induce injections on cohomology.
\end{example}

\begin{example}
Another pair of CW complexes that are not homotopy equivalent but have isomorphic homotopy groups is \(S^2\) and \(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty\). Indeed, \(\mathbb{C}P^\infty\) is an Eilenberg--MacLane space \(K(\mathbb{Z},2)\), so
\[
\pi_n(\mathbb{C}P^\infty)=
\begin{cases}
\mathbb{Z}, & n=2,\\
0, & n\neq 2.
\end{cases}
\]
Therefore
\[
\pi_n(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty)
\cong
\pi_n(S^3)\oplus \pi_n(\mathbb{C}P^\infty)
\]
for all \(n\ge 1\), and hence
\[
\pi_n(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty)\cong
\begin{cases}
\mathbb{Z}, & n=2,\\
\pi_n(S^3), & n\ge 3.
\end{cases}
\]

Now consider the Hopf fibration
\[
S^1 \longrightarrow S^3 \longrightarrow S^2.
\]
Its long exact sequence in homotopy implies that
\[
\pi_n(S^3)\cong \pi_n(S^2)
\qquad\text{for all } n\ge 3,
\]
since \(\pi_n(S^1)=0\) for \(n\ge 2\). Also,
\[
\pi_2(S^2)\cong \mathbb{Z},
\qquad
\pi_1(S^2)=0.
\]
Since \(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty\) is simply connected as well, it follows that
\[
\pi_n(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty)\cong \pi_n(S^2)
\qquad\text{for all } n\ge 1.
\]

However, these spaces are not homotopy equivalent. For example,
\[
H_4(S^2)=0,
\]
while
\[
H_4(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty)
\cong
H_4(\mathbb{C}P^\infty)
\cong
\mathbb{Z},
\]
so their homology groups are not isomorphic. Therefore \(S^2\) and \(S^3\times \mathbb{C}P^\infty\) cannot be homotopy equivalent.
\end{example}

A map $f: X \to Y$ is \textbf{cellular} if it sends the $n$-skeleton of $X$ to the $n$-skeleton of $Y$ for all $n$. \begin{theorem}
    Any map $f:X\to Y$ between CW complexes is homotopic to a cellular map. If $f$ is already cellular on a subcomplex $A\subset X$, then the homotopy can be taken to be relative to $A$.
\end{theorem}
It immediately follows that $\pi_k(S^n) = 0$ for $k < n$ because the $k$-skeleton of $S^n$ is a point.

\begin{definition}
    A map $f: X \to Y$ is a weak homotopy equivalence if it induces isomorphisms on all homotopy groups. 
\end{definition}
Whitehead's theorem reformaulated says that a weak homotopy equivalence between CW complexes is a homotopy equivalence. However, a weak homotopy equivalence between general spaces need not be a homotopy equivalence. For example, there exist spaces which have trivial homotopy groups but are not contractible. For these spaces, the constant map to a point is a weak homotopy equivalence but not a homotopy equivalence.

\begin{example}
    Consider the quasi-circle $Y$ which is a closed subspace of $\R^2$ obtained by taking a portion of the graph of $y = \sin(1/x)$, the segment $[-1,1]$ in the $y$-axis, and connecting them with a curve. This space comes with a map $p: Y \to S^1$ which collapses the sine curve and the interval to a point. Then this space has trivial homotopy groups. Any map from $S^n$ to this space is nullhomotopic because the image is compact, so it must meet the sine-curve in finitely many oscillations. This will clearly be nullhomotopic.  
    
    However, the quasi-circle is not contractible because if it were, then $p$ would be nullhomotopic and we could lift $p$ to the universal cover $\R$ of $S^1$. The lifted map $\tilde p: Y \to \R$ in fact factors through $Y/I = S^1$ because the quotient map collapses $I$ to a point. Then this gives a section of the universal cover $\R \to S^1$, which is impossible.
\end{example}

\begin{definition}
    Let $Y$ be a topological space. A \textit{CW--approximation} of $Y$ is a CW complex $X$ together with a map $f:X\to Y$ such that for every CW complex $Z$, the induced map $f_*:[Z,X]\to [Z,Y]$ is a bijection, where $[-,-]$ denotes the set of homotopy classes of maps.
\end{definition}
Taking $Z = S^n$ for all $n$ shows that a CW approximation is a weak homotopy equivalence. Conversely, if $f:X\to Y$ is a weak homotopy equivalence and $X$ is a CW complex, then $f$ is a CW approximation by Whitehead's theorem. 

\begin{theorem}
    Every topological space $Y$ admits a CW approximation $X\to Y$. If $X$ is path connected, $X$ can be chosen to have a single $0$ cell, with all other cells attached by basepoint-preserving maps. 
\end{theorem}

There is also a relative version of CW approximation.  

\begin{theorem}[Relative CW approximation]
Let $(X,A)$ be a pair of spaces with $A$ a CW complex (or more generally a
subspace). Then there exists a relative CW complex $(Z,A)$ and a map of pairs
\[
f:(Z,A)\longrightarrow (X,A)
\]
such that:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item $f|_A = \mathrm{id}_A$,
    \item $f$ is a weak homotopy equivalence of pairs, i.e.
    \[
    \pi_k(Z,A) \xrightarrow{\;\cong\;} \pi_k(X,A)
    \quad \text{for all } k.
    \]
\end{enumerate}
If $(X,A)$ is $n$-connected,
i.e.
\[
\pi_k(X,A)=0 \quad \text{for all } k\le n,
\]
then there exists a relative CW complex $(Z,A)$ and a weak equivalence of
pairs
\[
(Z,A)\xrightarrow{\;\simeq\;} (X,A)
\]
such that $(Z,A)$ is obtained from $A$ by attaching no cells in dimensions $\le n$. Equivalently, the first relative cells occur in dimension $n+1$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Homotopy excision]
Let $X$ and $Y$ be subcomplexes of a CW complex $Z=X\cup Y$, and let
\[
A=X\cap Y.
\]
Assume that the pairs $(X,A)$ and $(Y,A)$ are $m$-connected and
$n$-connected respectively. Then inclusion of pairs induces a map
\[
\pi_k(X,A)\longrightarrow \pi_k(X\cup Y,\,Y).
\]
The map is an isomorphism for $k<m+n$ and a surjection for $k=m+n$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}
    Let $Y = CA$ be the cone on $A$ as in the previous setup. Note that the long exact sequence of the pair $(Y,A)$ shows that $(Y,A)$ is $n+1$-connected if $A$ is $n$-connected. Then $X \cup_A CA = X/A$ and since $CA$ is contractible, we get \begin{align*}
        \pi_k(X \cup_A CA, CA) & \cong \pi_k(X/A)
    \end{align*}
    For $(X,A)$ $m$-connected and $A$ $(n-1)$-connected, homotopy excision gives a comparision map \begin{align*}
    \pi_k(X,A) & \to \pi_k(X/A)
    \end{align*} which is an isomorphism for $k < m+n$ and a surjection for $k = m+n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{corollary}
    [Freudenthal suspension theorem] Let $Z$ be an $n$-connected space. Then the suspension map $\pi_k(Z) \to \pi_{k+1}(\Sigma Z)$ is an isomorphism for $k < 2n$ and a surjection for $k = 2n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    Write the suspension \begin{align*}
        \Sigma Z & = CZ \cup_X CZ
     \end{align*} as the union of two cones on $Z$ glued along $Z$. Now apply homotopy excision to $X = CZ$ and $Y = CZ$. Then $A = Z$ and the pairs $(X,A)$ and $(Y,A)$ are $n+1$-connected by the long exact sequence in homotopy.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
    [Hurewicz theorem] Let $X$ be a path connected space. For each $n$ there is a map \begin{align*}
    \pi_n(X) & \to H_n(X)
    \end{align*} which takes $[f:S^n \to X]$ to $f_*([S^n])$. If $X$ is $(n-1)$-connected, then this map is an isomorphism in degree $n$, zero in degree less than $n$, and a surjection in degree $n+1$.
\end{theorem}

The Hurewicz theorem recovers the Hopf theorem from differential topology which says that if $M$ is an n-dimensional compact connected oriented manifold and $f,g$ are two continuous maps $M \to S^n$, then the degree of $f$ equals the degree of $g$ if and only if $f$ and $g$ are homotopic. 

This result can be established traditionally by understanding the degree of a smooth map as the signed count of the preimages of a regular value, where a preimage has a positive sign if the derivative is orientation preserving and a negative sign if the derivative is orientation reversing. 

\begin{corollary}
    $\pi_n(S^n) \cong \Z$ for all $n$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
    The Hurewicz map $\pi_n(S^n) \to H_n(S^n)$ is an isomorphism because $S^n$ is $(n-1)$-connected. Since $H_n(S^n) \cong \Z$, we get $\pi_n(S^n) \cong \Z$ as well.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
    The Hopf fibration $S^1 \to S^3 \to S^2$ can be constructed as follows. We can identify $S^3$ with the unit quaternions, and $S^2$ with the unit imaginary quaternions. Then the map $S^3 \to S^2$ is given by $q \mapsto q i q^{-1}$. The fiber over $i$ is the subgroup of unit quaternions that commute with $i$, which is precisely the unit complex numbers, i.e. $S^1$. The long exact sequence in homotopy associated to this fibration gives $\pi_n(S^3) \cong \pi_n(S^2)$ for all $n\ge 3$.

In particular $\pi_3(S^2) \cong \Z$ is generated by the Hopf map. An application of the Freudenthal suspension theorem shows that $\pi_3(S^2) \to \pi_4(S^3)$ is a surjection, and in fact indeed $\pi_4(S^3) \cong \Z/2\Z$ is generated by the suspension of the Hopf map. 
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    We can calculate all of the homotopy groups of $\C\P^\infty$. The Hopf fibrations $S^1 \to S^{2n+1} \to \C\P^n$ give rise to the infinite Hopf fibration $S^1 \to S^\infty \to \C\P^\infty$. Since $S^\infty$ is contractible, the long exact sequence in homotopy associated to this fibration gives $\pi_n(\C\P^\infty) = 0$ for $n\ge 3$ and $\pi_2(\C\P^\infty) \cong \pi_1(S^1) \cong \Z$. Clearly $\pi_1(\C\P^\infty) = 0$ as well, so we have computed all of the homotopy groups of $\C\P^\infty$.
\end{example}

The previous computation shows that $\C\P^\infty$ is an Eilenberg--MacLane space $K(\Z,2)$. 

\begin{definition}
    Let $G$ be a group and $n\ge 1$. An Eilenberg--MacLane space of type $K(G,n)$ is a path connected space $X$ such that $\pi_n(X) \cong G$ and $\pi_k(X) = 0$ for all $k\neq n$. For $n\ge 2$, the group $G$ must be abelian since $\pi_n(X)$ is abelian for $n\ge 2$.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
    An $H$-space is a space $X$ and a point $e\in X$ together with a continuous map $\mu: X\times X \to X$ such that $\mu(x,-)$ and $\mu(-,x)$ are homotopic to $x$ through $(X,e)$-based homotopies for all $x\in X$. 
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
    Eilenberg--MacLane spaces of type $K(G,n)$ exist for all groups $G$ and all $n\ge 1$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We first discuss existence. For $n=1$, let $G$ be any group. Choose a connected CW complex $X$ with
\[
\pi_1(X)\cong G.
\]
For example, take a wedge of circles with one circle for each generator of $G$, and attach $2$-cells realizing the relations. This constructs a connected CW complex with fundamental group $G$.

The higher homotopy groups of $X$ may be nonzero. We kill them inductively by attaching cells. Suppose we have constructed a CW complex $X_r$ with
\[
\pi_1(X_r)\cong G
\]
and such that $\pi_k(X_r)=0$ for $2\le k<r$. To kill $\pi_r(X_r)$, attach $(r+1)$-cells along representatives
\[
S^r\to X_r
\]
of elements of $\pi_r(X_r)$. Attaching an $(r+1)$-cell along a map $S^r\to X_r$ makes that class nullhomotopic. This is because if $X' = X_r \cup_f D^{r+1}$ where $f: S^r \to X_r$ is the attaching map, then $f:S^r \to X'$ extends across the disk $D^{r+1}$, so $f$ becomes nullhomotopic in $X'$, i.e. $i_*(f) = 0$.

Attaching enough such cells kills all of $\pi_r(X_r)$ without changing $\pi_1$ or the already-vanishing lower homotopy groups. This is because the relative CW complex $(X',X_r)$ has no cells in dimensions $\le r$. Continuing inductively gives a CW complex $K(G,1)$ with
\[
\pi_1(K(G,1))\cong G,
\qquad
\pi_k(K(G,1))=0 \quad \text{for } k\ge 2.
\]

For $n\ge 2$, the group $G$ must be abelian. One starts with a CW complex whose only first nonzero homotopy group is $G$ in degree $n$. For example, if $G$ is free abelian, take a product or wedge of $n$-spheres and then arrange the desired group in $\pi_n$. For a general abelian group, one can build a Moore space $M(G,n)$ satisfying
\[
\widetilde H_k(M(G,n);\mathbb Z)=0 \quad \text{for } k\neq n,
\qquad
\widetilde H_n(M(G,n);\mathbb Z)\cong G.
\]
By the Hurewicz theorem, if $M(G,n)$ is chosen $(n-1)$-connected, then
\[
\pi_n(M(G,n))\cong H_n(M(G,n))\cong G.
\]
The higher homotopy groups may again be nonzero. Attach cells of dimension $>n+1$ inductively to kill
\[
\pi_{n+1},\pi_{n+2},\dots
\]
without changing $\pi_n$. This produces a CW complex $K(G,n)$ with
\[
\pi_n(K(G,n))\cong G,
\qquad
\pi_k(K(G,n))=0 \quad \text{for } k\neq n \qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}
    $K(G,n)$ represents cohomology in the sense that for any CW complex $X$,
\[[X,K(G,n)]\cong H^n(X;G)
\]
$K(G,n)$ is unique up to homotopy equivalence. The addition law on cohomology gives $K(G,n)$ the structure of an $H$-space.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Since $K(G,n)$ has no homotopy below degree $n$, any map on $X^{n-1}$ to $K(G,n)$ is nullhomotopic. To extend over an $n$-cell $e^n$ having fixed the boundary map $S^{n-1} \to K(G,n)$, is to choose a map $S^n \to K(G,n)$, which is the same as choosing an element of $\pi_n(K(G,n))$. Having fixed the boundary $g:S^{n-1} \to K(G,n)$, two extensions $f,f':D^n \to K(G,n)$ determine a map $S^n \to K(G,n)$ by gluing $f$ and $f'$ along the boundary. This gives an element $[f-f']\in \pi_n(K(G,n))\cong G$. So choices of extensions form a torsor for $\pi_n(Y)$.

It remains to explain uniqueness. Let $X$ and $Y$ be two $K(G,n)$ spaces. By obstruction theory, maps from a CW complex into a $K(G,n)$ are classified by degree-$n$ cohomology:
\[
[X,K(G,n)]\cong H^n(X;G).
\]
Equivalently, a map $f:X\to K(G,n)$ is determined up to homotopy by the pullback of the universal class
\[
u\in H^n(K(G,n);G).
\]
Now suppose $X$ and $Y$ are both $K(G,n)$ spaces. Choose an isomorphism
\[
\varphi:\pi_n(X)\xrightarrow{\cong}\pi_n(Y).
\]
Since $Y$ is a $K(G,n)$, choosing a map $X\to Y$ is the same as choosing a class in $H^n(X;\pi_n(Y))$
On the other hand, since $X$ is also a $K(G,n)$, the Hurewicz theorem and universal coefficients identify the relevant degree-$n$ cohomology with
\[
H^n(X;\pi_n(Y))\cong \operatorname{Hom}(\pi_n(X),\pi_n(Y)).
\]
Under this identification, choose the class corresponding to $\varphi$. Obstruction theory gives a map $f:X\to Y$
whose induced map on $\pi_n$ is precisely $\varphi$.

Since $X$ and $Y$ have no other nonzero homotopy groups, $f$ induces isomorphisms on all homotopy groups. Therefore $f$ is a weak homotopy equivalence, and by Whitehead's theorem it is a homotopy equivalence.

Let $u\in H^n(K(G,n);G)$ be the universal class, corresponding to the identity map under the isomorphism $[K(G,n),K(G,n)]\cong H^n(K(G,n);G)$. On the product $K(G,n)\times K(G,n)$, we have the class $p_1^*u + p_2^*u$ where $p_1$ and $p_2$ are the projections. Their sum is a class $p_1^*u + p_2^*u$ in $H^n(K(G,n)\times K(G,n);G)$ and is classified by a map $\mu: K(G,n)\times K(G,n) \to K(G,n)$. Now for any space $X$, given two maps $f,g:X\to K(G,n)$ their sum is defined by
\[
X\xrightarrow{(f,g)}K(G,n)\times K(G,n)
\xrightarrow{\mu}K(G,n).
\]
Then
\[(\mu\circ(f,g))^*u
=
(f,g)^*\mu^*u
=
(f,g)^*(p_1^*u+p_2^*u)
=
f^*u+g^*u\]
In particular $\mu$ is defined so that $[f]+[g]
=[\mu\circ(f,g)]$. \qedhere
\end{proof}

\begin{example}
    Recall that the standard model for a contractible space with a free $S^1$ action is $ES^1 \cong S^\infty$. The quotient $BS^1 = ES^1/S^1$ is modeled by $\C\P^\infty$ and has the property that it classifies $S^1$-bundles in the sense that for any space $X$, there is a natural bijection
\[[X,\C\P^\infty]\cong \{\text{isomorphism classes of }S^1\text{-bundles on }X\}.\]
In particular, topological line bundles on $X$ are classified by maps $X \to \C\P^\infty$, which are determined up to homotopy by their first Chern class $f^*u \in H^2(X;\Z)$ where $u$ is the universal class in $H^2(\C\P^\infty;\Z)$ and $f:X\to \C\P^\infty$ is the classifying map of the line bundle. In terms of the $H$-space structure on $\C\P^\infty$, the tensor product of line bundles corresponds to the addition of maps $X \to \C\P^\infty$ under the group law on $\C\P^\infty$.
\end{example}

\subsection{Exercises}
\begin{example}
    We compute $\pi_1$ and $\pi_2$, homology, and cohomology of $X = S^1 \vee S^2$. By Van Kampen's theorem, $\pi_1(X) \cong \pi_1(S^1) * \pi_1(S^2) \cong \Z$. The universal cover of $S^1 \vee S^2$ is a wedge of countably many copies of $S^2$ and therefore $\pi_2(S^1 \vee S^2)$ is a countable direct sum of copies of $\Z$. There is an action of $\pi_1(X) = \Z$ acting by deck transformations on the universal cover, and this induces an action of $\Z$ on $\pi_2(X)$ by translating the summands.

    Reduced homology of a wedge is the direct sum of reduced homologies, so therefore \[H_k(X) \cong H_k(S^1) \oplus H_k(S^2)\] for $k\ge 1$. By the universal coefficient theorem, since the homology is free abelian, the cohomology agrees with the homology. The cup product is trivial because the cohomology classes are supported on different wedge summands.
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    We write down the cohomology ring of the torus $T^2$ and $S^2 \vee S^1 \vee S^1$. Writing $T^2 = S^1 \times S^1$, the K\"unneth formula gives
\[H^*(T^2) \cong H^*(S^1) \otimes H^*(S^1)\]
Write $\alpha$ for the pullback of the generator of $H^1(S^1)$ along the first projection and $\beta$ for the pullback of the generator of $H^1(S^1)$ along the second projection. Then $\alpha^2=(\eta\otimes 1)^2=\eta^2\otimes 1=0$ and similarly $\beta^2=0$. However, $\alpha\beta = (\eta\otimes 1)(1\otimes \eta) = \eta\otimes \eta$, and $\beta\alpha = (1\otimes \eta)(\eta\otimes 1) = -\eta\otimes \eta$
\begin{align*}
    H^*(T^2) & \cong \Lambda_\Z(\alpha,\beta)
\end{align*} the exterior algebra on two generators in degree $1$. However, the cohomology ring of $S^2 \vee S^1 \vee S^1$ is isomorphic to $H^*(S^2) \oplus H^*(S^1) \oplus H^*(S^1)$ as a graded abelian group, and the cup product is trivial because the cohomology classes are supported on different wedge summands. 
\end{example}

\begin{example}
    We compute the homology and cohomology of $\SO(3)$.
    The double cover $\SU(2) \to \SO(3)$ induced by letting $\SU(2)$ act on the imaginary quaternions by conjugation is a universal cover and identifies $\SO(3)$ with $\R\P^3$. Therefore \begin{align*}
        H_k(\SO(3)) & \cong H_k(\R\P^3) \\
        & \cong
        \begin{cases}
        \Z, & k=0,\\
        \Z/2\Z, & k=1,\\
        0, & k=2,\\
        \Z, & k=3,\\
        0, & k>3.
        \end{cases}
    \end{align*}
    An application of the universal coefficient theorem gives
    \begin{align*}
        H^k(\SO(3)) & \cong H^k(\R\P^3) \\
        & \cong
        \begin{cases}
        \Z, & k=0,3,\\
        0, & k=1,\\
        \Z/2\Z, & k=2,\\
        0, & k>3.
        \end{cases}
    \end{align*}
\end{example}
